NIGERIA
NORTH EASTERN STATE
APPRAISAL OF GOMBE AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        The Federal Military Government of Nigeria (FMG) has requested Bank
assistance to finance an agricultural development project in the North Eastern
State of the Federation. The project was identified by a Bank mission in
November 1972, and was subsequently prepared by the Bank in collaboration with
the North Eastern State Authorities. This report is based on the findings of
an appraisal mission that visited Nigeria in September and October 1973.
ii.       Under the present economic circumstances and associated industrial
growth, industry and the urban population are becoming more dependent on the
rural population for assured food and industrial crop supplies, and at the
same time the gap in income earnings between urban and rural communities is
becoming ever wider. The northern States of Nigeria and their rural popula-
tions are the main producers of grain crops for human consumption and of
industrial crops for processing industries, and yet their per capita income
of about US$40 per annum is the lowest in the Federation.
iii.      Except for the extreme northern areas, mu..inly north of 12' latitude,
the soils and climate of the northern States are generally suitable for ex-
tensive annual crop production. Research workers and the more responsive
farmers using improved inputs have adequately shown the response of improved
seeds to economic applications of fertilizers, and the viability of insecti-
cides for pest control purposes. Due to the disruptions caused by the civil
war (1966-68) and its aftermath, the lack of planning resources, and until
recently considerable financial constraints, very little had been done to
improve the production of the smallfarmers. Even now experienced manpower
to plan and implement rational developments in agriculture is in extremely
short supply. The Federal and State Governments recognizing the need to
increase the production from the small farm sector, 70% of the population,
now plan to accelerate the development of agriculture throughout Nigeria.
This project refers to one of three projects in northern Nigeria that the
Federal Military Government of Nigeria (FMG) would develop with the Bank's
assistance.
iv.       The project area (5,LQ00 km2) is centered around Gombe in part of
Bauchi Province of the North Eastern State. The area supports a population
of about 640,000 people (6% of the State's population) including some 58,000
families farming on average 4 ha each. The net annual family income including
the value of produce consumed on the farm is currently about US$330. The
population is expanding, the soils are rapidly declining in fertility, and the
people, are under constant pressure to increase output. As such, conditions
are ripe for project action anc. farmer response is expected to be high.



v.        The project would provide an integrated investment to cover physical
infrastructure, support services and farm inputs; specifically it would:
construct 1,000 km of low cost agricuLtural roads; 85 small earth dams and
160 ponds to provide water supplies to humans and livestock; a project admin-
istrative center at Gombe, 5 development unit centers and 50 farm service
centers; it would develop and equip two seed multiplication farms to produce
improved high yielding seed; and it would expand and create new training
facilities for project staff and fanaers. The project would also provide
better support services in the fornm of a tenfold increase in extension staff,
introduce new credit and marketing services to the farmers; and the associated
staff houses, offices and transport facilities required to support these
services. The project would procure Larm inputs to sell to farmers for cash
or credit. An evaluation unit would be established to evaluate the operations
and benefits of the proposed inlvestment, and feasibility studies would be
undertaken for the preparation of similar projects to be located in other
states.
vi.       The project would be managed by a special Management Unit responsi-
ble to an Executive Committee, both to be established under a gazetted order
of the North East State Executive Council. The committee would be chaired
by the Permanent Secretary of the State's Ministry of Natural Resources. The
Management Unit would be suitably staffed through transfer of existing Govern-
ment staff or personnel presently under training. Deficiencies in specialized
technical skills and high level management staff, unavailable in Nigeria, would
be made up through international recruitment.
vii.      The proposed Bank Loan of US$21 million would be made to the
Federal Government of Nigeria for a trerm of 20 years, including a five-year
grace period.  The loan would be 50% of total project costs, estimated at
US$42.1 million and would cover all the foreign costs estimated at US$20.2
million and 4%; of local costs equivalent to US$0.8 million. Of the US$21
million Bank loan, US$20.6 million would be onlent to the North Eastern
State Government (NES), and the remaining US $0.4 million would be retained
by FMG to finance the preparation of agricultural feasibility studies. NES
would provide US$19.3 million or 46% of total project costs for project
investment and operating costs. The remaining project costs estimated at
US$1.8 million, or 4%, would be contributed by participating farmers in the
form of cash payments for seasonal inputs and down payments on farm equipment.
viii.     Procurement of ve.licies, plaat equipment, fertilizers, insecticides,
farm tractors and implements, having a value of more than US$25,000 would be
through international competic.ve btdd'ing (ICB), and would have an estimated
value of US$13.2 million.  Contracts for civil works such as houses, offices
and warehouses valued at USS6.1 million would not be suitable for ICB, due
to the dispersed locations and slai.l size of indIvidual contracts, and would
be awarded under locally advertised comipetitive bidding. Foreign firms and
suppliers who are well represen,ed in Nigeria would not be precluded, but
bonafide domestic contractors would receive a 7-1/2% preference in bid evalua-
tion.  Construction of roads, water supplies and soil conservation works
estimated at US$6.7 millior -,2ruld` be carried out on force account due to the
shortage of suitable medi-um .ca e contractors who normally would undertake
this work, however, petty conc'ractors would be employed to the maximum extent



- iii -
feasible. A large part of project costs, an estimated US$12.3 million, would
be for labor, staff salaries and allowances, vehicle maintenance and operation,
general services, project evaluation, and preparation of feasibility studies
and as such would be unsuitable for competitive bidding. Internationally re-
cruited staff would be employed under terms and conditions acceptable to the
Bank.
ix.       Proceeds of the Bank loan would be disbursed to cover:  (a) 100% of
the CIF costs of directly imported vehicles, tractors, heavy plant and
equipment, or 75% of the cost of these items if locally procured - US$2.1
million; (b) 100% of the CIF costs of directly imported farm tractors and
equipment, ox drawn equipment, spraying machines, fertilizers, insecticides,
battery sets and seeds, or 71% of these items if procured locally - US$4.1
million; (c) 100% of the cost of expatriate staff - US$1.5 million; (d) 60%
of the cost of buildings, houses, furnishings, and construction materials
and labor for roads, dams and soil conservation works - US$3.7 million; (e)
60% of local staff costs - US$3.2 million; (f) 60% of the cost of the field
evaluation unit - US$0.5 million; (g) 100% of cost of consultant services
to prepare feasibility studies - US$0.3 million; and (h) US$5.6 million to
cover unallocated expenses. Disbursements of the Bank loan would be against
import documentation, contracts and certified records of expenditure, the
latter would be scrutinized from time to time by the Bank during supervision
missions.
x.        The principal direct benefits would, through the output of 32,000
project farmers, be from project year 5 the annual incremental production
of 21,000 tons of seed cotton (97,000 bales of lint), 6,000 tons of groundnuts,
8,000 tons of sorghum, 35,000 tons of maize, and 1,000 tons of cowpeas;
the project would indirectly benefit a further 26,000 farm families through
provision of better communications, water supplies and markets. Including
the non-farming population, some 640,000 people would one way or other receive
some benefit from the project, not least the project would provide additional
employment - equivalent to 12,000 manyears per annum. Intangible benefits
would include the provision of a basic infrastructure on which health and
other social services could be developed, expansion of local trade to the
non-farming population, and the strengthening of institutions including the
Local Authorities, and the State Ministry of Natural Resources. The project
established evaluation unit would provide considerable information to support
the future planning of Federal and State agricultural policies. The project
would train Nigerian staff in management and technical aspects applicable to
rural development. The economic rate of return is 17%, a 10% reduction in
commodity prices would reduce the rate of return to 9% and the project would
still be viable (10% rate of return) if production targets were reduced to
70% of those projected. The net farm income for farmers directly affected by
the project would increase frorz US$330 to US$480 per annum at full development,
and the net per capita income of the total farming population would rise from
US$41 to US$60 inferring an annual income growth rate of 8% per annum, corpat&Ct
to the present no growth situation, and is achievable provided that the physical
inputs and management needs are provided.
xi.       On the basis of the assurances that were obtained during negotiaLions,
and set out in Chapter IX, the project is suitable for a Bank loan of US$21
million.






I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      This report appraises a loan application made to the Bank by the
Federal Military Government of Nigeria (FMG). The purpose of the loan would
be to assist Nigeria's North Eastern State Government (NES) to finance an agri-
cultural development program involving 58,000 small farmers in the Gombe
Division of Bauchi Province. The project would involve the construction of
vital crop extraction roads and water supplies, and would provide farmers
with the technical, marketing, and credit services, and inputs needed to
insure significant increases in production and farmer incomes. Currently,
the latter are low, about US$330 per family, or US$41 per capita.
1.02      The project is deliberately large; this is essential in order to
make a meaningful impact in such a large and populous country. The risks
that do exist are minimized provided competent management is available,
because the technical innovations proposed have been proven, the farmers
are hardworking, and the production induced by the project is in high demand
on domestic and export markets. The project concept could be applied through-
out most of northern Nigeria. One important role of the project would provide
a practical training ground for Nigerian staff in the technical and admin-
istrative management of projects of this type.
1.03      The project is within the frame of policies outlined in Nigeria's
"Perspective Plan for Agricultural Development to 1985." In particular,
it follows FMG policies to increase production of both food and industrial
crops to supply Nigeria's rapidly increasing urban populations and industries
including intensive livestock production enterprises, particularly in the
southern States. The project would assist to develop sound agricultural
credit systems tailored to provide credit to large numbers of small farmers.
It would provide also a much needed base for the expansion and improvement
of health and other social services currently planned by the State Government;
the project road development program would be a vital first phase to facilitate
the improvement of these social services.
1.04      This report refers to similar projects at Gusau (NW State) and
Funtua (NC State). These projects were appraised concurrently and are similar
in nature; while administratively totally separate, they have common production
policies, techniques and services. The three projects represent a first, but
very substantial, step by the Federal and State Governments to increase the
production and raise the living standards of large numbers of peoples in the
relatively less developed and environmentally harsh northern States.
II. BACKGROUND
A. General
2.01      The 12-State Federal Republic of Nigeria has a total area of about
914,000 sq km, and in 1971, a population estimated at 68 million. About 35%
of GDP estimated at US$9.1 billion in 1972/3, is generated in the agricul-
ture sector, which employs 70% of the labor force. In 1972/3, total domestic



-2-
exports amounted to US$2.1 billion, of which petroleum represented about
82% (US$1.8 billion). The main agricultural exports are cocoa, groundnuts,
and groundnut oil and cake. Oil palm products used to be exported, but with
increasing domestic consumption and a general decline in the industry, palm
oil is no longer exported, and palm kernel exports are dwindling. Cotton,
once a major export, is now fully utilized by the local milling industry and
local supply cannot meet demand. By and large, Nigeria is self-sufficient
in food; food imports are estimated at US$2.6 per capita.
2.02      Nigeria can be divided into three major agricu:Ltural and ecological
zones (see map): a southern wet forest zone, south of the confluence of
the Benue and Niger Rivers, mainly growing perennial tree crops, including
cocoa, oil palm and rubber; a central dry forest zone known as the middle
belt which is underpopulated and tsetse infested, but has a high potential
for annual food crops and livestock; and a northern zone, covering about 50%
of the country, with a tree savannah ecology, and which supports Nigeria's
cattle population, and produces its main cereal crops, and groundnut:s and
cotton.
B. Agriculture in Northern Nigeria
2.03      North Eastern State is one of four States in northern Nigeria
which have very similar agricultural and ecological systems. The four have
also a closely associated history of institutional development and agricul-
tural policies, as prior to '968, the whole region, including two other
States, was administered as one from a regional capital at Kaduna.  Thus,
a description of the North Eastern State, its policies and problems, would
apply closely to the situation in North Western, North Central and Kano
States.
2.04      North Eastern State, with a population of about 10 million, liIs
between latitudes 7-13' north and 9-14° east; comprises about 270,000 km
and is the largest state in the Federation. Within the State there is
considerable climatic variation, and rainfall varies from 1,500 mm in its
south to less than 750 mm on the border with Niger. Much of the State has
an assured monomodal rainfall (4-5 months) of at least 750 mm per annum;
although the distribution within the total is sometimes erratic, leading to
"drought" years such as occurre-d in 1973.  Most of the State has a tree
savannah ecology, and in those areas of higher tree densities, the incidence
of tsetse is high and trypanosona&-asil is a constraint to livestock production,
as well as being a human health -probiem.  The proposed project which would be
centered on Gombe covers about 2% of the State and includes 6% of the population.
2.05      The major crops are ainuals such as millet and sorghum, the princi-
pal cereals, and groundnuts, co:ton, and cowpeas. Minor crops include maize,
tomatoes, chilies, cassava a:n. yams. Livestock plays an important role in
the economy and include cattle, sheep, goats and poultry.  The Fulani people,
who are nomadically inclined, oxwn most of the livestock, and have a consider-
able impact on the arable farmer. Traditionally, cotton and groundnuts have
been the north's main cash crops and are likely to remain so; but as the



- 3 -
savannah areas of Nigeria are generally well suited for cereal production,
they could play an important role in the future in producing cereals for the
expanding market created by intensive livestock industries and urban popul-
ations of the south.
C. Agricultural Policy
2.06      Agricultural production in the State is virtually stagnant, and
any increase that is occurring is due to population growth. Although signi-
ficant improvements in crop yields have been demonstrated to be technically
and economically feasible by the research institutions, very little has been
done to help the farmer to obtain and use the innovations necessary to improve
his production. Until very recently, NES had no clear-cut policy for in-
creasing production, and, in the absence of specific plans to implement the
policy, the Ministry of Natural Resources (MNR) still tends to function on a
basis of maintaining the status quo. The extension service is weak and poorly
staffed, credit and marketing institutions barely exist, planning is poor, and
the expertise needed to implement successfully major agricultural and rural
development projects is in very short supply.
2.07      Nigeria's manufacturing industries that are based on farm products
such as cotton and groundnuts, and the rapidly expanding urban population
(estimated to be increasing at 5% annually) are totally dependent on the
small traditional farmer for their raw materials and food supplies. These
farmers, as throughout Nigeria in general, are very poorly serviced, do not
use improved and drought resistant varieties and have very limited access
to fertilizers and agro-chemicals. Production is, therefore, irregular and
is governed by the vagaries of climate and other influences, such as pest
attack, that remain beyond the farmer's control. Given the foregoing, the
small farmer is unable to bank on producing an assured surplus.
2.08      FMG is becoming increasingly aware of the need to rectify the
current situation, and in its discussions with the Bank and NES has agreed
that, in the short term, at least, the quickest way of improving farm pro-
duction and incomes would be to provide a concentration of support services
to areas of reasonable potential and dense farm population. In such areas,
farm sizes cannot increase and farmers, with declining land fertility, are
facing the problem of maintaining, let alone increasing, production. Such
farmers are likely to respond well to any improvements that may be offered to
them. For the longer term, it will be important to develop programs to open
up areas of unused land, particularly in the now tsetse-infested middle belt,
for settlement by farmers and graziers.
2.09      The project appraised in tais report would provide a package of
services to existing farming communities with the objective of increasing
production of cash and food crops; it would train agricultural, credit,
marketing, administrative and engineering staff, and would develop NES
agricultural planning capacity. The increase in farm incomes, generated
by project actions, would be significant. Further background information



- 4 -
is available in Report 416a UNI titled "Current Economic Position and Prospects
of Nigeria" dated August 5, 1974 with special reference to Vol III on "Problems
and Prospects of Food Production."
III. PROJECT AREA
A. General
3.01      The project area covers 5,300 km2 and comprises three districts in
Gombe emirate, and part of West Tangale, Waja and Kaltungo Districts, all
within Gombe Administrative Division of Bauchi Province. Map shows area
location within Nigeria.
3.02      The annual rainfall is about 900 mm and is confined to a 7-month
period from April through October. Under reasonable management and utilization
of improved seeds and fertilizers, high yields can be achieved under the
prevailing climatic regime. Due to the seasonality of rainfall and the im-
pervious underlying basement complex, groundwater is severely restricted, and
with increasing land pressures stream flow has markedly diminished and water
supplies for human and livestock resources are a limiting factor to develop-
ment.
3.03      The project area is occupied by the people of the Hausa tribe,
many of whom have emigrated from the Sokoto area of the North Western State,
and by mid-1974 is estimated to have some 58,000 farm families that support a
total project area population of 640,000. The social structure is significant
because of the relative strength and influence of the hierarchical structure
of the Local Authority. The part of project area comes within the jurisdic-
tion of the Emir of Gombe whose hereditary authority is delegated through
salaried district and village heads. The latter are responsible for village
level administration of land tenure arrangements based on Islamic law and
on customary usufructory rights.
3.04      Information on farm size is scant but it is estimated that the
average size farm holding is 4.0 ha. There are some large farms (over 50
ha) within the project area, but these are relatively few. Cropping patterns
(see Annex 1) are dominated by sorghum and millet as food grains, and cotton
and groundnuts as the major cash crops. Numerous minor crops are grown.
3.05      The project area is well served by main roads, and has a railhead
at Gombe. However, crop extraction and minor roads are virtually non-
existent, and communities are seasonally cut off from each other and the
outside world. Transportation of farmer produce and inputs is therefore
difficult and expensive. Further project area data is at Annex 2.



- 5 -
B. Institutions
3.06      State institutions that would be concerned with the project and
their relevant responsibilities are described in Annex 2. They include: the
Ministry of Natural Resources (MNR) that would have the responsibility of
carrying out the project; the Ministry of Works (MOW), responsible for State-
financed public works programs; the Ministry of Health (MOH), responsible for
all aspects of human health in the State; the Ministry of Industry, Trade, and
Cooperatives (MITC); and the Gombe (GLA) and Tangale Waja (TWLA) Local Authori-
ties. The project area comes under the control of these LA, whose functions
include the collection of tax in respect of people and cattle (haraji and
jangali, respectively); the administration of land allocation; and the pro-
vision of services such as feeder road construction and maintenance, dispen-
saries, and primary education as a complement to State Government activities
in these fields.
3.07      Other institutions that would be concerned with or affected by the
project and which are described in Annex 2 would be the Gombe Cooperative
Union; and the Institute of Agricultural Research (IAR) which was created in
the early 1960's from the Research Division of the then Northern Nigeria
Ministry of Agriculture, and which now is a semi-autonomous body linked with
Ahmadu Bello University (ABU).
3.08      Institutions concerned with crop marketing and processing are the
Northern States Marketing Board (NSMB) which as purchasing agent of the Nigerian
Produce Marketing Company (NPMC) is responsible for organizing the domestic
purchase, through its Licensed Buying Agents (LBA), of controlled crops such
as cotton and groundnuts at prices predetermined by FMG, and the Cotton and
Agricultural Processors Ltd. (CAP) which was formerly the British Cotton
Growers' Association (Nigeria) Ltd. and which has had a monopoly for ginning
Nigerian cotton since exports began over half a century ago. CAP has ginneries
throughout the cotton-growing zones of the northern states. The present gin-
ing agreement between CAP and NSMB expires in 1976 and for this reason assur-
ances were obtained from NES during negotiations that any future ginning agree-
ment would not adversely affect the production, marketing or ginning of cotton
within the project. The Produce Inspection Division (PID) of MNR has important
responsibilities for grading controlled produce. Crop processing, marketing
and grading arrangements are described in Annex 2.
IV. THE PROJECT
A. Description
4.01      The project would be carried out over a five-year investment period
1975/76-1979/80, and would involve:
-    constructing about 1,000 km of low cost agricultural roads,
85 earth dams, 160 tapkis (ponds), and associated soil
conservation structures;



-6-
constructing an administrative center in Gombe, 5 development
unit centers, and 50 farm service centers;
developing and equipp:ing seed multiplication farms at Kumo
and Tumu;
expanding training facilities at Tumu and constructing
a new project training center at Kumo;
provision of adequate farmer extension, credit and marketing
services, including staff, vehicles and equipment;
provision of seasonal and medium-term loans to farmers for
the purchase of crop inputs and farm equipment;
establishing a project evaluation unit;
provision of consultant services for the preparation of
feasibility studies; and
-    preparation of plans for the continued provision of project
services after the investment phase of the project is
completed.
4.02      The project would be managed by the Gombe Project Management Unit
(GOPMU) which would carry out the day-to-day management operations. GOPMU
would be responsible to the Gombe Project Executive Committee (GOPEC) for
budgetary and policy control. A committee would be set up to coordinate
project activities at Federal level. Details at Chapter VI.
B. Detailed Features
4.03      A summary showing the expected scheduling of project activities
is at Annex 2, Table 1; it includes the construction program, the rate of
village involvement, and the number of farmers affected by the project
annually.
4.04      Agricultural Road Development.  The project would provide a network
of 1,000 km of agricultural roads, of a design adequate to allow light traffic
during most of the wet season, and heavy trucks during the dry season. The
roads would permit the efficient evacuation of farm produce, the timely
supply of inputs, the effective movement of project service staff, and much
improved general access to farms and hamlets. This basic infrastructure would
allow other Government programs including health and education to expand into
areas wihich currently are inaccessible. Annex 4 gives details of construction
methods, specifications, and plant and equipment requirements. In view of an
acute shortage of local contracting capacity, and the probable lack of interest
of foreign contractors in work of this sort, construction would be undertaken
by force account through a project operated and managed field construction
unit which would make some use of petty contractors and would thereby assist



- 7 -
in their development. Maintenance of roads and dams would be the responsi-
bility of the Gombe and Tangale Waja Local Authorities. In order to build up
GLA and TWLA operational effectiveness, their respective road maintenance staff
would be seconded to the project for training and operational control. The
project would provide, during the investment period, funds for the purchase
and operation of the necessary maintenance equipment.
4.05      Water Development.  The project would set up and operate a unit
to construct some 85 Small and nedium size earth dams, each of a minimum
capacity of 100,000 m , equipped with simple systems to reticulate the water
away from the reservoir. The reservoirs would supply water for human and
livestock needs and would be located in strategic areas. They would also be
used to supply water for compaction purposes during road construction. In
addition, some 160 tapkis (ponds) would be developed to provide a better
distribution of water for livestock for all but the last 3 months of the dry
season. In a very few instances, the dams and tapkis, where land and water
availability allows, would be used for the irrigation of village vegetable
production. Abundant dam sites are available. Annex 5 provides detail on
design and specifications.
4.06      Soil Conservation.  In association with the water development
program, soil conservation me sures would be undertaken within the catchment
areas of the dams (about 5 km each). These would include the construction
of cutoff ditches, artificial waterways and contour ridging and be aimed to
reduce siltation of the dams and control erosion. Further soil conservation
measures would be taken in providing the farmers with marker ridges to assist
in contour reglignment of crop ridges. During the project investment period,
some 2,700 km or 51% of the project area would be so protected; these
programs should be continued beyond the investment period. Annex 5 provides
the details of this project component.
4.07      Building Development.  The project would include construction of
houses, offices and stores needed for project services. It would include
the construction of 260 houses of various sizes, a project office, 5 devel-
opment center offices, a railhead store and other facilities. In addition,
50 farm service centers, each including a 500-ton capacity warehouse
would be constructed, and improvements made to three market depots associated
with existing and future cotton ginneries. Those houses and buildings not
required by MNR after the project investment period would be taken over by
other Government departments operating within the area.
4.08      Seed Multiplication Farms.  The project would develop and equip
new seed multiplication farms at Kumo and Tumu. Investments would include farm
buildings, water supplies, farm machinery and equipment. These seed farms
would, in association with larger farmers, provide project and other State
farmers with improved seeds, act as demonstration and research centers, and
provide practical training in farm management. Details are at Annex 3,
Schedule 1. During loan negotiations, assurances were obtained from NES
that the forest reserve at Kumo and suitable land in the vicinity of Tumu
would be made available to GOPMU for development as seed multiplication
farms.



- 8 -
4.09      Training Facilities.  The project would expand boarding accommoda-
tion at the Tumu training center from 20 to 40 trainees and construct a new
center at Kumo, also catering for 40 residential trainees. Initially, the
centers would be used for project staff training, but progressively emphasis
would be on farmer training.
4.10      Farmer Support Services  The project would expand and intensify
support services for farmers. It would increase the farmer extension person-
nel ratio from the current 1:2,150 to 1:210. This would involve an increase
in extension agents from 28 to 280. The extension personnel would include,
besides basic extension agents, personnel specialized in livestock husbandry,
seed multiplication and farm management, farmer training, and farm equipment.
The tractor hire service now operated by MNR at a loss would be phased out,
and gradually replaced by project sponsored and assisted private operators.
A credit service would be established to operate a project controlled credit
revolving fund and the contingent services to farmers (para. 6.17). A market-
ing service would also be established to improve market liaison, quality con-
trol, price information, produce weighing and to control traders. The credit
and marketing services would employ some 180 staff of various grades; of these
only about 15, mainly produce examiners are now employed by MNR. Consequently,
the majority of personnel employed in these services would receive in-service
training from the project (details are at Annex 6).
4.11      Other MNR services such as veterinary, fisheries and forestry
would continue to work within the project area, and would coordinate their
activities with GOPMU.
4.12      Farm Inputs.  Under the project, seasonal inputs would be made
available to farmers for cash and credit. Such inputs and equipment are
estimated to amount, during the project investment period, to 32,000 tons of
fertilizer, 2,400 tons improved seeds, 900 tons insecticides, 33,000 ultra
low volume insecticide sprayers, 5,000 ox carts, 5,000 ox plows and 50 tractors
and associated implements. Inputs and equipment would be sold at the farm
service centers. Details are at Annexes 7 and 8.
4.13      Project Evaluation.  A field evaluation unit comprising of two
enumeration units would be established to collect relevant data necessary
for the evaluation of project actions and resultant social and economic
benefits. Much of the data would be processed by the evaluation section of
the Project Monitoring and Evaluation Unit (PMEU) to be established by the
Federal Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources (FMANR) and financed
under the loan for the Funtua Agricultural Development Project. The field
evaluation unit would receive technical direction from PMEU, but would be
administered by the Project (Annex 9 for details).
4.14      Provision would be made for the necessary support items such as
motor vehicles, equipment, etc. to ensure that the objectives of the project
are fulfilled. Details at Annex 10.



- 9 -
4.15      Feasibility Studies.  Provision is made under the project for the
FMANR to employ consultants (60 man months) to assist Federal and State
Government agricultural planning teams to prepare feasibility studies for
similar projects. In most instances consultants would be individually
employed.  Support services for the consultants would be provided from normal
Government budget allocations. During negotiations assurances were obtained
that the terms of reference, qualifications and conditions of employment of
the consultants would be mutually satisfactory to the Bank and FMG.
4.16      Post Project Development.  During the project investment period,
MNR and GOPMU would draw up detailed plans and arrange for the allocation
of staff to allow project services to continue undisrupted to farmers within
the project area after the end of the investment period. Presupposing no
radical changes in NES organizatiorn extension and development services would
be handled by MNR's regular provincial organization suitably strengthened;
the field evaluation units would be absorbed by the MNR Planning Unit; NSMB
would take over market control and liaison; and the Gombe and Tangale Waja
Local Authorities or any other appropriate agency would maintain roads and
water supplies provided under the project. Assurances that roads and dams
would be maintained were obtained during negotiations.
4.17      During the project the evaluation section of PMEU would consider
various alternatives for the future use and operations of the revolving
credit and the supply of farm inputs. It is important that by 1980 credit
and farm supplies should be commercially orientated, and that the institutions
involved should be relatively independent of Government with control in the
hands of participating farmers. Prior to negotiations the intention was to
set up a Farmer Finance Company to control and operate the revolving credit
fund. During negotiations FMG indicated that it would prefer to postpone
the formation of such a company to a time when (a) further evaluations of
farmer credit institutions and requirements had been made by Pl'EU during
the first three years of the project; and (b) evidence, if any, of improve-
ments in the performance of the existing cooperative system under new Federal
policy and guidance. The exclusion of the proposed Farmer Finance Company
should have no adverse effect on the project during the development period,
provided that firm plans are made for the proper transfer of the fund to the
appropriate institution prior to the project's completion. Thus, PMEU would
play an important role in evaluating the problems and requirements for future
commercial services to project farmers.
V. COST ESTIMATES AND FINANCIAL ARRANGEMENTS
A. Project Costs
5.01      Project costs are estimated to total N 27.7 million (US$42.1 million)
of which the foreign exchange component would be N 13.3 million (US$20.2
million) or 48% of total costs.

expatriate staff are based on current international salaries, and include
standard benefits and allowances. In the case of farm inputs, only incremental
requirements are costed. Participating farmers would, as now, receive a 50%
subsidy on the cost of phosphatic and nitrogenous fertilizers and the costs
of this subsidy is included in project costs. Project costs include import
duties and taxes (internationally recruited staff salaries and the cost of
specialized consultant services are estimated net of tax). Total taxes amount
to N 0.9 million (US$1.4 million). Analysis of the costs, Annex 10, Table 1,
shows that 7% (N 2.0 million) is for project administration; 25% (N 7.0 million)
is for extension, credit and market services; 16% (N 4.4 million) is for road,
soil and water conservation; 25% (N 6.9 million) for incremental farm inputs
and fertilizer subsidies; 2% (N 0.5 million) for project evaluation; 1% (N 0.2
million) for feasibility studies; and 24% (N 6.7 million) for contingencies.
Internationally recruited staff costs are estimated at 4% of total costs,
about half of which would be for specialized technicians currently unavailable
or in critically short supply in Nigeria.
B. Proposed Financing
5.04      It is proposed that IBRD make a loan of US$21 million to FMG,
repayable at 8% over 20 years including a five-year grace period for principal
during which interest and commitment charges on the loan would be paid. Such
a loan would be equivalent to 50% of total project costs and would cover all
foreign costs, estimated at US$20.2 million (N 13.3 million), and 4% of local
costs amounting to US$0.8 million (N 0.5 million). Of the US$21 million,
N13.6 million (US$20.6 million) would be onlent to the NES, and N 0.2 million
(US$0.4 million) would be retained by FMG to finance the preparation of
feasibility studies. During negotiations it was agreed that the subsidiary
loan agreement between FMG and NES would have been approved by the Bank.
5.05      NES would provide N 12.7 million (US$19.3 million) or 46% of
total project cost as its contribution to investment and operating costs.
The remainder of project costs estimated at N 1.2 million (US$1.8 million)
or 4% would be contributed by participating farmers in the form of cash
purchases of seasonal inputs and down payments on farm equipment. Details
of the project financing plan are shown at Annex 11, Table 1, and are summa-
rized in the following table:

5.06      It was agreed that as from the date of loan negotiations retro-
active financing of up to US$150,000 would be made available for the employ-
ment of key personnel (para 6.09).
C. Procurement
5.07      Procurement of vehicles, plant, equipment, fertilizers, insecticides,
farm tractors and implements, where orders or contracts had a value of more
than USS25,000, would be through international competitive bidding (ICB) in
accordance with IBRD guidelines. Such procurement is estimated to have a
value of about N 8.8 million (US$13.4 million). Domestically manufactured
goods would be allowed a 15% preference when comparing domestic bids with
those of foreign manufacturers. Contracts for the construction of buildings,



- 13 -
houses, and the purchase of construction materials and furnishings valued at
N 4.0 million (USS6.1 million) would not be attractive to foreign contractors,
due to the disbursed location of the work and the small size of individual
contracts and orders, and would be awarded under locally advertised competi-
tive bidding. In the latter case, foreign firms and suppliers would not be
precluded from bidding as such work would be advertized in at least one local
newspaper, but bonafide domestic contractors would receive a 7-1/2% preference
in bid evaluation. Construction of roads, water supplies and soil conserva-
tion works estimated at N 4.4 million (US$6.7 million) would be carried out on
force account due to the shortage of suitable medium scale contractors who
normally would undertake this work, however, petty contractors would be
employed to the maximum extent feasible. A large part of project costs, an
estimated N 8.1 million (US$12.3 million), would be for labor, staff salaries
and allowances, vehicle maintenance and operation, general services, project
evaluation, and feasibility study preparation, and as such generally be
unsuitable for competitive bidding. Those items not included specifically
under procedures for ICB or local competitive bidding would be purchased
through local commercial channels. The remaining N 6.7 million (US$10.2
million) of project costs would be contingencies. Nigeria has no preferential
import tariff arrangements and competition between foreign manufacturers on
the domestic market is keen.
D. Disbursement
5.08      The proceeds of the Bank loan (US$21 million) would be disbursed
over five years (1975/76-1979/80) to cover 50% of total project costs and
would be expended against the following categories:
100% of the cif cost of directly imported vehicles,
heavy plant, and equipment (Annex 10, Table 2) or 75% of the
cost of these items if locally procured totalling US$2.1
million (N1.4 million);
100% of the cif cost of directly imported farm tractors
and equipment, ox drawn equipment, spraying machines,
fertilizers, insecticides, battery sets and seeds
(Annex 7, Tables 2-5) or 71% of the cost of these
items if procured locally totalling US$4.1 million
(N 2.7 million);
100% of the cost of internationally recruited staff (Annex 10,
Table 4) totalling US$1.5 million (Ni.0 million);
60% of the cost of build-ngs, houses, furnishings,
and construction material and labor for roads, dams
and soil conservation works (Annex 10, Table 3) totalline
US$3.7 million (N2.4 million);
60% of local staff costs (Annex 10, Table 4) totalling
US$3.2 million (N 2.1 million);



- 14 -
60% of the cost of the field evaluation unit US$0.5
million (N 0.3 million) (Annex 9, Table 1);
100% of the cost of consultant services to prepare
feasibility studies US$0.3 million (N 0.2 million); and
an unallocated amount totalling US$5.6 million (N 3.7
million) to cover physical and price contingencies.
5.09      Disbursements of the Bank loan would be against import documentation,
contracts and certified records of expenditures. For disbursements made
against certified records of expenditures, documentation would not be sub-
mitted for review as a matter of course, but would be retained by the project
management unit for scrutiny by Bank supervision missions. Any surplus loan
funds after the completion of the project would be used to further develop
the project area providing expanded services to farmers.
E. Budgetary Control Funding Procedures Accounts and Audit
5.10      Budgets.  Annual budgets would be prepared by GOPMU management and
approved by GOPEC for inclusion in the MNR development budget.  The initial
budget would use as its base the cost estimates set out in this report, but
would be amended where necessary to tie in with changes in costs and project
policies and development schedules. Major changes in policies and procedures
would have to be approved by NES and the Bank. GOPMU management would, there-
after, amend its monthly cash flow statement indicating costs, revenues and
working capital requirements. These monthly statements would be submitted
to GOPEC for monitoring but GOPMU would be authorized to operate on such
monthly statements unless officially informed to the contrary by the Chairman
of GOPMU. Assurances that the above arrangements would be employed were
obtained from NES during negotiations.
5.11      Funding Procedures.  On the basis of the annual budget approved by
GOPEC, NES would make the necessary allocations in its annual development
budget to cover its contribution to project costs as well as about 25% of the
remaining annual costs of the project to meet working capital requirements,
the latter amounting to about N 0.7 million. In order to insure an adequate
flow of funds to the project, assurances were obtained during negotiations,
that when necessary adequate guaranteed draw down procedures would be made
by NES with a bank of its choice to allow GOPMU full access to funds within
the limitation of the approved annual budget. As a condition of loan effec-
tiveness, NES would furnish the Bank with satisfactory evidence that a project
bank account with adequate funds had been established.
5.12      Accounts and Audit.  Accounts for GOPMU would be kept under accept-
able commercial accounting procedures. However, such accounts would be set
up to facilitate the retrieval of cost accounting and project evaluation data,
as well as for audit control. Accounts would be audited annually by independent
auditors, mutually acceptable to NES, FMG and the Bank. Audited annual accounte



- 15 -
balance sheets and operating statements for GOPMU would be submitted simul-
taneously to NES, FMG and the Bank within four months of the closing of the
financial year. During negotiations, assurances to these effects were obtained.
VI. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. Organization
6.01      The project being the first of its kind in the State would have to
be carefully controlled. Because of its size and responsibilities for various
development aspects not normally associated with any one Government Ministry,
it would need a degree of autonomy not normally given to Ministerial Depart-
ments. Thus, the project would be implemented by the Gombe Project Management
Unit (GOPMU), headed by a Project Manager who would be responsible to the
Gombe Project Executive Committee (GOPEC); the chairman of which would be the
Permanent Secretary of the Ministry of Natural Resources of NES. GOPMU would
have its headquarters at Gombe, the Divisional town and center of the project
area. GOPMU would coordinate its activities with other similar projects at
a Federal level through a Federal Coordinating Committee. As a condition of
loan effectiveness, the North East State Executive Council would gazette an order,
mutually acceptable to FMG and the Bank, establishing GOPMU, setting out its
terms of reference and responsibilities. Assurances to this effect were
obtained during negotiations (a draft proposal for the gazetted order is at
Annex 6, Schedule 2).
6.02      Gombe Project Executive Committee would, inter alia, determine
policy, coordinate project activities with those of associated State Ministries
and Departments, exercise budgetary and audit control, approve the appointment
of professional grade staff, and review and set salary scales. GOPEC would
meet at least once a quarter and would comprise: the Permanent Secretary
MNR as Chairman, and representatives of the Ministries of Finance, Local
Government Works, Economic Planning, and NES Water Board, a representative
of the Federal Department of Agriculture, Chief Agricultural Officer for the
North East State, and two members residtng in the project area to be appointed
by the Military Governor. The Project Marager would be secretary of GOPEC.
6.03      A Federal Coordinating Committee would be established to review the
progress of the Gombe and similar projects in the northern states. It would
provide coordination between other Bank-financed agricultural projects of
similar nature in northern Nigeria, and would ensure a full exchange of ideas
and results. In its review of project progress, it would be aided by the
evaluation section of the Proiect Monitoring and Evaluation Unit (PMEU) set
up under the Bank loan for the Funtua Agricultural Development Project. The
committee would meet at least twice a year and would comprise: The Director
of the Federal Department of Agric.ilture (FDA) as Chairman; Chief Agricultural
Planning Officer (FDA) as Secretary; a representative from the respective
project executive committees; Project Managers of Gombe, Funtua and Gusau;



- 16 -
Director of the Institute of Agricultural Research, the Read of PMEU evaluation
section; representatives of NAB and NPMC; and representatives of the Federal
Ministry of Finance. During negotiations assurances were obtained that a
Federal Coordinatina Committee would be established, with terms of reference
and composition satisfactory to the Bank, within three months of loan effec-
tiveness.
6.04      Project Administration would fall under the direct responsibility
of the Project Manager for all operational procedures and actions. He would
be responsible to GOPEC. GOPEC, in consultation with the Project Manager,
would appoint one of the senior project staff as Deputy Project Manager.
The Project Manager would be assisted by: Chief Administrative Officer,
Chief Accountant, Chief Technical Officer, Chief Engineer, Chief Marketing
Officer, and Credit Manager (Annex 6, Schedule 3 provides details of the
tasks of key personnel). A summary showing the functions of the project's
six operational sections follows:
(a) Administration Section would be responsible for all project
personnel matters, staff transportation, registry, security
and public relations.
(b)  Finance Section would be responsible for project accounts,
procurement, stores, internal audit, budget control and would
set up systems to provide accurate cost accounts for evaluation
and management purposes.
(c) Technical Services Section would be responsible for farm
extension advice, staff and farmer training, seed multipli-
cation, farm management advice to larger farmers, and tractor
hire services. The main development effort would be through
the extensiorn service and structurally it would consist of
five development unit centers based on the towns of Gombe,
Kumo, Tumu, Wagari and Telassie, each headed by a development
officer, 1/ who would supervise agricultural assistants (AA)
posted to 50 farm service centers of the project area. Each
AA would supervise an average of four agricultural instructors
(Al) who would live in the hamlets associated with the service
center and who would work w-cth the hamlet farmers and their
committees. In -his way, tne extension system would be
tailored to the traditional village structure.
(d) Credit Services Section. Credit services would work parallel
with the extension services. Credit Officers would be posted
to Development Unit Centers, and two Credit Assistants to each
Farm service center.
1/   Normally, the minimum grace or the officer filling this post would be
that of agricultural officer. However, in the early stages of the
project, it is likely that most of the posts would be filled by
agricultural superintendents.



- 17 -
(e) Market Services Section would monitor the existing marketing
system, liaise and provide market information for buyers and
farmers, control the activities of Licensed Buying Agents (LBA),
and supervise the work of produce examiners (PE). A Marketing
Supervisor would be assisted by two Marketing Assistants posted
to each Development Unit Center and a Produce Examiner would be
posted to each market. Market Services would be responsible
for the logistics involved in procurement of farm inputs and
for their distribution and handling. As such, the produce
examiner would carry out the functions of store keeper when
the markets are closed.
(f) Engineering Services Section would be a specialized service
responsible for land use planning, road construction, water
development, soil conservation, building supervision and
workshop management. It would prepare tender documentation
for procurement of engineering equipment and supplies,
supervise building contractors, and implement all construc-
tion aspects of the project not undertaken by contractors.
It would maintain project vehicles and equipment.
6.05      Project field evaluation would be directed by the evaluation section
of PMIEU. The field enumeration staff would be administratively responsible
to the Project Manager. Details of the field unit are at Annex 9.
B. Staffing
6.06      Both Federal and State Governments recognize the need for qualified
and experienced staff to administer the project and assure high technical
standards in its field implementation. The northern States are generally
short of qualified personnel to fill key project management and senior techni-
cal posts, and the demand for personnel with such expertise is high. NES
is fully aware of the problem of staffing the project adequately and is
prepared to recruit staff internationally where qualified Nigerian personnel
are not available. Medium level technical and administrative staff needed
for the project are generally available in Nigeria, though not necessarily
in the North Eastern State. Most lower level staff needs would be met from
within the State: existing civil servants; or the output of existing training
schemes; or staff recruited and trained directly by the project.
6.07      Management Staff.  International recruitment is expected to be
necessary for the Project Manager, Chief Accountant, Credit Manager, and Chief
Engineer. The Chief Administrative Officer, Chief Marketing Office and Chief
Technical Officer would be Nigerian appointments. During negotiations assur-
ances were obtained that the qualifications, experience and terms and conditions
of employment of appointees to the above posts would be mutually acceptable to
the Bank and to NES. A condition of effectiveness would be that the Project
Manager, Chief Administrative Officer, Chief Accountant, Chief Engineer and
Chief Technical Officer had been appointed.



- 18 -
6.08      High Level Technical Staff.  Nigeria is critically short of expe-
rienced technical personnel. Given the shortage of experienced and available
technical staff, it is likely that a farm manager, a seeds officer, a land
use planner, a road engineer, a mechanical engineer, a water and soil conser-
conservation engineer and a senior evaluation officer would have to be recruit
internationally. During negotiations assurances were obtained that their
qualifications and terms and conditions of employment would be acceptable to
the Bank and NES.
6.09      Medium Level Staff.  Professional officer (e.g., Agricultural
Officer) or senior technical officer (e.g., Agricultural Superintendent)
grades would be recruited from within the State or from other States in
Nigeria. All field technical, credit and marketing service staff would be
required to be ilausa speaking. Medium and lower level staff exist, although
many are assigned currently to non-productive posts. During negotiations
assurances were obtained from NES that a minimum of 3 Agricultural Officers,
10 Agricultural Superintendents and 48 Agricultural Assistants or experienced
agricultural instructors would be made available to the project during the
first two years.
6.10      Where possible, project staff, particularly agricultural staff,
would be appointed to the project as Government employees on full pensionable
service. Expatriate staff and other Nigerian staff such as engineers, credit
and marketing personnel would be appointed on three-year contracts renewable
on negotiation. Salaries would generally follow acceptable Government scales.
Every effort would be made to avoid transfer of staff away from the project,
except on the grounds of unsuitability, and assurances were obtained during
negotiations that transfers of senior staff into and from the project organi-
zation would be undertaken only after consultation with GOPEC.
6.11      Project staff of technical grades (or equivalent) and above would
be provided with project housing both at Gombe and in the field.  Non-
technical grades such as agricultural instructors (Al) and produce examiners
(PE), as well as clerks, drivers, messengers, etc., would be expected to
find their own accommodation in the towns and villages. At the discretion
of project management, staff would be provided with project transport.
Project staff would qualify for normal Government allowances. Annex 10,
Tables 3-4, give details. Due to expected delays in construction of houses,
costs are included for renting private accommodation (Annex 10, Table 6).
C. Staff Training
6.12      GOPMU would undertake the training of lower level staff through
special courses at project training centers (para 4.09). The training of
higher level staff would be undertaken on an inservice basis. GOPMU would
pay particular attention to training selected staff to fill managerial
positions as these are vacated by internationally recruited personnel (see
Annex 6 Tables 1-5 for possible schedule) and would undertake, as requested,
to train staff to be employed for other NES development projects. Such



- 19 -
staff would receive practical management training on the project and would
have the opportunity of decision making at various levels within the project,
but would retain executive responsiblity for their own section of the project.
NES and the Bank would ensure that adequate training of potential management
staff was undertaken, and GOPMU management would report regularly on the
progress of project training.
D. Farm Inputs - Procurement and Distribution and Credit Procedures
6.13      GOPMU would be responsible for the procurement, distribution and sale,
for cash or credit, of fertilizers, seeds, insecticides, sprayers, oxdrawn
equipment, tractors, and other necessary farm supplies; and would decide on
the suitability and composition of any package of inputs offered to farmers.
It is expected that 75% of seasonal inputs and 100% of farm equipment would
be sold on credit. The value of inputs sold during the project life is
estimated at N 6.3 million for fertilizer; N 2.0 million for insecticides,
seeds, and batteries; and N 1.6 million for farm equipment; a total of N 9.9
million.  The net cash requirement for inputs would be a total of N 6.9
million of which N 3.1 million would be the NES subsidy on fertilizers.
Annex 7 describes farm inputs and their procurement.
6.14      GOPMU marketing services would be responsible, on advice of the
technical staff, for the procurement of farm inputs and for the logistics
involved in their handling within the project area.
6.15      Currently NES subsidizes nitrogenous and phosphatic fertilizers
by between 30 and 50% of their cost delivered MNR store. The actual rate
depends on the efficiency and cost of NES handling. Under the project
farmers would receive nitrogenous and phosphatic fertilizer at 50% of cost
cif farm service center. Such a subsidy is justified (a) as an incentive
for farmers to use fertilizer, 1/ and (b) to compensate for generally low
marketing board prices for statutory marketed crops. During loan negotiations,
assurances were obtained that the present subsidy on fertilizers would be
maintained. Currently insecticides receive a 50% subsidy. However, as usage
is insignificant at present and the benefits of their use clearly discernible
and profitable, insecticides would be sold to farmers at their full cost.
Farm Credit
6.16      The organization of the delivery and recovery of farm credit is
described in Annex 8. The net incremental credit requirement and gross
credit disbursed during the five-year project period is estimated at N 2.9
million and N 4.6 million respectively.
1/   The gross cost of fertilizer is high in northern Nigeria, and to induce
the farmer to use more fertilizer subsidies are an added incentive.
Fertilizer subsidies are general throughout Nigeria.



- 20 -
6.17      GOPMU would set up a revolving credit fund to be operated by the
Credit Services Section of the project. Prior to the beginning of each
planting season, a schedule of proposed lending terms would be furnished by
GOPMU to GOPEC, which in consultation with appropriate Nigerian authorities
and the Bank would apply the lending terms for the said season. Interest
rates for subloans would be between 5 - 9-1/2%, and would be determined on
a basis of the category of farmers or type of inputs to be provided. Assur-
ances to these effects were obtained during negotiations.
6.18      As a basis for preparing crop budgets shown at Annex 1, it is
assumed that GOPMU would lend at a flat service rate of 10%, a charge which
can be afforded by the farmers and would include sufficient margin to cover
bad debts, etc. Repayments would be made for seasonal loans within 12 months;
sprayers over 2 years; ox-drawn equipment over 3 years; and tractors and
implements over 4 years. Except in cases of crop failure, when a moratorium
would be declared by GOPEC, a borrower not repaying his loan by the due date
would be charged a penalty of 1% per month. Funds have not been specifically
provided under the loan for cash credits to cover the employment of farm
labor as it is considered that any additional labor requirements would be
met from within the family unit, particularly as the project is directed
towards the small family operated farm holding. However, in the light of
current changes in Nigeria, specifically the advent of compulsory free edu-
cation, the need for hired labor may increase. During the project life
GOPMU in association with PMEU would establish the parameters, if any, for
cash credits, and the logistics involved in their disbursement and collection.
If the findings are positive, the Government and the Bank would agree on the
utilization of local funds or disbursements from undisbursed portions of the
Bank loan.
6.19      The revolving credit fund is an intimate part of the project, and
of necessity has to evolve into a more permanent institution so that farmers
can utilize its funds after the project is completed. Such an institution
might take the form of cooperatives, farmer finance companies, or branches
of state or national credit banks. So as to ensure a proper transfer of
responsibilities, the Evaluation Section of PMEU would, as one of its duties,
determine the future institutional form of the fund, taking into account the
performance of existing lending institutions and the needs of the farmers.
6.21      Operating procedures, which are described in Annex 8, would be
subject to modification as necessary, would be devised by the project credit
service but would include the following essential components: lending would
be limited to input items carried by the project at its service center stores,
and be in amounts specified on a simple farm management plan drawn up for
each borrower by extension service staff. Sale of items would be recorded
on a bookkeeping machine. As the farm service centers develop into the main
markets for cash crops, farmers would be encouraged to repay their debts to
GOPMU at time of sale. All borrowers would have a laminated identity card
containing their photograph, serial number, and signature or mark to facil-
itate identification and record keeping.



- 21 -
6.22      The creditworthiness of each loan applicant would be certified by
his hamlet or village head, or a project approved farmers association; this
would be part of the mutual guarantee policy that GOPMU would pursue. Under
this, once a year GOPMU would review all hamlet accounts, and where defaults
were found the village, hamlet or farmer association authorities, whichever
the smallest unit, would be informed of their defaulters and urged to collect
from defaulters, unless due cause is presented for default. If defaults were
not made good, GOPMU would withhold credit to all farmers in the defaulting
unit. It is believed that this technique would be a powerful influence in
avoiding default, under northern Nigerian circumstances. Provision would be
made to extend credit to groups or to village farmer associations if farmers
wish to pool the input requirements and assume joint liability for the credit.
VII. PRODUCTION, MARKETS, FARMERS' BENEFITS AND
FINANCIAL INPLICATION TO GOVERNMENT
A. Yields and Outputs
7.01      The complexity of cropping mixtures and patterns in the project
area make yield variations between farms inevitable and substantial. It has
been assumed, however, that by project maturity in 1980 project area farmers
would fall into one of two or three categories for each of the five crops
that would be influenced by the project. The categories would be determined
by the extent to which the farmer had taken up improved practices. Details
are given in Annex 1. Yield improvements and increases in gross production
due to project actions are summarized for each of the five crops:
-----Before Development             ----After Development--
Area  Av. Yield  Total Prod.       Area  Av. Yield  Total Prod.
(ha)    (kg/ha) ('000 m tons)    (ha)   (kg/ha)  ('000 m tons)
Seed Cotton        51,500      310         16          60,800      600       37
Sorghum            69,600       700        49          57,600     990        57
Maize               4,800       900         3          23,000   1,600        38
Groundnuts         10,000       500         5          12,600      860       11
Cowpea              5,000       200         1           5,600      360        2
7.02      Yield improvements are conservative and fall well within experimental
and field results obtained in northern Nigeria and other similar ecological
zones elsewhere in West Africa. The proposed crop inputs to achieve these
expected increases have been well tested, and are economic. Further details
are at Annexes 1 and 3.



- 22 -
B. Markets and Prices
7.03      Currently food crops grown in the project area are marketed via
rural markets. Marketing is not efficient since although local competition
may be strong, the market open to the farmer is very small and only a fraction
of the national market. The main defects spring from the fragmented nature
of the market and farmers' lack of both accurate information and bargaining
power; the project would seek to rectify this position (details are given in
Annex 12, paras 38 and 39).
7.04      Following a FMG announcement in early 1973, statutory marketing of
cotton and groundnuts together with other cash crops in Nigeria is undergoing
a series of organizational changes which include: (a) federal responsibility
for producer price determination and support price policies; this could lead
eventually to subsidization of primary and secondary marketing operations
by FMG; (b) abolition of producer taxes; (c) closer relationship between
world and producer prices and thus an end to indirect taxation through accu-
mulation of surpluses by the Marketing Boards; and (d) the Northern States
Marketing Board (NSMB) becoming an agent for the Nigerian Produce Marketing
Company (NPMC), the latter wholly owned by FMG now being the price controlling
and setting organization within the statutory system. The reforms are being
effected, and the 1974 producer prices reflect the actual world market situ-
ation, and should provide considerable production incentives.
7.05      As there is need for considerable improvement in primary marketing
the project would take various measures to improve marketing in general.
More specifically, the project would provide improved weighing and measuring
devices and village storage facilities, and would relocate markets to improve
the ease of crop marketing. For the statutory crops a number of marketing
reforms would be necessary, such reforms initially to be confined to the
project areas include: (a) time]y announcement of producer prices; (b) pro-
vision of adequate funds to LBA for produce purchase; (c) establishment and
enforcement of quality standards; and (d) disciplining of inefficient or
dishonest LBA and produce inspection staff. During negotiations assurances
were obtained that: (i) NES would cause the NSMB to pay particular attention
to efficient produce buying within the project area, and (ii) where necessary
encourage groups of farmers to operate as Licensed Buying Agents within the
project area.
7.06      Producer prices for industrial and export crops, such as cotton and
groundnuts, are expected under the new FMG policies to improve during the
early years of the project, but would then be expected to decline by 1980
in line with prices projected by the Bank's Economic Analysis and Projections
Department. Food crop prices are expected to decline somewhat from present
levels in consequence of more intensive cereal production in the project
area but are expected to remain substantially above world (export parity)
prices. The crop budgets shown in Annex 1 and farmers' budgets discussed
below use projected 1980 producer prices, expressed in 1974 constant Naira,
as shown in the following table:

C. Farmer Benefits
7.07      Farmers participating in project activities would obtain substantial
direct income benefits through utilizing project services and inputs. Addi-
tional indirect benefits due to improved roads, water supplies, soil conser-
vation and marketing would accrue to all farmers within the project area.
7.08      Estimated direct benefits have been calculated on a per ha basis
for the five principal crops. The summarized crop budgets below indicate
the overall benefits of improved technology over traditional practices, and
in particular show the possibilities of maize as a major cash crop within
the area.
