7.09      The report has made no attempt to produce a typical farm budget
since farm sizes and production combinations vary considerably throughout
the project area, and any such budget would not be realistic. Instead,
expected farmer benefits have been derived from a series of crop budgets for
1 ha units of production. Project actions would result in measurable addi-
tions to net family incomes totalling N 5.7 million per annum in 1980.
Studies undertaken in areas similar to the project show net farm incomes
including income from livestock and the value of produce consumed to be in
the order of N 217 (US$330) per family or about N 27 (US$41) per capita,
The net income for the five project crops accounts for about N 143 (US$217)
per family. The additional net income derived from the improved output of
the five crops would be N 98 (US$149) per family, an increase of 45%. The
average per capita income would increase from US$41 to US$60. Such an
improvement infers a growth in net farm income of 8% per annum and would
appear feasible given efficient project management.
D. Financial Implication for Government
7.10      The cash flow for NES is summarized in Annex 11, Table 2.  The
average cost to NES inclusive of loan servicing, would be about N 3.9 million
annually until 1995. Thereafter the cost would be reduced to N 2.6 million
annually. Considerable reductions, in Government support finance, could be
made if and when the fertilizer subsidy is reduced or totally removed; such
savings would amount to N 1.5 million annually. NES could further recoup up
to N 3.0 million when the revolving credit fund is institutionalized, thereby
allowing NES to retire part or all of its capital. The recent abolition of
producer taxes on statutory crops throughout Nigeria, including cotton ancl
groundnuts, would result in NES receiving no tangible financial benefits from
the project.
7.11      The Federal Government would, through secondary or tertiary multi-
plier benefits, conservatively estimated for Nigeria at between 8 to 20%,
collect indirect taxation revenues of about N 0.7 million annually.
VIII. ECONOMIC BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
8.01      The project's principal direct benefit would be the incremental
increase in farm production that it would generate, and which would result
directly in higher incomes for some 32,000 farm families, and better living
standards for a further 26,000 who would benefit through the improved infra-
structure and marketing arrangements within the project area. If the non-
farming population, traders, laborers, etc., are included, then about 640,000
people would in one way or other receive primary or secondary benefits through
project activities. The economic rate of return from investment in the project
is estimated at 17%. Important assumptions used in the calculation at Annex 13
are:



- 25 -
(a) a shadow wage rate of N 0.40 per 6-hr man/day, as against
the official rate of N 0.60;
(b) the exclusion of identif:Lable taxes from economic costs;
(c) a shadow foreign exchange rate of US$1.27 per N 1.00 as
against the official rate of US$1.52 per N 1.00;
(d) economic farm gate prices for 1980 in 1974 constant Naira
as projected by the Bank's Economic Analysis and Projections
Division (para 7.06) based on export parity for groundnuts
and cotton and import substitution for maize, sorghum and
cowpeas; and
(e) the exclusion of water and associated soil conservation
development costs, and the costs of the field evaluation
unit and feasibility studies since the benefits attributed
to them are intangible.
8.02      If no adjustment is made for the shadow rate of foreign exchange,
the economic rate of return would be reduced to 10%. It is possible that the
project might not meet the proposed production targets for a number of reasons,
including poor management, lack of farmer response, and the possible failure
of delivery of farm inputs due to uncontrollable circumstances; and as such
it is important to test the economic rate of return against various levels
of reduced production and the associated reduction in farm inputs, allowing
all other factors to remain constant. This analysis is set out below:
Production Levels as Percentage of Estimated
Project Output
100%     90%      80%     70%      60%   550%
Economic rate of return %    17        14       12       10       6        2
8.03      Such a sensitivity analysis, indicates that the project would
remain viable even when production targets fall short by as much as 30%.
Production at levels lower than 70% of target would infer the need for
restructuring of overhead investments, including reductions in staff and
associated investments. A comprehensive sensitivity analysis is shown at
Annex 13.
8.04      The project would have important secondary benefits that would
accrue both to the people of the project area, and for the State and Federation
as a whole. The more important ones would be for:
(a) the people of the project area would benefit from (a) a general
improvement in communications and public utilities, due to project
actions initiating change and providing a basis for extending,
by other Government institutions, social improvements including
health and education; (b) an expected increase in local trade



- 26 -
that would offer better opportunities of employment for the
non-fanming population; and (c) the strengthening of local
institutions including the local authorities and local
departments of Government.
(b) the North Eastern State would benefit principally through
part of its population being stabilized and becoming more
productive. Increased net employment accounting for 12,000
man years per annum would result from projected actions.
The State would use the project to test new development ideas
that could, if successful, be easily transposed together with
trained staff to other areas. The Ministry of Natural
Resources would be strengthened, and would derive from the
project, new credit and marketing techniques that could be
easily offered to other State farmers. The project would
set up seed multiplication centers that could provide seed
to other farmers elsewhere in the State. Finally, the
project would produce additional raw material and would demand
additional input resources to support the growth of the State's
industrial expansion.
(c) the Federal Republic of Nigeria would benefit from annual
net incremental foreign exchange earnings amounting at full
production to N 8.4 million (US$12.8 million). Project
evaluation through the project established evaluation unit
would provide a wealth of information that could assist in
determining Federal agricultural policy, and resource and
financial allocations throughout Nigeria.
8.05      Even though the economic rate of return appears very favorable, in
the long run indirect tangible benefits and intangible benefits may prove
far more valuable than direct tangible benefits. Improved farm management
practices and profits to farming would far outlive the project. A step would
be taken in the right direction to reduce income disparity and to ensure a
more even food supply for Nigeria's urban populations and expanding commercial
livestock industries. Perhaps most importantly, the people of the project
area would have the peace of mind that would come from more assured water
supplies in the dry season, the added confidence in an improved marketing
system, the satisfaction of a little more money to spend, and the hope that
they and their children would live a little better in the future.
IX. RECOMMENDATIONS
9.01      During negotiations assurances were obtained from FMG that:
(a) the terms of reference, qualifications and conditions of
employment of the consultants employed to undertake feasibility
studies would be mutually satisfactory to the Bank and FMG
(para 4.15); and



- 27 -
(b) a Federal Coordinating Committee would be established,
with terms of reference and composition satisfactory to
the Bank, within three months of loan effectiveness.
9.02      During negotiations assurances were obtained from NES that:
(a) any new future ginning agreement would not adversely affect
the present arrangements as to the production, marketing or
ginning of cotton within the project area (para 3.08);
(b) the forest reserve at Kumo and suitable land in the vicinity
of Tumu would be made available to GOPMU for the development
as seed multiplication farms (para 4.08);
(c) Gombe Local Authority and Tangale-Waja Local Authority or
any other appropriate agency would maintain roads and water
supplies provided under the project (para 4.16);
(d) GOPEC would be responsible for approval of GOPMU annual
budgets and monthly cash flow statements and GOPMU management
would be authorized to operate on such monthly statements
unless officially informed to the contrary by the Chairman
of GOPEC (para 5.10);
(e)  when necessary adequate guaranteed draw-down procedures
would be made with a bank of its choice to allow GOPMU
full access to funds within the limitation of the approved
annual budget (para 5.11);
(f) audited annual accounts for GOPMU would be prepared by
independent auditors, mutually acceptable to NES and the
Bank, and would be submitted simultaneously to the Bank,
NES and FMG within 4 months of the closing of the financial
year (para 5.12);
(g) the qualifications, experience and terms and conditions
of employment of the Project Manager, Chief Accountant,
Credit Manager, Chief Engineer, Chief Marketing Officer,
Chief Technical Officer and Chief Administrative Officer
would be mutually acceptable to the Bank and NES (para 6.07);
(h) a farm manager, a seeds officer, a land use planner, a
road engineer, a mechanical engineer, a water and soil
conservation engineer, and a senior evaluation officer
would be appointed with qualifications and terms and
conditions of employment mutually acceptable to the Bank
and NES (para 6.08);
(i) a minimum of 3 Agricultural Officers, 10 Agricultural
Superintendents and 48 Agricultural Assistants or experienced
agricultural instructors would be made available to the project
during the first two years (para 6.09);



- 28 -
(j) transfer of senior staff into and from the project would be
undertaken only after consultation with GOPEC (para 6.10);
(k) (i) NES would cause the NSMB to pay particular attention to
its efficient produce buying within the project area, and
(ii) where necessary encourage groups of farmers to operate
as Licensed Buying Agents within the project area (para 7.05).
9.03      Retroactive financing would be available for the employment of key
personnel (para 6.07) appointed as from date of loan negotiations amounting
to no more than US$150,000 (para 5.06).
9.04      Conditions of effectiveness of loan would be that:
(a) NES would furnish the Bank with satisfactory evidence that
a project bank account with adequate funds had been estab-
lished (para 5.11);
(b) North Eastern State Executive Council had officially
established GOPEC and GOPMU with terms of reference and
responsibilities mutually acceptable to the Bank and NES
(para 6.01); and
(c) the Project Manager, Chief Administrative Officer, Chief
Accountant, Chief Engineer, and Chief Technical Officer
would have been appointed (para 6.07).
9.05      On the basis of the above assurances, the project would be suitable
for a US$21 million loan to the Federal Republic of Nigeria.



ANNEX 1
Page 1
NIGERIA
NORTH EASTERN STATE
Gombe Agricultural Development Project
Farm and Crop Development
A. General
1.        The major crops in the project area are sorghum and millet, cotton,
groundnuts and cowpeas. Minor crops include maize, sugarcane, chilies and
okra. Production data for cotton and groundnuts are compiled from purchases
by the Northern States Marketirg Board (NSMB) (Tables 1 and 2). No production
data are available for other crops; nor is there other than qualitative in-
formation on farmers' yields, farm sizes or cropping patterns. Ecological
and sociological conditions are comparable in a number of respects between
the project area and other localities, mainly in North Central State, which
have been subjected to detailed socio-economic survey. 1/ To the extent
possible this work has been used to supplement mission judgments and local
opinion on the formulation of assumptions on present and projected farming
activities.
2.        Cattle, sheep and goats are kept throughout the project area by
farmers, and in substantially larger numbers by semi-nomadic pastoralists
(Fulani). The latter utilize fallow land (a rapidly diminishing resource),
forest and grazing reserves, and crop residues for their livestock. They
exist in an increasingly uneasy symbiosis with settled cultivators as pressure
on land increases with rising populations. Livestock management systems are
generally poor and productivity is low. There are no reliable data on stock
numbers.
3.        Cultivations are carried out mainly by hand hoe and cutlass.  How-
ever, at least 6,000 farmers are known to own work oxen and equipment. Th(
MNR tractor hiring service plows and ridges land for farmers at subsidized
rates but its overall impact is negligible.
B. The Influence of Climate on Cropping
4.        From May to October, the widest range between mean monthly maximum
and minimum temperatures is 100° to 70' F (38e C to 21e C). At project area
altitudes of 150 to 450 m cold spells do not cause any problems and rainfall
1/   An Economic Survey of three villages in Zaria Province, Samaru
Miscellaneous Paper 37, 1972: D.W. Norman.



AiNEX I
Page 2
is the major factor for limiting crop growth. Monthly and annual data for
Gombe ginnery are given at Table 3. Instead of presenting these in the
traditional manner, they have been arranged in rank order to provide an
estimate to probability First the modal value (half above and half below)
provides a demonstrable 50% probability not normally associated with arith-
metic averages. However, for a basically subsistence agriculture a more
stringent requirement is appropriate and an 80% probability has been chosen.
At this probability rainfall is still quite adequate for crop growth, April
can expect 0.2 inches and October 0.7 inches. Rainfall distribution is
critical to favorable production, and the bumper crop years have always
been associated with seasons with extended rainfall at the beginning and
end of season. Basically the farmer is a pretty busy fellow who has limit-
ed resources for carrying out his land preparation and subsequent cultiva-
tion. Thus eight years out of ten he can expect sufficient rain in April,
and occasionally as early as March, to allow timely cultivations and subsequent
planting operations (see Table 4). If the sequence is broken then late
planting particularly of cotton and sorghum occurs which result in low yields.
Provision of ox drawn equipment should allow for more timely land preparation.
At the end of the season, sometimes in September, and certainly in October,
it is important to conserve soil moisture and reduce water runoff. Thus
water conservation measures such as contour bunding and the development of
better ground cover (including closer planting of groundnuts) are all tech-
niques that should be encouraged. A progressive farmer, if taking such
measures, should have little difficulty in improving his level of output.
C. Farming Systems
5.        Although no detailed surveys have been carried out, field obser-
vations tend to verify estimates based on population and local opinion which
point to a mean farm size approximating 4.0 ha.
Size of Farm Family
6.        The Norman study 1/ reports an average farm family of 8.5.  This
figure is generally agreed to be typical of the project area. Composition of
such a family has been assumed to be as follows:
Small             Large         Female          Male          Total
Child             Child         Adult           Adult       Residents
2.2                1.5           2.6            2.2            8.5
Labor Availability
7.        Labor availability in mandays per day, on the basis of manday
equivalents used by Norman 1/ would be as follows:
1/   op cit.

Small    Large    Female    Male
Child    Child    Adult     Adult      Total
Farm Family                2.2      1.5     2.6        2.2        8.5
Manday Equivalents           -      0.5     0.75       1.0
Available Mandays            -      0.75    1.95       2.20       4.9
In the Zaria study 1/ male adult labor accounted for 89 percent of the total
family labor input; female adult labor was insignificant.  Thus, assuming 50
percent availability of labor from the large child in an average farm family,
daily labor availability would approximate 2.5 mandays.
8.        Labor requirements assessed by Norman 1/ on traditional methods
of cultivation, which imply mixed cropping and low yields, appear to be low
relative to data on similar crops grown under comparable conditions in other
areas. Substantially higher labor requirements have been assumed for pro-
posed project crops as shown in the model crop budgets (Tables 5 through 9).
9.        Adequate labor profiles are not available and, while it appears that
total labor availability (at 2.5 manday equivalents per day) is adequate for
the average small farm there may be seasonal labor shortages, particularly
at planting and weeding periods in some seasons. However, the project would
appeal primarily to the more vigorous and efficient farmers and for this rea-
son, labor availability is not regarded as a serious constraint to develop-
ment.
Cropping Patterns
10.       Intercropping is commonly practiced in a wide range of combinations.
The cropping pattern of an average farm, on the basis of adjusted areas of
sole crops might be as follows (ha):
Sorghum and Other Cereals            1.50
Cotton                               0.90
Groundnuts and Cowpeas               0.41
Others                               0.52
Fallow                               0.67
Total                           4.00
11.       This pattern may be expected to change as a result of the project
which would promote sole cropping and the introduction of improved production
practices for cotton, sorghum, maize, groundnuts and cowpeas, and would result
in changes in areas planted to different crops. Overall farm size would not
be affected by the project, however, the amount of land in fallow each year
is likely to decrease. This decrease in fallow is currently resulting in
marked decline in soil fertility, particularly in areas furthest from the

homesteads which receive no farm yard manure. Some of these deficiencies
would be made up in the short term by supply of fertilizers, and in the long
term through the introduction of pasture. A summary of changes in crorping

D. Cotton
Background
13.       Cotton of the improved Allen type (Gossypium hirsuttum) has been
grown in the project area for the past half century. Production fluctuates
widely due to the effects of climate and producer price. The mean level in
recent years approximates 16,000 tons of seed cotton, some 12 percent of mean
total production from the northern States (Table 1), and 70% of Bauchi Province
production.
14.       Seed of improved varieties produced at the Institute of Agricultural
Research (IAR) at Samaru is bulked up at the Daudawa seed multiplication farm
in the North Central State and at a temporary farm presently sited at Dadin
Kowa before distribution to farmers throughout the northern States.
15.       Seed supplies are provided free by the NSMB at a series of dumps
throughout the project area. Seed for planting is treated at the ginnery
against Bacterial Blight (Xanthomonanas malvacearum) which is eivDemic to the
area. Any one of three dressings: mercurial (3 percent) at 1:180; copper
(45 percent) at 1:180; or Bronopal (12 percent) at 1:170, are effective and
may be used.
Variety
16.       The present variety, S72 has been selected to yield highly and to
meet the needs of the textile industry. Under farmers' conditions, yields
average no more that 300 kg of seed cotton per ha, about 12% of the variety's
yield potential. Its main characteristics are shown below:
Ginning percentage                      33  )
Fibre length (32nds of an inch)         39.3)   1" to 1-1/16"
Fibre strength (g per ton)              23.1)
Yarn strength (40's) CSP /1          27766  )
/1   Count Strength Product.
Recommended Production Practices
17.       Research carried out by IAR at Samaru and at outstations has given
rise to a set of recommended practices for the crop. These presuppose a low
level of technical assistance to farmers and do not reflect optimal produc-
tion conditions. More advanced techniques including the use of ultra low



ANNEX 1
Page 6
volume (ULV) spraying for insect control are being tested under farmers'
conditions at several sites in the project area. They are likely to be fully
approved for use on a field scale under the conditions proposed for the
project in the near future.
18.       Land should be ridged on the contour at 0.91 m (3 ft) centers,
and 20-25 cm (8-10 in) high. Ridges should be completed to allow for planting
in mid-June. 6-8 seeds should be sown 2.4 cm (1 in) deep at 45 cm (18 in)
along the top of each ridge. 100 kg single superphosphate should be applied
broadcast before ridging. Three weeks after planting, 100 kg of calcium
ammonium nitrate per ha should be applied close to planting holes. Plants
should be thinned to two per stand about three weeks after planting and
preferably after a good rain, to minimize the effects of root disturbance.
Weeding and ridge maintenance should be undertaken before weed competition
is serious. Normally three weedings are required.
19.       The cotton crop is attacked by a range of pests at different
stages in the growing period.
(a) Period between germination and flowering:
No major pests, Aphis gossypii may occur but seldom
build up to serious proportions. Empoasca spp occur but
as the cotton is bred for jassid resistance no serious
damage is caused.
(b) Period of flowering and boll growth:
Pests of vegetation include the mirid Campylomma which
can be serious, Lygus which is less serious, Sylepta
derogata (F) (the cotton leaf roller), various grass-
hoppers, leaf hoppers, and leaf eating beetles occur in
August and September and are of varying seriousness
depending on the season. Pests of the flowers and bolls
include stainers, mainly Dysdercus superstitiousus (F) but
also D. Fasciatus, and the red bolloworm Diparopsis Watersi
(Roths) which are usually more serious than the American
bollworm h-eliothis artrigera (Hb), the spiny bollworm
E.arias insulana (Boisd) ard Earias biplaga (Wlk), the False
codling i-.,th crv   zr   _eucotreta (Meyr) and the pink
bollworm Pectinopnora gossypiella (Sound).
(c) Pests of bolls after the rains:
Bollworms (particu_ariy the red) and adult stainers
increase until the bolls have matured.



ANNEX 1
Page 7
20.       Ideally insecticidal applications should be timed according to
pest identification in the field. Since spotting of eggs demands considerable
skill and where pest identification may not occur, a precautionary spray
starting at 6 weeks after germination would ensure the protection of the
earlier bolls. Thereafter a succession of sprays would be undertaken and it
is likely that the advanced farmers would repeat the sprayings about 6 - 8
times.
21.       Harvesting should begin in October and be continued at regular
intervals to prevent boll spoilage. Picking should aim to avoid dirt, trash
and stained or damaged lint in order to qualify seed cotton for purchase as
Grade 1. All plants have to be uprooted and burnt by March 1, and no cotton
can be planted until June 1.
Project Proposals
22.       Increased production is expected to derive partly from increased
area planted but mainly from the adoption of new techniques.
23.       Area expansion over the project period is expected to be from
51,000 ha in 1975 to 61,000 ha in 1979, an increase of 4 percent annually.
This would come mainly from natural population growth but there would be
some area redistribution. It is estimated that the average farmer current-
ly grows 0.9 ha of cotton. At the end of the project period those using
"advanced" techniques are expected to handle about an average of 3.0 ha;
those using intermediate techniques an average 1.0 ha; and those continuing
to use traditional methods, 0.8 ha, providing an overall average of 1.00 ha
per 78,000 cotton g-owing farmers.
24.       Adoption of new techniques would be at two levels.  The use of
early planting, sole cropping, fertilizer and ULV spraying for insect control
is described as "advanced."  Such measures would result in yields averaging
1,100 kg seed cotton per ha.  The intermediate grower with more limited
resources would be unlikely to plant near the optimum date and is expected
to use about half of the inputs of the advanced farmer and would average
750 kg/ha.  Currently little or no spraying is carried out due to lack of
supply of inputs and very limited and sparsely located suppliers of water
required for the conventional high volume water based insecticides. During
the last three seasons ultra low volume sprayers (ULV) have been tested
within the project area and have proven successful. They are also being used
in an IDA financed project in Malawi, as well as in other East African coun-
tries with success. In East Africa the option for the use of high volume
sprayers exist; in the project area, due to difficulties in locating good
groundwater any large expansion of production using high volume spraying
techniques is out of the question. During the 1971/72 season in Mozambique
11,000 ha of ULV sprayed cotton averaged 666 kg/ha as against 36,000 ha of
unsprayed cotton averaging 262 kg/ha. The insecticide to be used would be
endosulphan, and would be applied at a rate of 3 liter/ha per spray.



ANNEX 1
Page 8
25.       Majority of farmers plant late and as such even under ideal con-
ditions of management the cotton crop would not yield at optimum levels
since the top bolls would not set. However, in order to assure the setting
of the bottom bolls it is essential that the farmer commences spraying before
the flower buds appear. As a crop insurance this should be at 5 weeks after
germination and continue at 10 day intervals for as much as 6 weeks, but
depending on the likelihood of continued crop production confined almost
entirely to the setting of the bottom bolls. The following table sets out
the proposed levels of technology:

practices.
E. Sorghum
Background
26.       Sharing with bulrush millet, 1/ the role of the traditional grain
staple of the Hausa people, sorghum is grown widely throughout the project
area. White to yeliowisha grained, open headed Guinea race types, known
locally as Farafara, predominate.   -e grain is normally ground to a coarse
flour aad eaten as porridge. The sLalks, which are seldom less than four
1/   Research work to date nas yielced little information on methods for
improving yields of miller, ancL existing varieties do not show economic
responses to fertilizer, therefore the project would not at this stage
take action to improve the procuction of millet.



ANNEX 1
Page 9
meters in height, are used for a variety of purposes of which building and
fencing are among the most important.
27.       Under the traditional system of cultivation, mean yields are said
to approximate 700 kg of threshed grain per ha, but they vary widely in
response to intensity of intercropping, soil and climatic effects. No data
are available on sorghum production in the project area. Nevertheless, it
is safe to conclude that the crop is grown on all holdings, alone or in a
variety of mixtures.
28.       Norman's studies 1/ indicate that sorghum occupied approximately
30% of total area planted and, whilst most commonly grown in a two crop com-
bination with bulrush millet, was also planted alone or with up to four
additional crops. Using this as a basis, it can be calculated that some
70,000 ha of sorghum are planted in the project area which, at an average
yield of 700 kg/ha, would produce some 49,000 tons or about 5% of estimated
production from North Eastern State.
29.       Although its effects have not been quantified, the parasitic weed
Striga hermontheca is probably the most significant pest of sorghum. Others
include a range of stalk borers, but particularly Busseola fusca, and Sorghum
Midge Contarinia sorghicola. Major diseases include Downy Mildew, Sclerospora
sorghi, a variety of leaf diseases causing spotting and striping, and a number
of smut diseases, mainly of the genus Sphacelotheca. The latter are to a
considerable extent controlled by seed dressing with copper fungicides which
are readily available commercially.
30.       As befits a staple grain, sorghum is normally planted on the main
farm in close proximity to the homestead. Prior to planting land is plowed
and ridged by hand hoe or occasionally mechanically, at approximately 0.9
meters between rows and varying distances within rows according to inter-
cropping practices. Sorghum is planted in the project area ir. May/June,
after such crops as early millet. Two or three weedings and earthings-up
are normally carried out. The crop flowers at the onset of the dry season,
in late September/early October, and matures, utilizing residual soil moist-0,
by late November/December.
31.       The stalks are cut near ground level and stacked in the field af.
removal of the heads, which are bundled and stored in mud plastered granaries
(rumbus) in the farm compound. Heads are removed from the granaries as
required for domestic consumption or for sale following threshing. Storage
losses are variable, one estimate is that on average 20% of the crop is lost
in store, and though control rethods involving granary sanitation and the use
of insecticides have been developed, they are not commonly used.
Recommended Practices
32.       Research on sorghum has been carried out in the northern States of
Nigeria for tbe past 23 years.           yiger yielding varieties have been selected
from local types. A number of dwarf varieties and hybrids have also been
1/  op. cit.



ANNEX 1
Page 10
developed. This work has been accompanied by investigations including time
of planting, spacing, and fertilizer responses. Improved local varieties
grown under improved agronomic practices showed yield gains over traditional
types of six to 25 percent (averaging 18.6% or about 240 kg/ha) in over 90
trials planted at different sites in main sorghum production areas up to 1970.
Such increases are of little interest to farmers. The main thrust of sorghum
research is now directed towards production of high yielding dwarf varieties
and hybrids. Materials now available has a yield potential under field
conditions of around 2,500 kg/ha and 4,000 kg/ha from dwarf varieties and
hybrids respectively. The grain from these types is not yet widely acceptable
for human food in rural areas but it can be sold readily in urban areas for
human and livestock food.
Project Proposals
33.       Total sorghum production in Nigeria has been estimated to approximate
four million tons per year, of which between five and ten percent is marketed.
Prices have risen overall in the past three years. However, any major pro-
duction increase could be expected to result in a marked fall in price. This
situation is unlikely to arise in the near future - in the light of the in-
effectiveness of existing extension, input supply and credit services ir the
northern States as a whole. Within the proposed project area, however, there
are good opportunities for promoting some increase in sorghum production,
utilizing available technology and proposed project services, despite the
fact that many farmers are likely to continue to grow at least some local
sorghum under traditional cultivation methods. In addition to the switch
from traditional to improved varieties of sorghum that is envisaged, it is
also believed that a substantial amount of land which in the past has been
used to grow sorghum will, under the project, be used for maize, see para. 38.
34.       One level of improved technology has been assumed, based upon
results from field trials and research recommendations.

F. Maize
Background
36.       Although well known, maize is presently a minor crop in the project
area. Cultivated mainly in crop mixtures on swampy areas (fadamas) primarily
as a vegetable for eating green - boiled or baked on the cob - it is unlikely
that total area exceeds 4,000 ha.
37.       High yielding, short season maize varieties and hybrids have been
developed recently by IAR and other researchers in the northern States.
Their promising performance under Northern Guinea zone conditions have created
marked interest in maize as a grain crop for the area. Trials at Daudawa,
North Central State, under different levels of plant population and fertilizer
rates using variety (096) Bulk 3 gave yields ranging from 2,000 to over 7,000
kg/ha of threshed grain depending on the level of treatments applied. Responses
under farmers' conditions in the North Central State in 1969/71 ranged from
1,400 through 2,400 to 4,000 kg/ha using comparable treatments. Planting
material with further improved yield potential is in course of development
and is likely to be available for multiplication and distribution to farmers
within two years.
Project Proposals
38.       These facts and the observation that the yellow grained varieties
produce meal which is an acceptable (some say a preferred) substitute for
sorghum meal in traditional porridge in North Eastern State, indicate that
the probability that the maize area and production could be increased markeAlv
under the project. Recent trends in maize prices, the burgeoning urban
population in the southern States, and the increasing demand for livestock
feeds and for industrial maize products provide further justification for
maize promotion under the project. Probable area increases are expected to
be at the expense of sorghum and millet, and would occur mainly on the farmns
of more progressive farmers. An expected increase in maize area from 4,000
to 23,000 ha of which 3,500 ha would be grown at the advanced levels on plots
averaging 1 ha, 16,000 ha (70 percent) would be grown under intermediate
technology on 0.75 ha plots and 3,500 ha (15 percent) would be grown under
traditional systems on 0.25 ha plots. About 39,000 farmers are expected to
grow maize within the project (Table 7 shows per hectare budget).

have been based on field trials and practices advocated by workers at the

G. Groundnuts
Background
40.       Groundnuts (Arachis hypogeas) are grown throughout the project area
but are of greatest commercial importance in drier, northern locations which
are cliniatically less well suited to cotton production. Production levels
m_v be inferred from purchases by the NSMB which have averaged about 5,000
tons annually in recent years (Table 2).
41.       The Nigerian groundnut crop is grown primarily for oil and average
kernels contain about 47Z oil with a Free Fatty Acid (FFA) content of 2 to
3 percent. Yields of groundnuts grown under traditional systems, which
include multiple intercropping, wide spacing and late planting, vary con-
siderably but are probably of the order of 500 kg/ha of shelled nuts. At
this yield level and accepting the above production assumption, area planted,
on a sole crop basis is about 10,000 ha. The high labor requirement and
the present comparatively unattractive returns from the crop probably limit
groundnut plot sizes to about 0.5 ha on farms which grow the crop for sale
to the NSMB.



ANNEX 1
Page 13
42.       A long series of agronomic trials and a number of selection and
breeding programs by staff of the IAR have resulted in the formulation of a
package of recommended practices for groundnut production. These regularly
lead to a doubling of kernel yields over traditional practices. More
advanced technology, including the use of fungicidal sprays to control
cercospora leaf spot disease is still under investigation and may be avail-
able for dissemination in the near future. However, a number of questions
still attach to the technical and economic viability of certain advanced
techniques.
43.       For these reasons, only one level of improved technology has been
suggested. This approximates to the practices recommended by ERLS. The
practices proposed, which are outlined below, would be fostered by the
provision of a physical package of inputs including seed, seed dressing,
fertilizer and advice. This would follow the lines of a successful ground-
nut seed production project in neighboring Kano State.
44.       The proposed level of improved technology would incorporate timely
planting, i.e., shortly after the first rains, on land which has been prepared
as for cotton (para 18). Fertilizer, single superphosphate at 75 kg per ha,
should be applied as a band in the old furrows before splitting to make
new ridges. Seed would be of the variety S61 which should be shelled shortly
before planting and dressed) with Aldran T or Fernasan D against seed and soil
borne diseases. Seed should be planted at two every 23 cm (9 in) on the top
of the ridges i.e., a seed rate of 40 kg/ha. By so doing, the foliar cover
achieved would be invaluable in preventing run off during the heavy rainfall
in August. Furthermore, after the early weeding and earthing, such ground
cover would reduce the frequency of weeding which must cease once pegging 1/
starts. Harvesting should begin immediately the crop reaches maturity. Pod
damage should be avoided by careful lifting. The crop should be dried by
inverting plants on the ridge i.e., with pods uppermost for about six days.
Thereafter the pods should be handpicked and transported to an appropriate
site for decortication. Care is needed, particularly in lifting, drying and
picking to avoid the creation of conditions favoring the development of the
fungus Aspergillus flavus, responsible for the carcinogen, aflatoxin, which
has been transmitted on mouldy groundnuts.
Project Proposals
45.       The improved groundnut technology proposed for the project is already
well established. Its rate of spread has been limited largely by the in-
adequacy of technical assistance and input supply services. For these reasons,
the rate of uptake under the project is expected to be rapid. By Year 5 (1979),
some 12,000 farmers are expected to be using the improved technology.
1/   Shoots which eventually bear the groundnut turn down into the Boil prior
to pod formation.

H.  Cowpeas
Background
46.       Cowpeas (vigna unguiculata) occupy an important place in traditional
Nigerian diets both in the North and South.  They provide valuable vegetable
protein for subsistence farmers and some cash income when surplus production
is sold. The crop is seldom planted sole and normally forms one part of a
number of crop mixtures of varying complexity. Under these circumstances
yields vary widely. Further, because of the range of insect pests to which
it is susceptible both before and after flowering, yields rarely average more
than 200 kg/ha of shelled peas. Local varieties have both upright and spread-
ing characteristics; those producing a pale (whitish) colored seed are preferred.
47.       Research on the crop has isolated major insect pests and established
the importance of their control for increased production. Major pests include
Ootheca mutabilis, a leaf eating beetle, Alcidodes leucogrammus, a weevil
which, in both the adult and larval forms, causes stem girdling, Maruca
testulalis, the larva of which bore into and feed on young flowers causing
them to drop, Rezotrachelus varium, a weevil which lays its eggs in green pods
wherein the larvae feed and pupate within the seeds. Further, a number of bugs
in the order Hemiptera feed on green pods causing premature drying and shri-
velling.
48.       Tlmproved Practices, including so'le cropping, the use of improved
upright varieties, phosphatic fert_lizer a-d insecticidal spraying result in
ten fold yield increases over tracitional yields. The latter treatment on
sole cropped cowpeas is th.e mnost s4ignificant.  Present recommendations are
for six weekly sprays containing 20 oercent DDT and two percent gamma BHC at
the rate of 5.5 liters of concentrate mixed in 109 liters of water per ha.
This recommendation is under review following investigation of ULV techniques.



ANNEX 1
Page 15
Project Proposals
49.       No data are available on the area presently planted with cowpeas
but it is unlikely that the crop is grown widely in sold stand. The project
would introduce cowpeas as a sole crop to those farmers who wished to grow
cotton using improved and advanced techniques since such farmers would already
own ULV sprayers and be competent in their use. It is not expected that large
plots would be planted, and a mean size of 0.25 ha has been assumed.
50.       One level of technology is proposed.  Its main element is summarized
below and is compared with the traditional system.

shelled peas (kg/ha)
51.       It is expected that uptake of improved technology would closely
follow cotton development since materials, equipment and techniques for
spraying cowpeas approximate those used for cotton, and the benefits are
considerable. For these reasons it is expected that most farmers with one
season's experience of spraying cotton would also grow cowpeas with project
advice and assistance. Furthermore, a small proportion could be expected
to introduce improved technology to their cotton and cowpea crops simul-
taneously (for detailed budget and crop projections see Tables 10 and 15).

ANNEX 2
Page 1
NIGERIA
NORTH EASTERN STATE
GOMBE AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPNENT PROJECT
Project Area, Institutions and Existing Processing and Marketing
A.   Project Area
1.        Bordered by the Gongola river on its north eastern side, and by
the Kaltungo Hills to the south, the project area lies between latitudes
9*45' and 10030' N, and longitudes 10°45' and 12°00' E. It extends to about
5,300 km2 or some two percent of the total area of North Eastern State. The
project area covers Ako, Yamalut and Gombe districts in Gombe emirate, West
Tangale district and the plains in the north of Waja and Kaltungo districts
in the Tangale Waja Federated Local Authority area. Gombe town in the
north of the project area is the headquarters of the Gombe Administrative
Division. Map shows location of the proposed project within Nigeria.
Climate
2.        Annual rainfall, confined almost entirely to the 7 months April
through October, is of the order of 900 mm (35 ins) with a 75 percent pro-
bability of obtaining 825 mm. Between November and March, there is virtually
no rain; this long, dry season is rendered very harsh by hot dry north-
easterly "harmattan" winds blowing from the Sahara.
3.        Beginning in April, when there is a 50 percent chance of obtaining
45 mm of rain (1.8 in), through September which has a 50 percent probability
of more than 168 mm (6.7 in), rainfall distribution is well suited to a
range of annual crops. However, due to limitations in timely land prepara-
tions and to a labor bottleneck at planting time, few farmers can plant all
their crops at the optimum time. Food crops are given priority over cash
crops, and the success of the latter is dependent on the farmer's residual
input resources, and because they are plar,ted late, on the vagaries of
climate. Gombe is more fortunate than some other parts of the Northern
States in that it is assured of some rainfall in April and October. In
particular the April rainfall allows for more time for planting of food
crops and hence the higher yields of cotton currently attained.
Water
4.        There are few perennial streams in the area, and groundwater
supplies are severely limited by the presence of the impervious underlying
basement complex.  With increasing pressure on land, the flow period of
streams has diminished markedly.  Wells comfnonly fail during the dry months,



ANNEX 2
Page 2
leading to prolonged water shortages which are becoming an increasingly
critical issue for both human and livestock populations. Fortunately, topo-
graphy is such that construction of small reservoirs appears a practicable
solution to the water problem.
Topography and Soils
5.        The area is underlain largely by sandstones of the Cretaceous period
with some tertiary sandstones to the west. The land form changes from rolling
plateaux in the west to undulating sandstone and clay plains in the east.
Altitudes range from 450 m in the west and south to 150 m in the east. A
number of small, ephemeral streams occur throughout the area, pointing to its
suitability for the proposed program of small reservoir construction.
6.        Soils vary widely from very deep red sandy loams in the west through
sandy to sandy clay loams to cracking clays derived from Cretaceous shales in
the east. The soils of the area are complex. The sandy soils have been
classified generally as leached ferruginous tropical soils generally without
iron concretions. The cracking clays are lithomorphic yertisols (known in
many parts of Africa as 'black cotton soils'). Drainage on these soils is
moderate to poor and they require careful management to maximize their
considerable crop production potential.
7.        Because the sandy soils are less difficvlt to cultivate with un-
sophisticated equipment much of the area of these soils has in fact been
overcropped and is probably low in organic matters. Fertilizer responses
at low application rates have been established for major crops on such soils,
but information concerning higher levels is not yet available. Nor is it
certain that the very high rates would provide economic returns. Less is
known about fertilizer responses on the cracking clays; however field trials
are underway on similar soils near Numan to the south east of the project
area. They should provide relevant information for refining fertilizer
recommendations for these soils.

Anogeissus and Combreturn spp or the sands and sandy loams to Acacia seyal
and A.  nolycantha savanna w.)od and on the heavier soils.  Grassland associa-

Land Use Capability
9.        According to Land Resource Study No. 9, 1/ the project area is
composed largely of land in Classes II and III with patches of Classes IV
and V confined mainly to the western quarter. The classification is related
to that used by the USDA and is biased towards mechanization. However, it
indicates clearly the project area is well suited to hand or ox farming,
and that, given attention to soil conservation practices, a degree of mech-
anization would be feasible in high potential areas.
Population
10.       The project area is occupied by people of the Hausa tribe, most
of whom are devout moslems. Literacy in English, Nigeria's official language,
and Hausa, the vernacular, is low among adults in rural areas, but is rising
rapidly with the younger generation. Available data are scant. The last
census, from which data are available was carried out in 1963 and remains
the best source of population data until the results of the December 1973
census become available in detail later in 1974. Such data will be of
immeasurable value in detailed project planning and evaluation.
11.       According to the census, population of the parts of the six districts
constituting the project area totalled 457,600 in 1963. A growth rate of
2.5 percent annually is the one normally used in connection with rural popu-
lations in the northern States. Population in the project area has probably
grown somewhat more rapidly due to immigration from other northern States.
For this reason, a growth rate of three percent per annum has been used.
At this rate, population of the project area would be 633,500 by 1974.
12.       Data on numbers of persons per family in the project area are
also sparse. No demographic surveys have been carried out. Tax data are
inconclusive due to marked variability between years and districts, probably
due in part to immigration. In the absence of data applicable to the project
area as a whole, 8.5 persons were arbitrarily selected as representing average
family size. This was in accordance with local opinion and is in line with
estimates used in other similar projects in the northern States. Thus, on
the basis of a family size of 8.5, and a population of 633,500, there would
be some 74,000 families in the area by l974. Numbers of farming families
were calculated on the assumption that about 22 percent would be engaged
primarily on non-agricultural activities. This figure was derived from
census data and observations on non-agricultural activities in the area.
On this basis, there would be about 58,000 farming families in the project
area by 1974.
1/   Transitional between the Sudan and Northern Guinea zones.  For a fuller
description of the project environment refer to Land Resource Study 9:
The Land Resources of North East Nigeria, 1972, Volumes 1-5 by Aitchison
et al, Land Resources Division, Foreign and Commonwealth Office, Overseas
Develop;.ent Administration, England.



ANNEX 2
Page 4
13.       It is proposed to divide the project area into five agricultural
development units (see Map). The estimated number of farm families attributed
to each unit would be as follows:
Biliri Unit             13,000
Kumo Unit               18,000
Gombe Unit              11,000
Wagari Unit              8,500
Telassie Unit            7,500
14.       The social structure of the area is significant because of the
relative strength and influence of the Local Authorities concerned. Part of
the project comes within the jurisdiction of the Emir of Gombe, the tradi-
tional ruler in three of the six districts affected; the remainder is in the
area of the Tangala Waja Local Authority whose Chairman has considerable
standing. In each case the Local Authorities are represented in their areas
by salaried district and village heads. Among other functions these officials
are responsible for the administration of land tenure arrangements at their
respective levels. The system operates on the allocation and maintenance of
customary usufructory rights. It is based on the grant of such rights on
land which is not so allocated, to any native Nigerian who expresses the in-
tention of settling and farming the land in question. This has happened to
a considerable extent in recent years in consequence of immigration by farmers
from more densely settled areas. There are now indications that a large part
of the surplus farm land has been allocated. Thus the system will continue
(as in other areas) such that family heads will sub-allocate land to family
members in accordance with assessed need and capability.  However, in the
event of dispute, the village heads are normally the first source of appeal.
15.       As representative of the Local Authority, village heads have con-
siderable political influence within their areas. Although this influence
is being eroded in the urban areas, such as the wards of Gombe town, it is
still considerable in the villages. For this reason, every effort would be
made to obtain the support of village heads for the project by acquainting
them with proposed and ongoing developments within their areas. District
heads would be similarly consulted and informed. Furthermore, as indicated
in Chapter VI, provision is made for Local Authority representation on the
Project Committee.
Farm Size
16.       Information on farm size is scant.  It is possible to impute a
possible mean farm size on the basis of assumed population, family size and
estimated area of cultivated land. The latter figure is estimated by NES
at 232,000 ha or 44% of total project area.  Given 58,000 farm families



ANNEX 2
Page 5
then average size of cultivated holding, i.e., excluding fallow, would be
4.0 ha.  This figure is in reasonable accord with local opinion in MNR and
Local Authority officials at district level concerned with land allocation to
immigrants in recent years.
Cropping Patterns
17.       Cropping patterns are dominated by sorghum and millet as food
grains, and cotton and groundnuts as the major cash crops. A diversity of
minor crops are grown. Local knowledge and socio-economic surveys in neigh-
boring parts of the northern States indicate that sorghum and millet typically
occupy about 45% and cotton about 25% of cultivated areas. In the north-
western part of the area cotton gives way to groundnuts-on the sandy free
draining soils. Minor crops, usually grown in mixtures with the main cereals
and cash crops, include maize, chilies, kenaf, okara and a range of vegetables.
Communications
18.       The project area is served by two main roads:  the trunk highway
connecting the north east of Nigeria with Jos and the south, passes through
the northern part, branching southwards near Combe to Numan on the Benue
river. These roads are tarred and are maintained by the Federal and State
Governments. In addition, Gombe lies on the Nigerian Railway Corporation
main line from Maiduguri (the State capital) to Jos and the south of Nigeria.
This line runs through the north of the project area and has significance as
a major channel for input delivery and produce evacuation to and from Gombe
town. There are no scheduled airports but there is an airstrip at Gombe.
Telephone facilities also exist at Gombe, operated by the Posts and Telegraphs
Department of the Federal Ministry of Communications.
19.       Unfortunately, the basic communication network barely extends beyond
that described above. Minor roads are non-existent or in poor repair.
Communities are seasonally cut off from each other and the outside world.
Transportation of produce and inputs to rural areas is both difficult and
expensive. Attempts to improve the situation by self help activities and
by the Local Authority Works Departments are hampered by shortages of funds
and executive capacity.
B. Institutions
20.       Institutions that would be concerned with the project and their
relevant responsibilities are described in the following paragraphs.



ANNEX 2
Page 6
The Ministry of Natural Resources (MNR)
21.       MNR would have the responsibility for carrying out the project.
The Ministry has representatives of its four technical divisions within the
project area, and it operates a cotton seed multiplication farm at Dadin
Kowa 1/ (see Map). While the functions of its Field Services and Produce
Inspection Divisions within the area would be incorporated into the project
organization, the administration of the two other Divisions would remain un-
changed. Thus, animal health, organization of grazing facilities and live-
stock improvement services, largely for the semi-nomadic Fulani pastoralists,
would continue to be provided by the Veterinary Division; and the Forestry
Division would continue its work, which is concerned mainly with the regulation
of reserved land, and planting of woodlots. These functions would be largely
unaffected by the project.
The Ministry of Works (MOW)
22.       Responsible for State-financed public works program, MOW has limited
facilities and a small technical staff deployed within the project area; MNW
is concerned mainly with road maintenance, building construction, and the
maintenance of State-financed structures. Executive capacity relative to
the State construction program is a major limitant to its operations.
The Ministry of Health (MOH)
23.       Responsible for all aspects of human health in the State, MOH is
in process of developing its services and facilities. Within the project
area, a Government hospital provides treatment services for both in-and-out-
patients at Gombe. This provides the referral link between the sparse network
of district dispensaries and the specialized hospital facilities operated
by MOH at the State headquarters at Maiduguri. It is expected that Rural
Health Centers and a closer network of dispensaries providing more compre-
hensive services are to be included in the forthcoming development plan
(1974-1978) of MOH.
Ministry of Industry, Trade and Cooperatives (MITC)
24.       MITC provides services to industrial investors and is involved,
for example, with the development of the new oil seed crushing mill being
constructed at Gombe. More relevantly it is responsible for the administra-
tion of cooperative societies within the State.
25.       Responsibility for the cooperative movement in North Eastern State
was transferred recently from MNR to MCDC. In common with other northern
States, cooperatives in North Eastern have made little impact on the bulk
of the rural population. Success has been hampered by shortage of support
1/   Currently leased from the New Nigerian Development Corporation.



ANNEX 2
Page 7
and supervisory staff, and by political interference in the early to mid-
1960's leading to widespread indebtedness. Primary producer and marketing
societies organized in the project area at that time formed Cooperative
Unions. Neither the primary societies nor the unions gained the full support
of farmers. In common with the cooperative movement elsewhere in the northern
States, these groups are to a greater or lesser extent indebted to the State
Government for preseason and marketing loans and are consequently inoperative.
The Goambe Cooperative Union is an exception to this general observation,
nevertheless its performance as a produce buyer for the NSNB leaves much to
be desired and it is heavily dependent on State Government for its day to
day management. For these reasons, little emphasis is placed on the coopera-
tive movement in the initial phases of proposed credit and marketing arrange-
ments for the project. On the other hand, participation by cooperative
organizations in project activities, particularly marketing, would be en-
couraged, provided that project management was satisfied as to their vi-
ability and competence.
The Local Authority
26.       The project area comes under the control of the Gombe Local Autho-
rity (GLA) and the Tangale Waja Local Authority (TWLA). Their main functions
include the collection of tax in respect of people and cattle (haraji and
jangali respectively); the administration of farming land allocations; and
the provision of services such as feeder road construction and maintenance,
dispensaries, and primary education as a complement to State Government
activities in these fields.
27.       Two functions of the LA are of immediate and direct importance
to the project. The 'irst relates to the need for open support and encourage-
ment of project activities and the cooperation of LA officials with project
management. Project concept and proposed activities were discussed at several
levels with both the Local Authorities. Reactions were favorable and full
cooperation was promised. The second is in connection with the maintenance
of the road network which the project would construct. In the course of the
development period the road construction unit of the project would liaise
closely with both the LA Works Departments in order to foster a smooth hand-
over of responsibility at full development.
The Institute of Agricultural ,esearch (IAR)
28.       Created in the early 1960's from the Research Division of the
then Northern Nigeria Ministry of Agriculture, IAR is a semi-autonomous body
linked with Ahmadu Bello University (ABU). Its governing body has represen-
tation from the Ministries of Agriculture and Natural Resources of the six
northern States. Its main researeh station is located at Samaru, near Zaria.
Further details on the organization and output of IAR are in Annex 3.
29.       IA1_ preserves contacLs vi:" :;ea' uarters and field staff of lMNR.
Field trials are carried out on an agency basis on MNR sites, supervised



ANNEX 2
Page 8
by ministry staff under the technical guidance of IAR researchers. Research
recommendations are conveyed to extension staff via the Extension Research
Liaison Service (ERLS) of IAR.
30.       While IAR has directed a major part of its resources to fundamantal
plant breeding in the search for improved high yielding varieties, wlhich
must continue, it must do more to find solutions to the practical everyday
problems of small farmers who struggle with seasonal limitations of labor
and severe shortages of capital and other resources. A practical program of
applied research aimed at identifying and resolving farmers' problems would
make IAR's program more immediately useful to the people it is intended to
serve.
Nigerian Agricultural Bank (NAB)
31.       NAB is a Federal Government institution which was inaugurated in
March 1973, with head offices in Kaduna. UNDP has provided financial assistance
to employ consultants to plan the role of NAB and to manage it for three
years; the Bank is executive agency. Four expatriate senior management staff,
including the General Manager, have been employed; and Nigerian officers have
been recruited.
Northern States Marketing Board (NSMB)
32.       NSMB is responsible for organizing the domestic purchase of controlled
crops, cotton and groundnuts at prices predetermined by FMG. As from the
1973/74 season, NSNB became purchasing agent of the Federal Nigerian Produce
Marketing Company (NPMC), under a new FMG program to improve crop purchasing.
Crop purchases take place at legally gazetted markets by Licensed Buying
Agents (LBA) of NSMB. In 1972, there were 41 cotton markets and 19 groundnut
buying points within the project area.
Cotton and Agricultural Processors Ltd. (CAP)
33.       CAP (formerly the British Cotton Growers' Association (Nigeria) Ltd.)
has had a monopoly for ginning Nigerian cotton since exports began over half
a century ago. CAP has ginneries throughout the cotton growing zones of the
northern States. In addition to ginning and lint classification, CAP arranges
for seed dressing against Bacterial Blight, seed distribution to village dumps,
and transportation of lint and seed as directed by NSNB. CAP is efficient and
capable of expanding its operations to meet the needs of the project. The
present ginning agreement expires in 1976, and CAP is exploring with State
Governments the possibilities of greater local participation in the industry.
C. Processing and Marketing
Cotton
34.       All cotton purchased for NSNB in the project area is ginned at
CAP facilities located either at Gombe or Kumo (see Map). Some increase in



ANNEX 2
Page 9
effective capacity would probably be needed towards the end of the project,
and CAP has indicated its willingness to make the necessary arrangements, in
consultation with MNR and NSMB.
35.       The gazetted cotton markets usually are temporary structures made
from grass mats, and are located on the basis of anticipated purchases
(minimum 50 tons/year) and distance between markets (minimum 8 km). A minimum
of two LBA is mandatory for each market, which is controlled by a Produce
Examiner (PE) from the Produce Inspection Division (PID) of the MNR. The
PE are responsible for the visual grading of seed cotton into one of two
grades, and should ensure that LBA pay correct amounts to farmers for their
produce. The PE are severely criticized as they seldom perform these func-
tions; apparently grading is virtually non-existent, and farmers allege that
many PE conspire with LBA to cheat farmers. The project would seek to correct
these deficiencies.
Cotton Seed
36.       Currently cotton seed is dressed against fungal infection by CAP
and is returned to the farmer. In the handling processes, enormous losses
occur which could be substantially reduced were an acid delinting process
employed. Through this, the seed germination rate would be improved, thus
requiring less seed to be returned to the farmer and releasing a greater
surplus of seed for the crushing industry. NSMB would request CAP to install
acid delinter equipment at their gin in Kumo; CAP have agreed in principal
to this suggestion.
Groundnuts
37.       All groundnuts purchased for NSNB are inspected and graded by PID
staff. Normally, this takes place after the purchases from farmers have been
made by LBA at buying points licensed by LA. Groundnut buying points like
cotton markets, are not properly supervised by PID and there are frequent
allegations that LBA manipulate weigh scales and prices at the expense of
farmers.
Other Crops
38.       Control is normally not exercised on the marketing or handling of
other crops, such as sorghum, maize and cowpeas.  Xowev r, LA has the power,
if agreed by NES, to restrict maovement GE toodcrops in times of shortage.
Such powers have been invoked in poor seasons from time to time.
39.       There is no national food zro- market as such, but rather a series
of fairly highly organized, But disconnected, local centers.  Consequently,
markets are thin and marke, tem;ra  a,. s?acial -rice irregularities occur.
The project would aim to improve  at;ers by the introduction of a market
intelligence service, increas_< .u.ers bargaining power through crop
assembly, and pzoviding Qarkc. ._      zc  ai," '-.ere appropriate helping
farmers groups to enter into contrac:s with large consumers such as livestock
feed producers and commercial starch facror_vs.
