NIGERIA
NORTH EASTERN STATE
Gombe Agricultural Development Project
Research and Seed Multiplication
A.   Research Implementation
Institution
1.        Technical and rural economic research pertaining to the project
area is the responsibility of the semi-autonomous Institute of Agricultural
Research (IAR). The Institute was created in the early 1960's and is linked
to the Ahmadu Bello University (ABU) at Zaria. It has its main station and
administrative headquarters at Samaru adjoining the ABU campus. IAR serves
the six northern States. Senior officials from their Ministries of Agri-
culture and Natural Resources (MANR) help to formulate IAR policy and ap-
prove research programs.
2.        The Institute has a staff of over 150 professionals, and modern
and expanding facilities. It has established apparently good working rela-
tions with other research institutions in Nigeria and the West African region.
Its standing in research circles is acknowledged to be high. Over the years
it has produced a w4de range of research results relating to most of the crops
commonly grown in the northern States. Implementation of the results has
been hampered by lack of technical assistance and input delivery services at
the village and farmer levels; and a tendency for research to be unrelated
to the conditions encountered on farmers' farms. The latter weakness has been
high-lighted recently as a result of the activities of the Agricultural Econo-
mics Department of IAR, whose staff are cooperating with technical personnel
in the formulation of research recommendations more nearly related to farmers'
needs and capabilities than has hitherto been the case.
3.        Responsibility for transmissioa of research results to extension
staff is vested in the Extension Research Liaison Service (ERLS) of IAR.
Headed by a deputy director of the Institute, ERLS produces a range of audio
and visual aids materials suitable for use by farmer contact extension staff.
The service is responsible for formulating draft recommended practices for
farmers.  These are based on research results and an assessment of the level
of supervision and technical competence of field level extension staff employed
by the State MANR.  This is generally low, hence recommended practices tend
to be simple and conservative in order to avoid the hazard of loss of confi-
dence by farmers in extension staff who give wrong advice (due to lack of
comprehension) or who are unable to arrange for supplies of necessary inputs.
ERLS has a staff of some 20 professionals and support personnel. Its chief
would be willing to tailor extension material to meet proposed project needs,
and to assist with its production.



ANNEX 3
Page 2
4.        The use of IAR research results as a basis for recommendations by
ERLS to farmers has limitations. The farmer does not have the ideal condi-
tions for crop growth enjoyed by IAR on its stations. His resources are
limited, which necessitates a cropping pattern dictated by his limited
resources. What is now urgently required is a range of experimental work
to show what can be accomplished under the conditions of crop successions
which the environment and limited manpower resources impose on the farmer.
5.        Demonstrations, on both individual holdings and seed multiplication
farms (see Schedule 1) using applicable IAR recommended inputs and improved
seed varieties, would play an important part in teaching new techniques to
farmers. These demonstrations must be of a very high standard to have a real
impact.
Research Programs and Recommendations
6.        Programs on the five crops proposed for the project have been
ongoing for many years. Thus, cotton has been the subject of continuing
research in the major disciplines over the past four decades. Programs are
staffed mainly by personnel of the Cotton Research Corporation (CRC). They
have resulted in the development of improved varieties. Thus S 72, which is
recommended for the project area, has a yield potential under Samaru condi-
tions of well over 2,000 kg of seed cotton per ha. Its main characteristics
are listed in Annex 1. In addition, agronomic practices, fertilizer res-
ponses, pest and disease problems of crop have been clearly defined and,
at least, partially solved, so that farm yields of about 1,100 kg can be
expected over wide areas.
7.        The cotton research 'rogram has provided definite recommendations
on variety, cultural practices and fertilizer applications applicable to
the project area. Currently it is in the course of evaluating systems of
insect control using ultra-low-volume (ULV) spraying techniques on a field
scale at different sites in th2 proposed Funtua project area of the North
Central State. Advice on ULV techniques suitable for farmers is likely to
be forthcoming early in 1974. These would be similar to techniques used by
small scale cotton producers in other parts of Africa. Annex 1 contains
the essence of research recommaendations for the cotton crop plus proposals
for ULV spraying based on experience elsewhere. Future work in the cotton
research program should include testing varieties that have a short growing
period and can thus be planted late and still have a reasonable yield expec-
tation. Late planting of cotton is a practice forced on many farmers because
of limited manpower resources.
8.        Groundnuts have been the sUbject of close research interest and
substantial effort over the past two decades. High yielding varieties suit-
able for the project area have been developed and a package of inputs and
practices capable of at least doubling farmer's yields has been available
for some years.



ANNEX 3
Page 3
9.        Improved practices proposed for the project are based on current
recommendations by ERLS. They include the use of the seed of the variety
S61, seed dressing with Aldran T or Fernasan D, single superphosphate ferti-
lizer at 75 kg per ha, early planting and close spacing. It may be that
ongoing research on control of cercospora leaf spot will lead shortly to
the possibility of incorporating this treatment in practices proposed for
groundnuts in the project area. This should be kept in mind, along with
opportunities for production expansion through new varieties and/or variations
in fertilizer dressing, all of which are under investigation.
10.       Sorghum research has progressed from attempts to improve local
varieties into the development of dwarf varieties and hybrids suitable for
the differing ecological conditions encountered in the northern States. Such
material has a yield potential of at least five times greater than farmers
presently obtain. Selection and breeding programs have been underway for
the past 20 years with substantial assistance in the past decade from the
US through USAID programs involving USDA, USARS and several universities.
Cooperative programs with other West African sorghum producers have also
been operated through IAR.
11.       The outcome of this work, and related agronomic investigations, has
not yet reached the stage at which ERLS is able to issue a series of recommended
practices for general distribution. However, highly promising results have
been obtained from a number of trials, replicated at Daudawa (North Central
State) and a number of other sites in ecologically comparable locations.
These have formed the basis for the proposed level of improved technology,
outlined in Annex 1, involving the use of dwarf sorghum varieties. Proposed
levels of fertilizer application and the specific types of sorghum most
suitable for the project area should be reviewed after 1973 and 1974 cropping
seasons in the light of results of ongoing and proposed field trials scheduled
for Daudawa and other sites. Acceptance for human consumption of new hybrid
varieties on the basis of palatability should be examined closely.
12.       Maize has the most exciting crop development potential in the
project. Recent research results in Nigeria, and experience in other African
countries having similar ecological conditions point to the probability of
a substantial expansion of area and production Jn the short to medium term.
Yields of over 7,000 kg/ha have been obtained under trial conditions over
several years at Samaru from synthetic varieties and hybrids. Trials in
the project area have yielded similarly, aid promising new materials is
presently being tested. Even .f a reduction of 50 percent from research
to farmer fields is assumed yield levels of at least 3,500 kg can be readily
obtained.
13.       These developments a-e comparatively recent in the northern States.
They have been initiated by a number of cooperative and regional programs,
partly funded by the US. Table 2 indicates yield potential of some of the
material being developed. Agronomic practices established elsewhere have



ANNEX 3
Page 4
been, and continue to be, tested under Nigerian conditions. Some refinements
are likely to be forthcoming, but basic production techniques utilizing com-
paratively heavy fertilizer applications have been proven in trials.
14.       Annex 1 sets out two levels of recommended maize technology based
on Nigerian research results and experience in other African countries having
similar ecological conditions. These differ from current recommended practices
since they presuppose a much closer degree of technical assistance to farmers
plus the provision of the inputs necessary to obtain predicted production
levels. As for sorghum, proposed technology should be reviewed in the light
of 1973 and 1974 field trial results and amended as necessary before incor-
poration in project activities.
15.       Cowpea is grown throughout the project area, and widely in Nigeria.
Research has centered mainly on control of insect pests, to which it is
highly susceptible both in the field and in store. However, some improved
varieties have been established and recommendations are available on cultural
practices. Responses to phosphatic fertilizer (single superphosphate) have
been demonstrated.
16.       Control of insect pests is possible, provided the crop is sole
planted using high volume sprays and DDT/BHC mixtures. The probability of
insect control using ULV techniques is currently under investigation at IAR.
Results suitable for dissemination to farmers are expected to be forthcoming
in the near future. In anticipation of this, an improved level of technology
is proposed for the project, that involves ULV spraying and fertilizer appli-
cation to sole crops planted as recommended by ERLS. Confirmation or possibly
modification of the treatment should be sought before their implementation,
following results of ongoing and planned investig&tions by IAR staff.
17.       The project management would undertake field trials in close
cooperation with IAR at the proposed Kumo and Tumu farms. Investigations
would form part of ongoing research programs in major commodities in the
project area. They would pay particular attention to the applicability of
results under project conditions.
Seed Multiplication
18.       Seed multiplication is the responsibility of State Ministries of
Agriculture and Natural Resources from parent nucleus material developed by
IAR. At the present time there is no private sector seed industry. With
the exception of the cotton seed multiplication scheme at Daudawa, Gusau and
Gombe, efforts to produce and distribute improved planting material are
generally inadequate to meet the needs of the northern States (for details
of stage multiplication of cotton see Table 1).
19.       Project proposals for a substantial uptake of improved planting
material of four of the five crops considered dictate that adequate multi-
plication facilities should be developed in the area. MNR already leases



ANNEX 3
Page 5
a 70 ha farm at Dadin Kowa at which cotton seed for the State, and other
areas is bulked up. The Dadin Kowa farm suffers in part from seasonal
flooding of the Gongola River. In order to ensure sufficient land for seed
multiplication the Government would either extend the Dadin Kowa to 200 ha
or open a new farm at Tumu. An additional farm of 400 ha would be developed
from the existing forest reserve, just south of Kumo ginnery. Development
details are at Schedule 1.
20.       Seed production is a specialized business calling for good manage-
ment standards and a high level of technical competence. Most MNR staff
at farm manager level are weak in those qualities, and it is questionable
whether existing MNR personnel could operate a seed production program of
the size proposed for the project. Ouantities of seed would not be large
initially, but would build up quite quickly as shown in Schedule 1.
21.       Parts of both sorghum and maize seed production programs would be
of the new hybrids; exact amounts would depend on farmer uptake which itself
depends on the successful demonstration of significant yield differences
between the new hybrids and current composite strains. Groundnut and cotton
seed production would pass through two multiplication stages on the project
seed farms; final multiplication would be on private seed farms. The 600 ha
of the two project seed farms (para 17) would be adequate to handle cotton and
groundnuts up to the third stage and the cereal production in the early years.
But the use of selected commercial farmers within the project area must be
envisaged for cereal quantities such as those projected from year 3 onwards.
Seed production developments under this project could be substantially expanded
to serve as a major seed producing location for the State.
22.       Schedule 1 of this Annex gives details on requirements, costs and
returns for Tumu (or Dadin Kowa) and Kumo seed farms. Both farms would be
operated by the Project.

Project Seed Requirements
1.        The project farms would produce the seed requirements of the project
farmers during the first two years of the project. Thereafter, any excess
requirement over and above production from project operated multiplication
farms would be produced by the larger "commercially" orientated farms within
the project area. Such farmers would be assisted by project staff. In the
unlikely event of a shortfall in seed it is likely that the surpluses gen-
erated hy the Funtua Agricultural Development Project would be available.
Due to the favorable conditions in Funtua considerable emphasis would be
placed there on seed production. The following summarises project seed
requirements:

2.        The project farmer involvement as indicated above takes into
account lower yields expected from the farms in comparison to project op-
erated seed farms at Kumo and Tumu. During the first year of the project,
Dadin Kowa farm would remain under project operation prior to the new farms
coming into operation in project year 2. Cereal seed production has been
estimated on the conservative basis that seed would be replaced annually.
This would not be the case for composite maize and sorghum seed. There is
a probability therefore of surpluses being available for distribution to
other farmers within the North Eastern State.
3.        Initially the seed farms would follow a cotton, groundnut, cereal
rotation, with between 15-20% of the land being out of crop at any one time.
The farm manager would be given considerable flexibility in choice of rotation
and cropping systems in the light of the lack of commercial farming data
available in Nigeria.

NIGERIA
NORTH EASTERN STATE
Gombe Agricultural Development Project
Agricultural Roads
General
1.        The lack of reasonable farm access constitutes a serious constraint
to development, the rectification of which is essential for the success of
the project. To enable most farmers to be within two kilometers of a reason-
able road requires development of 1,000 km of agricultural roads in the
Gbmbe area. These roads are needed so that vital agricultural extension
work can reach the farmer during the wet season when his crops are growing,
and for the delivery of farm inputs and evacuation of produce during the dry
season. In addition, social benefits by way of easier and more economical
haul of other supplies, better bus services, and easier travel by the farmer's
own modes of transport such as bicycles, donkey, ox cart and tractor, would
result.
2.        Traffic densities are difficult to predict with accuracy, but it
is certain that most of the heavy lorry traffic would occur in the dry season
when haul of farm inputs and produce is at its peak. Light traffic would
continue throughout the year mostly consisting of local movement between
villages. Because of this traffic pattern, where the heavier axle loads
occur in the dry months, it should be possible to use most of the roads
throughout the year, the main obstacle being those roads intersected by a
major river crossing which could cut off traffic for several months in the
wet season. Careful road location is necessary to reduce the frequency of
such obstacles.
3.        During the five year development period, the project would construct
1,000 km of agricultural roads, of which, about 750 km would be unsurfaced and
250 km would be surfaced. Roads would only be surfaced - with appropriate
thicknesses of natural occurring gravels - on main extraction routes to market
centers and where soil conditions make this essential (see map for proposed
road layout).
4.        Construction Standards. 1f  Roads would be constructed on the water-
sheds 2/ as such alignments are self draining and require minimal earthworks.
1/   Basic standards and data are calculated using English measurements.
2/   Lines of separation between drainage basins.



ANNEX 4
Page 2
Construction would involve 1,500-2,500 YC/mile over a 32 ft formation, and
would be undertaken most effectively by tractors for site clearance, and
motor graders for shaping the formation and providing turn-out drains from
longitudinal side drains; the use of motor graders would predominate.
Stripping (or topsoiling) over the formation width would seldom be required
on watersheds where weathering had taken place exposing material which is
capable, without surfacing, of adequately carrying the low volumes of traffic
likely to use such roads. Proposed typical road cross sections are attached
for (a) construction by grader and (b) low embankments that would be required
where low-lying areas cannot be avoided (see Figure 1 of Annex 4).
5.   Construction Technique
(i) Selection of road center line would be carefully carried out
by stereoscopic examination of aerial photography (provided for
under the project, Annex 10, Table 2) and large-scale maps, and
by site inspection and levelling to locate the tops of ridges,
which are often very flat. The alignments would slavishly
follow selected watersheds.
(ii) Clearing of the formation would be carried out by tractor/dozer/
scraper combinations and debris moved outside the formation
width.
(iii)  Topsoiling (or soil stripping) would only be carried out where
absolutely necessary, e.g., where topsoil or unsuitable material
exists and where the use of such soil in construction would
reduce road bearing strength.
(iv) Construction by grader. On watershed routes, the main method
of construction would be by motor grader. Material from
graderblade cut side drains, depth 12" - 18", would be moved
towards the center of the formation width and shaped to a
smooth camber. This is essentially a lateral earth movement
although some longitudinal regularization would occur along
the road center line as the grader shapes the formation.
Scarifying the material in the side drains may be necessary
and occasionally a tractor-mounted ripper might be needed to
loosen material for easier movement by the grader.
(v)  Provision of turn-out (offshoot drains) from the longitudinal
side drains of the formation would be grader constructed to
discharge water from the formation and so prevent erosion in
the side drains being caused by long uninterrupted drainage
flows. Care would be taken to ensure that turnout drains had
the correct slope to take water away from the formation.
(vi) Compaction of formation after shaping is desirable. The supply
of water for consumption by project staff and for road and dam
construction would be difficult. Rivers do not flow continually



ANNEX 4
Page 3
throughout one year, very few dams exist, shallow wells generally
yield low, and frequently fail in the dry season; and bore holes
(tube wells) have not proved successful in the basement complex
of the project area.
The initial stage of the construction program would be confined
for the first dry season of the project to areas within a work-
able range of existing water supplies, say, 20 miles. Thereafter
the dams constructed in the first stage, after filling during
the rains, would become the water source for wider reaching
road and dam construction activities.
(vii) Surfacing would only be carried out by tractor and scraper
over sections where the subgrade is found to have inadequate
bearing strength even for low traffic volumes, and where supplies
of surfacing material within the practical working haul of
tracked tractors. Scrapers would require the support of a pusher
during such operations. The principal function of the tractors
would be clearing, dozing and ripping and not surfacing (see
para 11). The self-loading scrapers proposed for the dam con-
struction unit would assist with surfacing in the wet season.
Local haulage contractors would be used where long hauls of
gravel are found necessary.
(viii) Minor embankments would be constructed by tractors and scrapers
on those sections where watershed construction is impossible.
Side borrow to fill would be used where practicable (see typical
cross-sections). In adopting predominantly watershed alignments,
embankments would be required in crossing from one watershed to
another and in doing so, at some sites, it would be possible to
construct dams and allow traffic to cross over a widened dam
wall.
(ix) Drainage structures. Corrugated metal pipe culverts would be
used because of the ease and speed with which these can be
transported, assembled and constructed. Bitumen-coating of
metal pipe would be an effective method of protecting culverts
from the slightly acid (pH 5-6) soil water to which they would
be exposed. Concrete headwalls, tailwalls and drop-pits using
standardized form-work would be constructed.
Method of Procurement
6.   Road Works - Three possibilities exist:
(i) By Contract. It is the opinion of Federal and State MOW
officials that major Nigerian (and foreign operations in
Nigeria) civil engineering contractors are fully (if not over)
committed with Federal and State highway development programs



ANNEX 4
Page 4
and that contracts for low-standard agricultural roads would
not interest them at the present time. The project program
is not of a type to interest new foreign contractors because
of their low unit cost of construction, long construction
period envisaged, and demand for a modest amount of equipment
and plant.
(ii) jIy State Ministries and Agencies. The State Ministries of
Works and Agriculture and Natural Resources would be unable
to undertake the construction of project roads and dams without
considerable strengthening in staff and equipment. In any
case MOW is already over committed and is extremely short of
adequately experienced supervisory and technical personnel.
(iii) By Project Construction Units. The establishment of these
units would involve recruitment of senior managerial, technical
and mechanical staff, procurement of plant, training of ope-
rators, setting up workshops, costing procedures and evaluation
of physical outputs. The recruitment of high calibre senior
personnel with experience in similar work is essential to the
success of project units. However, the main advantage of project
construction by force account would be lower cost of construction,
flexible use of equipment between roads and dams, and the training
of personnel to undertake similar work elsewhere in the State.
7.        Other integrated agricultural projects financed by the Bank have
In some instances involved the development of infrastructure through project
construction units. Construction has generally been efficient, at low cost,
and has provided an excellent training ground for young indigenous engineers.
In the light of such experiences, the inadequacy of existing Nigerian insti-
tutions, the lack of available contractors, and the need for flexibility,
the project would set up a special unit to undertake the construction of
roads, dams and soil conservation measures.
Construction Costs
8.        The average overall road construction cost is estimated at about
N 2,500 (US$3,800) per km. The cost includes engineering overheads, plant
capital and operating costs, workshop capital and operating costs, labor and
engineering supplies (see Tables 1-4).
Plant Requirement
9.        Plant requirements for agricultural road construction units have
been estimated by using plant output figures for similar work under similar
terrain and rainfall conditions and by estimating the total work quantities
required. Table 2 shows recommended major plant items. The predominant
method of construction would be by motor grader with one more powerful grader



ANNEX 4
Page 5
for work in harder soil conditions. Other major equipment includes two
tracked tractors with dozers and scrapers for clearing and forming shallow
embankments, and two vibratory rollers for compaction or formation and sur-
facing, where required.
Staff Requirements
10.       These have been related to the numbers and types of construction
equipment and to the tasks involved in alignment selection, grade construc-
tion, surfacing and drainage structures. A high degree of mobility is
desirable; caravans (dwelling trailers) would be provided for senior personnel
and portable accommodation for artisans, operators and laborers (Annex 6,
Table 5).
Road Maintenance
11.       Although some road maintenance would be undertaken by the project
during the development period, the allocation of construction plant for
maintenance would not be allowed to interfere with output of new road; thus
once a new road had been built maintenance responsibility would be handed
over to the respective Local Authority (LA). Funds have been included under
Annex 10, Table 6 for the purchase and operation of maintenance equipment,
and under the project respective LA staff would, during the project, be
seconded to the project engineering service for purposes of training and
operating control.
Bridges
12.       Small streams would be crossed by constructing "Irish" crossings -
a concrete slab on a suitable rock foundation - or through the use of ARMCO
type culverts. No large bridges would be constructed, since most rivers are
extremely wide and run dry for 8 months of the year, at a time when crops
are being evacuated, and inputs are being delivered to service center stores.
All the roads would have year round access from at least one end so that
during the wet season access can be gained. In the dry season road main-
tenance units would cut new approaches to the river beds, which would be
sufficient to allow ingress to traffic.
Compensation
13.       Compensation for loss of arable land due to road construction would
be made at established LA rates and the amounts (provided for under Annex 10
Table 6) would be determined by LA.

ANNEX 5
Page 1
NIGERIA
NORTH EASTERN STATE
GOMBE AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Water and Soil Conservation
1.        The project area suffers from extreme shortage of water for domestic
purposes during the dry season and part of the wet season until village wells
start to yield properlv. The area is also prone to soil erosion principally
in the catchment areas of small streams and rivers. Under the ecological and
climatic conditions prevailing in the project area, soil and water conservation
are synonymous with each other, and where possible would be treated together,
particularly as proposed under the project, water development would be through
the construction of surface water dams.
A. Water Conservation and Rural Water Supplies
2.        Shortage of water supplies for domestic use during the dry season
and beginning of the wet season, January through August, is acute in the
project area. It is estimated that the existing wells, dams and tapkis (ponds)
can serve only 10-20X of total demand. Villagers depend for their supplies on
water from wells that on average can only be dug to 10-20 m, most of which dry
up during the dry season. An alternative source of water are tapkis and small
earth dams, but these are infrequent, and at times poorly sited and constructed,
resulting in poor holding capacity and at times breakages. The final alter-
native is stream bed wells. These are seasonally hand dug in water bearing
sands of some, but not all, rivers and streams. In many instances these are
last resort supplies resulting in carry distances of up to 5 miles. Water
collected from such sources is either head or donkey carried, and during the
crisis period (from February to June) is sold at a standard rate of NO.10
(US$0.15) per 20 liters. The lack of water is a serious social and technical
constraint, and unless efforts are made to conserve and improve water supplies
the project might only partially succeed.
3.        Existing plans by the State Government to improve water supplies in
rural areas has been given high budgetary priority. Plans are being prepared
to improve water supplied to larger towns, but for rural villages efforts
rarely go beyond providing assistance for well improvements. The latter is
not the correct policy since the ground water available in the shallow aquifer
is limited, and new wells themselves dry up and accelerate the drying up of
existing wells. In short it would seem that the draw off of ground water is
greater than its reservoir supply.



ANNEX 5
Page 2
4.        The possibilities of utilizing ground water from deep aquifers
have been reviewed. Unfortunately geological conditions in the area are
such that the basement complex is of shallow formation and precludes the
possibilities of deep aquifers. Furthermore even if utilization were
possible some form of mechanical pumping would be necessary raising
problems of maintenance. Experience shows that most of the simple pumps
installed in the past have been broken within a year and subsequently
have been rarely repaired.
5.        For the reasons outlined above hleavy reliance on dugwells and/or
deep boreholes are impracticable.  Fortunately it is possible to exploit
surface water supplies and to impound wet season (June-September) run off
in small earth damns. The topography and stream pattern for the project
area indicate a protiising potential for earth dam construction.  This is
further substantiated by the 1:50,000 topographical maps which are available
(with 50 and 25 foot contour intervals) and which indicate a large number
of potential locations for small dani sites.
6.        liydrological data are generally poor, but enough for small dam
development purposes. Annual rainfall in the project area varies from
700 mm-1,200 mm, and a runoff yield varying from 0.080-0.150 m3/km2 can
be expected. Evaporation from the natural water surface is very high
being about 2,000 mm per year.
7.        A careful analysis of the topography, availability of local
construction materials and hydrology indicates that a network of small dams
of about 120,000 m3 in total capacity each, should be built.  Each would
be fed by runoff from a catchment of 3 to 4 km2 in area and would be
capable of meeting the year-round demands of 2,000 people and their live-
stock. The dams would be equipped to provide a gravitational distribution
system for conveying water from the reservoir outlet to delivery points at
separate locations for people and livestock.
8.        A typical earthdam for the project area would have specifications
as detailed below:
(i)  Features of average dam
-    Total maximum height                           6.70 m
-    Free board                                     1.50 m
-    Depth at spillway level                        5.2  m3
-    Total capacity                             120,000  m3
-    Dead storage or sediment space              20,000  mn3
-    Useful storage capacity                    100,(00  m3
-    The spillway overflow with concrete sills with rip-rap in
between (it may be necessary to peg down the rip-rap with
gabion wire in special cases) would be designed by the Soil
Conservation Engineer after he had surveyed the catchment,
studied its characteristics, rainfall intensity and worked
out the run-off.



ANNEX 5
Page 3
Dam - earth-fill with clay core if necessary, having 4 m
top width and 1:2 to 1:3 side slopes with rip-rap on up-
stream face, containing about 7,000 m3 of fill.
Foundation - treated to impervious zone.  Core wall would be
installed to an impervious foundation.
-    Outlet work - outlet pipes of 7.5 cm in diameter laid in
concrete, with at least three concrete flanges, equipped
with a gate valve on the downstream.
-    Dam design - as portions of the project area are under soils
which contain a high percentage of clay and others under
deep soils containing high sand content, the design of each
dam would be based on the results of soil tests, the proposed
borrow and foundation materials.
(ii) Distribution System
-    Main gravity pipeline - iron pipe of 7.5 cm in diameter
laid at 1:1.000 slope for a distance to clear valley bottom,
not exceeding 300 m.
-    Branch pipeline - iron of 5 cm in diameter conveying water
from the main pipe to the separate delivery points at a
distance of at least 50 m for villagers and 100 m for
livestock from the mlain pipe.
-    Villager delivery points - installed with an adequate
concrete platform and faucets. 1/
-    2 livestock delivery points - installed with an adequate
brick trough and faucets.
(iii)  Useful life of Tank:       20 years 2/
Design sediment space in tank: 1,200 x 20 x 0.85 = 20,000 m3
Total tank capacity:
-    Active or useful storage                   100,000 m3
-    Space for sediment deposit                  20,000 m3
-    Total capacity                             120,000 m3
1/   The possibility of using hydrograms, for pumping water from the larger
dams to nearby villages should be considered as to cost and applicability.
2/   For this purpose, the first 20 years after completion of the tank are
considered as 'Productive life of the reservoir' and years beyond this
are considered as 'falling' or 'depleting' life.



ANNEX 5
Page 4
9.        In the very sandy areas where dams are impractical subsurface dams
would be constructed. This would involve selecting a site with rocks occurring
on both sides of the river, and digging a trench down to the impervious layer,
a reinforced concrete wall would be built (to a height to be decided upon by
the Conservation Engineer). The area behind the wall would silt up and the
water would be retained in the sand, resulting in minimum evaporation. Water
would be obtained through the traditional system of digging shallow wells on
the water bearing sands. The cost would be less than that of a dam.
10.       The project could not in the first instance provide sufficient
dams to satisfy all project farmers. However, it would attempt to satisfy
demand in the more critical areas, and provide reserve capacity for most
village unit areas. 35 dams would be constructed over the 5 years of the
project; 5 would be built in year 1, followed by 20 in each subsequent year.
Thus on average there would be one dam to 60 km which approximates to one
per 700 farming families.
B.  Soil Conservation Measures
11.       Soil erosion occurs through overstocking and concentration of stock
at sparsely located watering points, and through poor cultivation methods in-
cluding badly aligned planting ridges and late planted crops at low plant
densities.
12.       Virtually no soil conservation measures have been undertaken on a
large scale in northern Nigeria and there are no practical specifications
based on past experience that can be followed. Consequently it would remain
for the project conservation engineer to modify the designs and layouts as
experience is gained during project implementation.
13.       Dam catchments would require special conservation measures to prevent
erosion and siltation. Conservation on other areas would be of a less inten-
sive nature but designed to provide a basis for any future more intensive con-
servation measures that may be required. To avoid concentration of livestock
at the limited number of existing watering places and planned project dams a
number of tapkis (ponds) would be constructed.
14.       Dam Catchment Conservation Measures
(i) Master ridges would be marked with a plow on the contour
so that all cultivation would follow the contour, 3 lines
per 20 ha should be sufficient.
(ii) The Conservation Engineer would locate natural or artificial
water ways approximately 1,000 m apart. He would shape them
to the required size and if necessary have them grassed. In
the second year when the grass had established, 'vee' ditches
or cut-off drains would be constructed.



ANNEX 5
Page 5
Specifications. The diversion ditches (cut-off drains) would be
approximately 1,000 m long draining both ways into the grassed waterway.
The ditches would have a cross sectional area of 0.8 m2 (37 cm deep and
3.6 m wide) and have a grade of 1:400 and be 250 m apart.
Equipment Required. 1 x 125 hp motor grader, 1 x 75 hp tractor
and plow.
15.       Conservation of Other Areas.  A conservation unit would be attached
to the road unit, and as the roads progressed master ridges would be construct-
ed with a plow over an area extending approximately 2 km each side of the road.
3 master ridges per 20 ha would be sufficient. At the same time lines for
future access roads to villages or group of farms would be marked, the natural
drainage ways would also be demarcated so that they would be left out of cul-
tivation and allowed to revert to grass. In addition farmers should be en-
couraged to practice tie ridges (boxed ridges). The ties would be 1 to 2 m
apart and half the height of ridges. This would give maximum absoption in the
early light rains. In the heavy rains the ties would break and allow the ex-
cess water to drain off. Ties would be replaced towards the end of the rains
to assist in improving the infiltration of the late rains.
16.       Tapkis.  These would be constructed by a 140 hp angle dozer in
shallow depressions with a catchment area of 15 to 20 ha. Should the site
chosen have insufficient catchment, the catchment would be increased by the
use of diversion ditches.
Method. The dozer would scoop out the earth over an area approx-
imately 40 m x 30 m with an average depth of 1.7 m. The embankment would be
made around the lower end giving a small increase to the holding capacity of
the tapki.  The diversion ditches would be constructed by the motor grader
on a 1:500 grade with a capacity of 0.8 m2 (37 cm deep and 3.6 m wide).
These would stop short of the tapki and be allowed to discharge towards a
bank 1 m high, 1 m thick and of sufficient length to allow the water to
circulate around the bank into the tapkis. This would reduce the velocity
and cause the silt to be deposited outside the tapki.
Equipment. No additional equipment would be required for tapki
construction as construction by the conservation unit would be done during
the crop growing season. About 160 tapkis would be constructed during the
life of the project.

NIGERIA
NORTH EASTERN STATE
GOMtBE AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Organization, Staff Requirements and Training
A.   Organization
1.        The project organization as described in Chapter VI of the main
report would assume responsibility for all existing functions of NNR within
the project area except for those aspects concerning animal health, fisheries,
forestry, and irrigation. The project organization would follow the pattern
as shown at Schedule 1 of this annex and would comprise a project executive
committee, primarily controlling policy, budgets and audits, and operational
project management unit responsible for the day-to-day operations involved in
implementation of project policies. The project executive committee and man-
agement unit would be established by a Govern-gazetted order, a proposed draft
which is at Schedule II.
2.        The Gombe Project Management Unit (GOPMU) would operate through a
number of sections; the head of each would be responsible to the Project
Manager. The sections would be: Administration, Finance, Technical Services,
Credit Services, Market Services, and Engineering Services.
3.        The Administration Section would be responsible for personnel
administration, maintenance of records, organization of project transport,
office administration, project security and public relations.
4.        The Finance Section would be responsible for budgeting, budget
control, internal audit, procurement, stores, salary payments, accounting
procedures, cost accounting, etc. It would help in setting up financial
procedures for the revolving credit fund, and would conduct the internal
audit of the fund.
5.        The Technical Services Section would provide basic technical support
and services to all those project actions designed to increase farmer produc-
tivity including:
(a) Development Services would provide the coordinating link between
marketing, credit and extension. The project would have 5
development centers each serving a development area and con-
trolled by a Development Officer who would coordinate all project
activities within the area. Apart from coordination, the Devel-
opment Officer would be directly responsible for extension



ANNEX 6
Page 2
services. The latter would be organized on the basis of one
Agricultural Assistant (AA) for each farm service center. 1/
In turn each AA would supervise a number of Agricultural
Instructors (AI) who would work at the hamlet level. The
AA and AI would provide basic extension advice to farmers
and groups of farmers. Associated with the extension
service would be a small livestock advisory unit that would
(i) identify livestock problems and make recommendations
for livestock programs within the area; and (ii) provide
advice on practical animal husbandry to those farmers engaged
in livestock operations sponsored by the project. In this
connection close liaison would be maintained with NNR
veterinary services.
(b) Seed Farms. Two seed farms at Kumo and Tumu would be devel-
oped to provide part of project seed requirements in conjunction
with contract operations by some of the larger farmers in nearby
areas. The seed farms would also be used for practical demon-
stration purposes, applied research and training. Seed farm
managers would also provide management advice to larger
"icommercial" farmers within the project area. These farmers
would be directed to NAB and/or commercial banks for farm
credit, and GOPMU would assist them in this.
(c) Tractor Hire Services, now supplied by MNR, would be con-
tinued under the project but would be phased out gradually in
favor of private contractors sponsored by the project.
(d) Training would be undertaken at the Tumu training center,
the capacity of which would be expanded from 20 to 40
students, and at a new center to be constructed at Kumo.
The two centers would be used initially for training and
retraining project staff in extension, credit, marketing,
and other disciplines. As the project develops, more of
the available training capacity would be allocated to
project area farmers.
6.        The Credit Services Section would be developed in parallel to the
development services and would be structured around the Development Centers
and at the village service centers at farmer level. The service would provide
farmers with inputs on credit or for cash, assist in recovery of repayments,
1/   Some of the villages in the project area comprise up to 8,000 farm
families, and cover a very wide area. Thus under the project, allow-
ance has been made for the location of service centres to be made on
an area basis of about one centre to 100 km2. Thus some village would
be served by more than 1 centre.



ANNEX 6
Page 3
and keep accounts. Credit service personnel would be project employees,
but in matters concerning the making of loans and recovery of credit. An
area Credit Officer would control credit activities within each development
area, and each village service center would have two Credit Assistants (Annex
9 gives details).
7.        The Market Services Section would be responsible for product inspec-
tion at produce markets, for market liaison and information, and for the control
and monitoring of Licensed Buying Agents (LBA). It would also be responsible
for the development of group marketing systems, and where necessary for linking
producers directly with distant consumers, e.g. contracts for maize supply to
southern feed mill operators. Each Development Center would have a marketing
supervisor assisted by two market assistants. A produce examiner would be
posted to each farm service center to service its market. Marketing services
would be responsible for the logistics involved in handling and distributing
farm inputs. In this respect the produce examiners would act as storekeepers
during the period when markets are closed, and when the main handling and
distribution of inputs would occur.
8.        The Engineering Services Section would be primarily responsible
for implementation of the physical infrastructural program for the project.
It would determine design and make specifications for buildings, houses,
offices, etc., prepare tender documents, evaluate bids, and supervise
contractors. It would be responsible for the procurement of all plant,
vehicles and equipment, and for their operation and maintenance. It would
prepare designs for roads and associated structures, dams, and soil conser-
vation works, anu would ensure their construction according to plan. It
would operate a project workshop. Finally, it would have a special land
use section to provide support for other project units as well as to provide
information directly relevant to civil works construction. In all instances,
it would work closely with Ministry of Works engineers.
B.   Staff Requirements
9.        Details of the number of staff needed for the project, their
availability within the State and probable shortfalls are shown at Tables
1-5 of this Annex. Job descriptions and qualifications for the more senior
positions are at Schedule 3. In general existing staff in the State ha-7e
received inadequate training and experience to permit them to effectively
manage and operate the project and, therefore, internationally recruited
staff would be necessary for some of the key posts. Existing staff in the
project area would come under the project and additional staff would be
transferred to the project from other areas of the State. Priority during
the first two years would be given to the project for those Agricultural
Assistants completing ongoing training courses. On this basis sufficient
professional and technical staff would be available. Junior technical staff,
particularly agricultural instructors (AI), would be recruited and trained
by the project at its own training centers. Such training would initially



ANNEX 6
Page 4
comprise of simple short courses on specific crops and associated problems.
The AI would receive further training after a period of field experience
and, thereafter, regular refresher courses. Inexperienced credit and market-
ing staff would be trained in a similar manner through a series of inservice
training courses.
10.       The Project Executive Committee on the advice of the Project Manager
would be responsible for the appointment of all senior staff. Staff appoint-
ments at lower levels would be the responsibility of the Manager in consulta-
tion with relevant Government departments from which particular transfers or
secondments might be made. Staff salaries are compatible with existing Gov-
ernment scales, and would from time to time be reviewed by the Committee.
C.   Staff Training
11.       Professional training is provided at universities throughout
Nigeria.  The Ahmadu Bello University at Zaria in the North Central State
caters especially for the six northern States, although northern students
attend both Federal and State financed universities elsewhere in Nigeria,
and abroad. A degree in agriculture is given by ABU and the project would
expect to recruit some of its professional staff as they graduate from
ABU.
12.       Diplomate (3 year course for holders of West African School
Certificate) and certificate (2 year) level training is the responsibility
of the Division of Agriculture and Livestock Service Training (DALST) of
ABU. Graduates of these courses are employed by the Ministries of Agriculture
and Natural Resources of the northern States.
13.       Four schools are operated by DALST:
(a) Samaru (North Central State)
Offering, at present, certificate courses in extension,
farm mechanics, home economics and a diploma course in
home economics. Expansion of this school, planned for
completion by 1975, would add diploma courses in farm
mechanics and extension to the syllabus.
(b) Bakura (North Western State)
Offering a certificate course in irrigation agronomy;
presently located at Samaru pending completion of the
new school at Bakura.
(c) Kaduna Livestock Service Training Center (North
Central State)
Offering certificate courses in livestock and range
management, and diploma courses in animal products
inspection, and range management.



ANNEX 6
Page 5
(d) Kabba (Kwara State)
Offering diploma courses in agriculture at present,
and certificate courses in agriculture from 1974.
14.       Present enrollment at these schools is as follows:
lst Year      2nd Year

/1   The capacity of these schools is being increased and it is forecast
that by 1975 Samaru would have a capacity for 600 students and the
other schools 300 students each.
/2   1st year general course with opportunity to specialize in the second
year.
/3   Special course to give existing Ministry technical staff the opportunity
to upgrade to AS (1972 only).
15.       The output of diplomates (Agricultural Superintendents) and certi-
ficate holders (Agricultural Assistants) bonded to work through the terms
of their scholarships for North Eastern State is forecast as follows:
1974 - 18; 1975 - 32; and 1976 - 36. These, together with intra State
transfers, would meet project requirements (see Table 2).
16.       The existing training of agricultural extension staff, graduates,
diplomates and certificate holders is sadly lacking in practical farm



ANNEX 6
Page 6
management, and the ability of senior staff in the field to give advice to
farmers directed at increasing profit margins needs to be greatly improved.
At present extension staff tend to give blanket advice without considering
economic implications. Training establishments, for both senior and junior
staff should improve the quality of their farm management training.
17.       Farm training centers are operated by North Eastern State and
graduate Agricultural Instructors (AI), the lowest grade of extension worker.
The State also has farm institutes for training farmers and farmers' sons.
Under the project, it is proposed to upgrade the existing Tumu Farm Institute
to a Farm Training Center, and to build a new Farm Training Center at Kumo.
These centers would be used to train, in the initial years of the project,
project staff particularly AI and Credit Assistants. Later on the emphasis
would be more on farmer training.
18.       The recruitment of individuals for training as agricultural
instructors, Credit Assistants, etc., should not cause too many problems
since the basic primary level of education is relatively good throughout
northern Nigeria, and there is an increasing surplus of West African School
Certificate holders on the labor market.
19.       Staff recruited by the project would be expected to have certain
minimum qualifications: Agricultural Officers - degree in agriculture;
Agricultural Superintendents - diploma or upgraded from lower level;
Agricultural Assistant - certificate; Agricultural Instructor - primary
seven; Credit Officer - degree or diploma, or certificate in accountancy
preferably with some commercial bank experience; Credit Assistants - West
African School Certificate; Market Supervisor - degree holder or relevant
experience in market organization; Marketing Assistant - West African
School Certificate; Produce Inspector - primary seven; Rural Engineers -
engineering degree.
20.       The terms and conditions of the employment of project personnel would
conform with those established for all employees of the State Government except
that professional and technical grade staff would be provided housing as an
incentive to live and work in remote areas, and that the Board would establish
contract terms for both expatriate and Nigerian contract staff. For expatriate
staff, contract terms would take into account the international market for such
staff and the benefits that normally accrue to such appointments. For Nigerian
staff contract terms would provide similar benefits as normal pensionable Gov-
ernment staff but would also provide for a gratuity (to be determined by GOPEC)
in lieu of a pension.
21.       Management Training.  In addition to technical staff training,
project management would play an important role in training staff to fill
managerial positions. In this respect internationally recruited staff would,
as one of their responsibilities, ensure the adequate training of their



ANNEX 6
Page 7
subordinates, and would be required to submit to GOPEC through the project
manager, quarterly reports on the training aspects of their section. The
project manager would have the overall responsibility for ensuring an adequate
management training program is maintained and would report regularly on its
progress. There are sufficient funds available for the recruitment of a
training specialist if the project manager so requires.

NIGERIA
NORTH EAST STATE
GOMBE AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPlENT PROJECT
Establishment of Project Management Unit
1.        As a condition of loan effectiveness the North East State Executive
Council would be required to publish an "order" in the State Government Gazette
establishing the Gombe Project Management Unit (GOPMU) and the Gombe Project
Executive Committee (GOPEC). The order would set out the objectives and re-
sponsibilities of GOPMU and GOPEC, and would include the following:
2.        The GOPMU would be managed by a Project Manager and its objectives
should be:
(a) development of the project area with a view to increasing
production of agriculture and raising the living standards
of the rural population;
(b) construction of roads, water supplies and soil conservation
works associated with agricultural development;
(c) development and management of seed multiplication farms;
(d) training of project staff and farmers;
(e) provision of village service centers and stores;
(f) provision of extension, credit and marketing services;
(g) procurement of farm inputs; and
(h) development of supporting infrastructure such as houses,
buildings, offices etc. for the above services.
3.        The GOPMU would be controlled by the Gombe Project Executive
Committee (GOPEC) which would be made up as follows:



ANNEX 6
Schedule 2
Page 2
(a)  Permanent Secretary  -  Ministry of Natural Resources
(b)  Representative        -  Ministry of Finance
(c)  Representative        -  Ministry of Local Government
(d)  Representative        -  Ministry of Transport and Works
(e)  Representative        -  Ministry of Economic Planning
(f)  Representative        -  NES Water Board
(g)  Director of Federal Department of Agriculture or representative
(h)  Chief Agricultural Officer, for the North East State
(i)  Representative of the Nigerian Agricultural Bank
(j)  Two members from the project area to be appointed by the
Military Governor
The Project Manager would be the Secretary of the GOPEC.
4.        GOPEC would be responsible to the Commissioner of Natural Resources
and would have the following responsibilities:
(a)  Policy.   GOPEC would deterrine agricultural and development
policy, and would consult with the Federal Government and the
World Bank when any major changes in policy may be required;
(b)  Finance.
(i)  GOPEC would approve the annual budget and would ensure
that sufficient Government funds are readily available
to the project;
(ii)  GOPEC would appoint independent auditors;
(iii) GOPEC would submit audited statement of accounts to th_!
Commissioner of Agriculture within four months of the
closure of each financial year;
(iv) GOPEC would approve the opening of project bank accounts-
and
(v)  the Chairm.an of GOPEC would approve the monthly cash klo^
statements submitted by the manager of GOPMU;
(vi)   approve annually the terms of lending to project fni-'
on the basis of recommendations made by GOPMUT;
(c)  Staff and Personnel Matters.  In consultation with the Man '.-
of GOPMU, GOPEC would be responsible for the appointment of a:.0.
senior project staff and employees, their terms and conditioss
of employment and annual review of salaries and allowances;



ANNEX 6
Schedule 2
Page 3
(d) Award of Contracts. GOPEC would on the advice of the Manager
of GOPI4U be responsible for approval of any single contract
valued at more than N 30,000;
(e) Inter-Departmental Coordination. The GOPEC through its members
would be responsible for the coordination at Ministry level
for those issues that may effect the proper development of
the project.
Meetings of GOPEC
5.   (a)  The Chairman would preside over all the meetings of the GOPEC
which he attends, and in his absence the present members would
appoint one of their number to preside over such a meeting.
(b) The Chairman of the GOPEC would invite the GOPEC to meet at such
time and place as he may think suitable; provided that no more
than three months would elapse between any meeting and the
next. In the absence of the Chairman, the Manager would call
the GOPEC to meet whenever he deems this necessary.
(c) Six members of the GOPEC would constitute a quorum of any meeting
of the GOPEC. The GOPEC would pass its decisions by the Majority
of the votes of its members present and voting, and in case of
equality of votes the Chairman would have a casting vote.
(d) The GOPEC may call any person to attend any of its meetings
without that person having a counted vote.
6.        GOPMU would be resronsible to GOPEC for the day to day operations of
the project. In particular it would be responsible for the carrying out of
the objectives as stated in para 2 above. GOPMU would be managed by a Project
Manager who would report directly to the Chairman of GOPEC. The main duties
of GOPMU would be:
(a) the implementation of an agricultural development program
within the project area following the policy guidelines of
the GOPEC;
(b) the control and operation of the project revolving credit fund;
(c) the preparation of annual budgets and cash flows for approval
by GOPEC. In particular GOPMU would submit to the Chairman of
the GOPEC monthly cash flow statements indicating any changes
in requirements for State Government ftnds due to changes in
project expenditures and inflow anticipated from the Federal
Government. Unless informed to the contrary within 5 days of
dispatch of such statements to the Chairman of the GOPEC, the
manager would be authorized to make the proposed expenditures.



ANNEX 6
Schedule 2
Page 4
(d) the recruitment of project staff, in consultation, when
necessary with other Government Departments, and for senior
staff, with approval of GOPEC;
(e) the advertising, negotiating, calling for tenders, and after
approval from GOPEC, for concluding such contracts with persons
and organizations either inside or outside the State for the
erection of buildings, the construction of civil works and
the supply of goods, services and materials as may seem to be
requisite for the proper discharge of its duties;
(f) the operation of project bank accounts;
(g) the preparation of reimbursement claims against project
expenditure, for submission to the Federal Government and
the World Bank.
7.        GOPMU would administer a field evaluation unit that would be tech-
nically directed by a Federal Government controlled Project Monitoring and
Evaluation Unit (PMEU).
X.        GOPMU would prepare quarterly and annual reports for tne simultaneous
submission to GOPEC, North East State Executive Council, Federal Government
of Nigeria and the World Bank.
9.        GOPMU would be delegated any additional duties and powers by GOPEC
to ensure the attainment of the objectives as set out in this order.

Schedule 3
Page 1
NIGERIA
NORTH EASTERN STATE
GOMBE AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Job Descriptions and Qualifications of Senior Staff
PROJECT MANAGER
Experience would be the main criteria for the selection of a
Project Manager, although a formal qualification in agriculture would be
important.
At least 10 years' experience in successful agricultural develop-
ment work in the field, including development-orientated projects, preferably
in Africa, would be required. A high degree of administrative ability, an
experience in dealing with the highest level of Government, especially in
committee work, and previous involvement in extension in small farmer commu-
nities would all be necessary.
The responsibilities of the Project Manager would be the overall
and complete control of all project activities and the coordination of all
project activities with those of other Federal and State Government agencies
and local authorities.
His duties would include:
(a) control of all project funds with the assistance of the
Chief accountant;
(b) establishment of a cost analysis and record keeping system
which would enable project progress and achievement to be
monitored and a meaningful evaluation of the project's
impact possible;
(c) responsibility for project personnel matters with the
assistance of the Chief Administrative Officer;
(d) coordination of all project activities;
(e) reporting project Drogress to Government through written
reports and through the various project committees; and
(f) training project staff and ensuring a proper management
training program is successfully implemented.



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 2
The Project Manager would be responsible to the Chairman of the
Gombe Project Executive Committee (the Chairman being the Permanent Secretary
of the Ministry of Natural Resources).
CHIEF ADMINISTRATIVE OFFICER
The Chief Administrative Officer would be a very senior civil
servant; or have had equivalent experience in a major public corporation or
private company.
In previous employment he would have undertaken senior administra-
tive responsibility, and have gained a thorough understanding of government
procedures.
His duties would include:
(a) responsibility for all project personnel;
(b) project transportation;
(c) maintenance of a project registry;
(d) the physical control and checking of project stores;
(e) the general project security;
(f) the public relations aspects of the project; and
(g) training administration staff.
He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager.
CHIEF ACCOUNTANT
The Chief Accountant would be a professionally qualified accountant
with additional training in business management. He should have had at least
10 years' experience in a senior position in a large public corporation or
commercial company. If possible, he would have a knowledge of banking and
credit procedures. Previous employment involving working with high ranking
government officials would be an advantage.
His main responsibility would be the control of all project
finances on behalf of the Project Manager. His duties would include:
(a) preparation of budgets, cash flows and estimates of expenditure
for the project;
(b) ensuring correct application for Droject funds;



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 3
(c) assisting the Credit Manager in establishing an efficient
and meaningful system of recording agricultural credit
transactions;
(d) the responsibilities for project stores and requisites,
with the assistance of the section and department heads,
and for stores control and audit procedures;
(e) internal auditing of project accounts; and
(f) training accounting staff.
CHIEF TECHNICAL OFFICER
The Chief Technical Officer would be a qualified Agriculturalist.
He would have a thorough knowledge of tropical crop production and related
problems.
He would have had at least 5 years experience in extension work
with small farmers where cotton, groundnuts and cereals were part of the
cropping system.
He would be responsible for all technical aspects of the project
and his duties specifically would include:
(a) organizing research, and seed multiplication and distribution,
with the assistance of the Seeds Officer and Farm Managers;
(b) supervision of the training program for all extension staff;
(c) organizing and ensuring the effectiveness of extension
services in the project area;
(d) supervising the Livestock Advisor, the Farm Machinery
Section and the Farm Management Advisor; and
(e) liaising with and assisting the Market Section to ensure
it meets the project's requirements in marketing.
He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager, and would
assist the Project Manager in his dealings with other government and local
authority agencies in matters related to the Technical Services.
CHIEF ENGINEER
The Chief Engineer should be a qualified civil engineer. Some
training in mechanical engineering would be an added advantage.



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 4
He would have had at least 5 years' experience in the problems of
rural engineering in developing countries. A sound knowledge and experience
in road, water, and soil conservation engineering would be considered essen-
tial. He would have had senior administrative responsibility in the engin-
eering field and would be accustomed to working with government agencies.
His duties would include:
(a) control of all project engineering activities;
(b) budgetary control;
(c) the preparation of all tenders, contracts, and quantity
surveys;
(d) supervision of land use planning, and the design of project
roads, buildings and dams; and
(e) for the efficient coordination of the different units of the
Project engineering services, and close liaison with those
other Government departments, such as the Ministry of Works and
the Ministry of Local Government who may be associated with the
project.
He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager.
CREDIT MANAGER
The Credit Manager would be a professionally qualified accountant
with a demonstrable sound working knowledge of the establishment and corpo-
ration of rural credit institutions. He would have at least 5 years of
experience in responsible positions in smallholder rural credit administra-
tion, and have a record of sound administrative ability. Experience in the
formation of farmerst credit groups or societies and working with these
organizations, and/or cooperatives, would be an advantage.
Hlis prime responsibility would be the control of project farmer
credit operation; which would involve initially the disbursement of loans
in kind and their recovery.
llis responsibilities would include:
(a) formation of a Revolving Credit Fund as outlined in the
project appraisal report;
(b) establishing an efficient accounting and recording system
for farmers' loans;
(c) requisitioning of funds and other required inputs for
timely disbursement and supply to farmers;



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 5
(d) liaison with the Evaluation Unit to provide data and
information needed in project evaluation; and
(e) training of credit staff.
The Credit Manager would be responsible to the Project Manager.
CHIEF MARKETING OFFICER
Senior management responsibility in a large marketing organization
would be the principal qualification of the Chief Marketing Officer.
He would have experience in the production, primary marketing and
selling of tropical crops, particularly cotton, groundnuts and maize.
He would be responsible for the project's Marketing Services, and
his duties would include:
(a) monitoring and controlling the operations of all licensed
buying agents operating in the project area:
(b) establishing a market information service;
(c) liaison with the statutory marketing boards and other
organizations private and public to ensure the effective
marketing and smooth flow of produce from the project
area; and
(d) coordinating with the Credit Manager to establish a system
of credit collection at time of crop marketing.
He would be directly responsible to the Project Manager.
SENIOR FARM MANAGER
The Senior Farm Manager would have some formal training in Agri-
culture, but more importantly would have had at least 10 years' successful
experience in managing large commercially oriented farms in the tropics.
A specific experience of cotton, groundnuts, sorghum and maize production,
or at least two of these crops would be required. Previous experience in
growing these and other crops for seed production would be helpful. A sound
knowledge of farm recording and accounting procedures would be essential.
The Senior Farm Manager would manage one of the project farms and
would, through another manager, be respons_ble for the second project farm.
In total he would be responsible for the eEficient operation of units growing
cotton, groundnuts and cereals for seed.



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 6
His duties would include:
(a) budget preparation and cash flow control for the farm;
(b)  preparation and implementation of a cropping plan for
the farm;
(c) establishment, maintenance and operating of a suitable
seed dressing and cleaning unit for the farm;
(d) procuring, maintaining, and operating appropriate farm
machinery;
(e) control of farm labor;
(f) assisting the Farm Management Advisor in providing advice
and assisting the large privately owned farm units of the
project area, especially those growing seed on contract;
(g) liaison with the Seeds Officer on technical seed production
matters; and
(h) training junior staff in practical large scale farming.
He would be responsible to the Chief Technical Officer.
SEEDS OFFICER
The Seeds Officer would have had formal training in Agriculture or
Agricultural Botany. He would have had specific experience in commercial
large scale seed production, preferably with at least two of the following:
cotton, groundnuts, maize, and sorghum. He would advise the Managers of the
Seed Multiplication Farms on seed production and would be responsible for
ensuring the distribution of improved seeds to farmers in the project area.
His duties would include:
(a) advising and assisting the Farm Managers in procuring and
operating suitable seed cleaning and dressing units;
(b) sponsoring progressive farmers in seed production and
thereafter for advising them in seed production techniques;
(c) the building up of a viable seed industry in the project
area; and
(d) the training of technical and professional project
personnel in seed production and distribution.



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 7
He would be responsible to the Chief Technical Officer.
LIVESTOCK OFFICER
The Livestock Officer would have formal training in Veterinary
Science or Animal Husbandry and would have had experience in Animal Husbandry
work in tropical countries.
He would be responsible for the preparation of a long term plan
for livestock development within the project area. This would be based on
an analysis of the potential for on-farm systems of livestock and associated
feed production, and inter alia, would be aimed at the evolvement of systems
to make better use of communal grazing lands.
LAND USE PLANNING OFFICER
The Land Use Planning Officer would have had formal training in
agricultural, soil science, or land resource use; land surveying and photo-
grammetry. Previous experience in Land Resource work and associated land
and agricultural development planning would be essential.
Duties of the Land Use Planning Officer would be to:
(a) prepare specifications for the production of aerial photographs
and uncontrolled mosaics of the project area;
(b)  undertake a complete photo analysis of the project area and
prepare land use resource maps and inventory of the area;
(c)  plan, in conjunction with the Chief Technical Officer,
the location of roads and dams to be constructed under
the project;
(d) prepare all drawings, etc. of dam, soil conservation and
road designs that may be submitted by the engineering
sections of the project;
(e) undertake any such other mapping and surveying considered
necessary by the Chief Engineer:
(f) undertake photoanalytical work that may he required by the
evaluation unit or Chief Technical Officer;
(g) give general guidance on Land Resource use to Engineering
and Technical Services staff; and
(h)  train Nigeria staff in land use and associated techniques.
He would be directly responsible to the Chief Engineer.



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 8
SOIL AND W4ATER CONSERVATION ENGINEER
The Soil and Water Conservation Engineer would have had formal
training in engineering, preferably related to dam construction and soil
conservation. He would have had at least 5 years' experience in earth dam
construction and soil conservation in Africa. He would be responsible for
overall supervision of the dam construction and soil conservation component
of the project. His responsibilities would include:
(a) survey and design of water catchment areas and dams;
(b) design planning, and supervision of construction of all
soil conservation work;
(c) liaison with the Road Engineer and Land Use Planning Officer
in integrating dam construction and soil conservation works
into a general land use plan and the road building program;
and
(d) training project staff in dam construction and soil conser-
vation work.
The Soil and Water Conservation Engineer would be responsible to
the Chief Engineer.
MECHANICAL ENGINEER
The Mechanical Engineer would be a professionally qualified engin-
eer with specific experience in operating and maintaining heavy earth moving
machinery. His principal duties and responsibilities would be supervising
the operation and maintenance of all project plant, equipment, and vehicles.
His duties would include:
(a) establishment of a workshop capable of maintaining project
plant, equipment and vehicles;
(b) ordering and maintaining an appropriate supply of stores
and spare parts;
(c) ordering and maintaining an adequate and suitable fuel
supply; and
(d) training of Nigerian mechanical engineers.
He would be directly responsible to the Chief Engineer.



ANNEX 6
Schedule 3
Page 9
ROAD ENGINEER
Successful experience in the design, construction and maintenance
of earth roads in tropical climates would be the main qualification of the
Road Engineer. The Road Engineer would be responsible for implementing the
project road program. His duties would include:
(a) advising the Chief Engineer on road design, drainage and water
disposal, culverts and river crossing designs:
(b) the direction of all machinery and equipment put at his disposal
for construction purposes;
(c) the preparation and maintenance of records adequate to enable
unit costs to be established;
(d) evaluation of road construction program and techniques used; and
(e) training Nigerian engineers in road design and construction
methods.
He would be directly responsible to the Chief Engineer.
SENIOR EVALUATION OFFICER
A senior evaluation officer would be required to direct the field
enumeration units attached to the project. IHis job would be essentially to
supervise enumerators, implementing programls set out by the Head of the
evaluation section of the Project Monitoring and Evaluation Unit (PMEU),
and feed back the required information for analysis. His views and sugges-
tions would be taken into account during the programming of the enumeration
process and any subsequent amendments to it. fie would be technically re-
sponsible to the Head of PILU evaluation section and administratively re-
sponsible to the project manager.
The senior evaluation officer would have post graduate training in
agricultural economics or rural economics and would have controlled and di-
rected field enumeration units.
