ANNEX 7
Page 1
NIGERIA
NORTH EASTERN STATE
GOMBE AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Farm Inputs
1.        The traditional agriculture of the Gombe area uses almost no modern
agricultural inputs. Currently there is very little demand for, and very
little supply of, such inputs as fertilizers, insecticides, sprayers and other
farm machinery. Yet, if the project is to achieve its goals, the project must
make available a supply of these inputs, create a demand for them, develop
the means for farmers to acquire them, and educate farmers in their use -
even supervise their use where necessary to assure results.
2.        At present suppliers of farm inputs in the project area are either
absent or inadequate. Therefore, the project would be an importer and re-
tailer of critical farm supplies and equipment. These supplies include 320
m tons fertilizer in year one, increasing to 16,000 m tons in year five;
1,600 sprayer kits in year one increasing to 15,000 in year five; as well as
ox drawn equipment, seeds, and insecticides. The logistics of acquiring,
moving and distributing these quantities would be substantial and would be
the responsibility of the Chief Marketing Officer of GOPMU. The selling price
of inputs would be based on an ex-village store cost.
3.        Inputs would be transported to the project center at Gombe where
a 4,000-ton storage facility would be constructed at Gombe railway yard and
to 50 farm service center stores each with a 500-ton storage capacity.
Rail to village store transportation would be arranged with private truckers,
many of whom would be LBA transport contractors who would welcome back haul
loads while moving produce from village to market.
4.        Seasonal inputs include fertilizers, insecticides, seeds and bat-
tery sets for ULV sprayers. Fertilizers would be purchased in 50 kg bags
clearly marked to distinguish kind. The landed price per m ton in the village
store (including a 10% handling charge) is estimated at N 196.9 for calcium
ammonium nitrate, N 183.7 for single super phosphate, and N  114.4 for muriate
of potash. insecticides for cotton and cowpeas would cost farmers an esti-
mated N 1.65 per kilogram and for maize about N 0.33 per kilogram. Battery
sets for sprayers would cost about N 2.64 per set. Improved seeds would be
made available at varying costs.
5.        Subsidies.  NES would continue to subsidize nitrogenous and phos-
phatic fertilizers to an amount of 50% of cif farm store cost. At present
farmers pay N 40 per m ton for ammonium sulphate and N 32 per m ton for sin-
gle superphosphate (less than projected cif Nigerian ports price). It has
been Government policy to subsidize cotton insecticides. In the expectations



ANNEX 7
Page 2
of increasing yields by as much as 400% under the project through spraying,
and associated practices, and because a large number of farmers would be in-
volved, compared to the negligible number at present, it is considered desir-
able that cotton insecticides would not be subsidized. The subsidy element
for fertilizers is shown in Table 1.
6.        Sales of Seasonal Inputs.  It is expected that 25% of seasonal in-
puts would be sold for cash at farm service centers. Payments would be
made at the service center, recorded by cash register, and simultaneously
entered in the stores register. The remaining 75% of seasonal inputs would
be sold on credit. The credit system is described in more detail in Annex 8.
Sales on credit would be in kind and would be similar to the system for a
cash sale except that they would be registered on the farmer's credit card.
Tables 2, 3, and 4 show the amounts involved.
7.        Medium Term Inputs and Farm Equipment include ULV sprayers, ox
drawn equipment kits and tractor kits. These would be procured by the pro-
ject for sale to farmers. ULV sprayers together with protective gloves and
face masks would be sold for N 15.7. An estimated 33,000 sprayer kits would
be made available to farmers over the 5 year development period (Table 5).
Ox drawn kit at N 165 each would include an ox cart, plow and ridger. An
estimated 5,000 ox drawn kits are expected to be taken up over 5 years in
the project area (Table 5); mair.ly to larger farmers owning at least 6-10 ha
of land under cultivation. The project would not supply oxen. Farmers in-
terested in obtaining ox equipment would be expected to acquire oxen with their
own resources. Extreme difficulties are involved in supplying oxen on credit,
given their relatively high value and the opportunity of realizing this through
sale for meat.
8.        Tractor and Equipment.  At present the Government supports a limited
but totally uneconomic tractor hire service within the project area. This
service would be phased out during the project period and would be replaced
by contract tractor operations provided by private entrepreneurs receiving
credit under the project. About 50 such loans would be made initially, and
would be made primarily to existing transport contractors, small businessmen
or larger farmers. The project would provide such contractors with maintenance
services, through its mechanical workshop and field servicing units, short
training courses for tractor drivers, and would direct operators to selected
areas. The tractor kit would include a 65 hp tractor, trailer, chisel plow,
disc harrow and a ridger. The project management would select a standard make
and specifications for the kit, taking into account prices obtained through
domestic competitive bidding, which would require a minimum level of local
maintenance service to be provided by the tractor suppliers. Project manage-
ment would help to establish realistic and fair hire rates for contractors.
By the fifth year of the project, tractor cultivations could be extended some
3,000 to 4,000 ha annually and if successful could be further expanded.

NIGERIA
NORTH EASTERN STATE
GOMBE AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Farm Credit
1.        Provision of inputs, and of technical services, is of little avail
unless farmers have the means for acquiring inputs. Thus farm credit plays
a critical role in the package of goods and services needed to make the agri-
culture of a region more productive.
2.        Almost no commercial credit is available at present to farmers in
the project area, especially to the large majority who are small farmers.
Although small farmers apparently get some credit from local traders and
other private persons, it is unreliable, costly, and totally inadequate for
generating a more productive agriculture. Conmmercial banks in the area are
not equipped to extend large numbers of small loans to farmers. The costs
of doing such business are prohibitative. Cooperative societies that have
extended credit to farmer members have failed more often than they have suc-
ceeded, partly because the inputs necessary to make enterprises productive
were not available, partly because no technical service or supervision ac-
companied the loan, and partly because of inadequate management of the co-
operatives themselves. In any case, sources of credit currently are total-
ly inadequate to serve the needs for an increasingly productive agricultural
economy.
Amounts of Credit Needed
3.        The credit system visualized for the project area would be limited
to providing farmers with specific inputs, namely fertilizers, insecticides,
seeds, sprayers, ox drawn equipment and a small amount of tractor equipment.
The estimated amounts of credit sales for each year of the 5-year project
period are:
Year 1      Year 2      Year 3      Year 4      Year 5
---------------------   N    000
Fertilizers                    22.7       133.8       357.2        717.2       1137.9
Insecticides, seeds, and
battery sets                41.0        93.9       210.8       415.6        720.2
Sprayers, oxdrawn equipment
and tractor equipment       38.8       103.6       228.2       311.3        395.5
Total                    102.5       331.3       796.2      1135.9       2253.6



ANNEX 8
Page 2
4.        fTo lend and collect these sums effectively and efficiently it would
be necessary and preferable to establish an organization that would be a
self-perpetuating institution capable of serving the credit needs of farmers
in the area for an indefinite period of time.
5.        Operating Procedures for GOPIfD Revolving Credit Fund.  Several
operating procedures lend themselves to adoption by GOPMU in so far as they
would assure good service to customers at reasonable cost. The following
procedures would be those which GOPMU would employ initially and modify as
required in the course of the project's life. At first, lending would be
limited to input items carried by project farm service center stores; such
lending would be in amounts specified on a simple farm management plan approved
by project's Development Service AA. A simple form of loan application would
be employed which would serve as a farm management plan and promisory note at
the same time.
6.        The Credit Assistant would calculate the farmers credit requirements
and after clearance on the borrowers credit worthiness from the village head the
borrower would be authorized to present his application to the village service
center. The items specified would be provided to the farmer and the sale re-
corded by a bookkeeping machine. At market time, the Farm service center store-
keeper would make it as convenient as possible for the borrower to repay his loan.
7.        Each borrower would have a laminated identity card containing his
picture, serial number (which would identify his village and hamlet) and sig-
nature or mark for convenient identification and record keeping.
8.        Seasonal loans and two-year sprayer loans involving a total out-
standing credit of less than N 100 to borrowers in goodstanding would be
approved by Credit Assistants at the village level; attention being paid to
ensure that the time between loan application and the borrower actually ob-
taining his input items would be minimal. Larger loans and loans for oxdrawn
equipment would need to be approved by the relevant Credit Officer. Loans of
over N 500 and all tractor loans would require the Credit Manager's specific
approval.
9.        GOPMU would lend up to a maximum of N 5,000 to any one borrower.
Larger borrowers would be directed to NAB or commercial banks.
10.       Group Obligation.  The procedures would provide that each borrower's
loan application be approved by the hamlet or village head, or a farmers'
association approved by the village authorities. Such approval would certify
to the applicants credit worthiness. Once each year, COPMU would summarize
all accounts of the hamlet or village. If defaults were found, the headman
would be-asked to assist in collections. GOPMU and the CA would be empowered
to withhold lending to anyone in that village or hamlet until defaults are
removed unless due cause for default is presented and accepted by GOPMU. The
group obligation would be a powerful influence to avoid default under social
structure pertaining to northern Nigeria.



ANNEX 8
Page 3
11.       Provision would be made to extend credit to groups, or to village
farmers associations, if farmers wished to pool their needs for inputs and
to assume joint liability for the credit extended.
Terms of Lending
12.       Lending terms to farmers would be established each year prior to
the upcoming season. Preferably these terms should be established well in
advance, and certainly no later than the preceeding November. The lending
terms would be determined by GOPMU management for approval by GOPEC after
consultation with relevant Federal Authorities and the World Bank. Interest
rates would vary between 5-9-1/2% depending on the viabilitv of the input
package provided to the borrowers. Most appropriately it should be applied
as follows:
-    seasonal loans for farm inputs - repayable within 12 months
at a flat service charge rate of 10%. No down pavments required;
-    sprayer loans repayable within two years in annual equal
installments at a flat service charge rate of 10% per annum.
No down payment required;
-    oxdrawn equipment loans within three years in three annual
equal installments at a flat service charge rate of 10%
per annum. A 20% down payment required;
-    tractors and tractor drawn equipment within four years in
four annual equal installments at a flat service charge
rate of 10% per annum. A 30% down payment required.
13.       The above terms may differ at the time of implementation depending
on the economic situation prevailing at the time. However, on present fore-
casts and estimates, see farm budgets at Annex 1, the proposed crop improve-
ments and input packages could easily carry the terms proposed in para 12.
It is strongly recommended, for ease of administration and appreciation by
the borrowers, that a flat service rate made up of interest and services is
applied to all loans.
14.       During the implementation of the project, management and PMEU would
make a thorough study of various lending systems, such as cooperatives etc.,
that exist in the project area, with a view to making firm recommendations
for the conversion, in the fourth or fifth year of the project, of the revolv-
ing credit fund into a form that can continue lending to the farmers after the
project development period is completed. It is therefore of great importance
that from the start of the project the revolving credit fund is operated on a
practical and commercial basis, and for this reason an illustrative cash flow
for the fund is shown at Table 1 of this Annex.

NIGERIA
NORTH EASTERN STATE
GOMBE AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Project Evaluation
1.        Rural development projects of the size and scope of the Gombe
project can have far reaching effects on the people in the project area and
on the economy of the State and the nation. The effects cannot be foreseen
with precision. Thus, an ongoing and thorough evaluation must be initiated
and pursued throughout the life of the project:
(a) to guide project management;
(b) to help establishment of project goals and scheduling of
project activities;
(c) to measure effectiveness and efficiency of project activities;
(d) to inform State and national governments and others of changes
stemming from project activities;
(e) to enhance knowledge of the rural development process for
future development planning; and
(f) to permit the continuous reappraisal of project goals and
assumptions underlying project design, particularly those
aspects affecting credit and input supplies and to allow
for progressive modification of project objectives and
structures.
2.        Evaluation must include such standard economic criteria as changes
in gross and net incomes of participants, changes in volume and value of
production, and measures of efficiency in use of project resources. It must
include also such direct and indirect benefits as changes in efficiency and
effectiveness of the marketing system, changes in employment, changes in the
volume and type of business in the region, changes in tax revenues and ex-
penditures, and changes in other economic and social indicators that would
show direct and indirect results of project activities. Equally important
may be changes in patterns of consumption, in participation in education, in
health, and in other measures of well being of people in the project area.
3.        Precise types of measures to be made and specific methods of data
collection and analyses must be developed by the evaluation staff on site.
Some of the data needed would be generated by project records. Other data
must be collected by surveys and from secondary sources outside the project
area. The proposed investment of substantial expertise and manpower in



ANNEX 9
Page 2
developing and conducting surveys and analyses designed to measure meaning-
fully the parameters of rural development, as partially indicated in para-
graph 2, should generate considerable benefits, both for future planning in
Nigeria and for the general knowledge of the rural development process.
4.        If the broader impact of the project is to be assessed, there is
no alternative to undertaking baseline surveys before the effect of the
project begin to manifest themselves, and to continue the assessment of
changes brough about by project actions during and after the implementation
period. No organization or project can properly evaluate itself; and there-
fore evaluation staff must be independent of project management in technical
direction and analysis methods; although of course they should work closely
with project staff for administrative purposes.
5.        In order to accomplish a meaningful evaluation, the Federal Minis-
try of Agriculture and Natural Resources (FMANR) would establish a Project
Mionitoring and Evaluation Unit (PMEU) at Kaduna. PMEU would have a dual
function: (a) using existing Federal staff, suitably strengthened, it would
undertake a continuing technical and financial review of project actions so
as to satisfy FMANR that the project accomplishes its general objectives;
(b) by establishing a special evaluation section and employing specialist
consultants, it would undertake an economic and social evaluation as out-
lined in paras 1-3.
6.        The evaluation section of PMEU would be set up under the World Bank
loan for the Funtua Agricultural Development Project and would be directed
by a "Head" who would be responsible for technical and budget control of the
unit. Certain key staff would be employed by the unit to be responsible for
developing an evaluation program and for the analysis of the results from
Gombe project and from other projects established in northern Nigeria. Each
project evaluated would have its own field enumeration teams that would re-
main administratively responsible to the respective projects.
7.        The Monitoring section would be funded from ongoing FMANR budget-
ary allocations and initially would be hardly more than a one-man unit. The
evaluation section would be funded by FMG from its own resources and a loan 1/
from the World Bank. A special bank account would be maintained for the
evaluation section. The field enumeration units would be funded by the
State Government from its own resources and from a Bank loan.
8.        The Federal Government has indicated that it would contract the
staffing of the evaluation section of PMEU to consultants. The consult-
ants would probably be a Nigerian university, in all likelihood the favor-
ably placed Ahmadu Bello University at Zaria. The consultants would be free
to draw upon the services of experienced individuals or possibly an outside
university to fill the posts provided for.
1/   This loan would be made under the Loan for the Funtua Agricultural
Development Project.



ANNEX 9
Page 3
9.        Initially, the evaluation section of PMEU would monitor and eval-
uate the Funtua, Gusau and Gombe Agricultural Development Projects, but as
time progresses, the evaluation section would be expanded to take in other
projects that might be developed, particularly as the State Governments would
in every case be responsible for administering the respective field enumera-
tion units.
10.       The Gombe field evaluation unit would be made up of two enumera-
tion units each consisting of 1 economist, 4 supervisors and 20 enumerators
(both men and women). Each unit would be responsible for approximately 600
samples, the actual number of samples depending upon the degree of intensity
of sampling laid down by PMEU. One senior evaluation officer based at proj-
ect headquarters would be responsible for the project evaluation unit which
would comprise himself, his support staff and the 2 field enumerator units
(see table for costs).
11.       The evaluation section of PMEU would prepare a summary analysis
report at least once each year for each project, and such other special
reports as needed and when available information may suggest. The summary
report would be reviewed with the Project Manager and subsequently made
available to the Federal Coordinating Committee. A final summary appraisal
would be prepared for each project at the end of its first five years of
operation.
12.       The project evaluation activities described in this annex would be
in addition to that which would be undertaken by each Project Manager as a
matter of routine to provide himself with performance and cost effectiveness
information necessary for good management and the achievement of project
goals.

NIGERIA
NORTH EASTERN STATE
GOMBE AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
MARKETING AND PRICES
A. Background Summary
1.        To date officially organized marketing in the northern States has
been confined to the so called cash crops, primarily cotton and groundnuts.
Arrangements for food crops have evolved through the private sector on a
laissez-faire basis with minimal assistance, or interference, from Federal
or State Governments. Producer prices for cash crops have been determined by
world levels (over which Nigeria has little influence) and the need to generate
Government revenue. Food prices have been determined by domestic supply demand
relationships in an essentially self sufficient environment largely uninfluenced
by world levels. The basic conflict between the systems has only recently be-
come apparent as the price effect of increased food grain demand has impacted
on overall cropping patterns.
2.        The geographical extent of the country, difficulty of communication
and the widespread nature of basically subsistence oriented food crop pro-
duction has prevented the development of an integrated national grain-market.
Consequently there has grown up a multiplicity of small, unrelated markets;
these are probably rather efficient in themselves but they nevertheless give
rise to marked spatial and temporal price irregularities. In such an imperfect
situation, inelasticity of market demand has a magnifying effect on price move-
ment and emphasises inadequacies in both structure and system. Prices for food
grains are now, however, sufficiently high to compete with traditional cash
crops where labor constraints permit. Nevertheless there is continued heavy
private and state investment in agro-industry, particularly seed crushing, and
textile mills much of which will remain underutilized unless significant in-
creases in marketed output of the traditional cash crops are achieved.
3.        In order to improve the situation, increase relative cash crop re-
turns and overcome imperfections and inefficiencies in statutory marketing
systems, FMG has initiated a series of policy and organizational changes.
Producer prices are now Federal rather than State responsibility, taxes have
been reduced or abolished, and some means of fixing marketing overheads are
being introduced. To stimulate food grain production a form of price support
is planned operating through a national storage and marketing program. A
series of national grain production units (State owner and operated commercial
farms) is also contemplated. Detailed plans for the implementation of these
policies have still to be announced. There is clearly considerable room, and
need, for improvements in marketing if proper benefits of better production
technology are to accrue to both producer and consumer; however recent and



ANNEX 12
Page 2
current intercrop price relationships show the importance of an overall,
balanced approach if production of one set of crops is not to be unduly en-
hanced at the expense of others. Certainly the development of a medium term
minimum price policy, coupled with timely preplanting announcement of producer
prices for gazetted crops is badly needed.
B. Statutory Marketing Organzations
4.        Scheduled crops 1/ are marketed through statutory organizations
controlling a series of franchise operations - each of which may have oppor-
tunities to exploit a monopolistic position. Until recently the primary
marketing organization (NSMB) enjoyed the dominant role, coordinating the
functions of the various participants in the system (LBA, CAP, NPMC). FMC
has now taken over NPMC as part of the program to reorganize and improve
marketing; 2/ NSMB will now act only as purchasing agent for its erstwhile
subsidiary, which will continue to carry out the export marketing function.
5.        Producer prices for statutory crops have become a Federal responsi-
bility, recommendations being made to the Head of State by the Technical
Committee on Produce Prices. NSMB will pass on produce to NPMC at a fixed
price, intended to take into account NSMB marketing costs and administrative
overheads. These have still to be determined and agreed. NSMB operations
are extensive and the difficulties exacerbated by lack of communication
facilities, particularly feeder roads. Apart from obvious consequent prob-
lems of crop movement, this limitation also results in considerable hindrance
to the exercise of proper financial and operational control over all those
involved in produce marketing.
6.        Rural buying and first assembly are performed through a system of
licensed buying agents (LBA) and subagents. Quality control is the responsi-
bility of the Produce Inspection Department (PID) of the Ministry of Natural
Resources. Cotton is officially graded by Produce Examiners (PE) prior to
purchase at gazetted markets. Groundnuts are officially graded by Produce
Inspectors (PI) after purchase and on presentation at designated inspection
points. Check grading takes place when goods are moved into or from NSMB sto-
rage points. LBA are required to arrange their own finance for purchases and
are paid (through discountable produce receipts) upon delivery to NSMB depot.
Despite this payment LBA remain responsible for "their" produce while it is
stored and until it is despatched to the consumer.
1/   In the project area cotton and groundnuts are the most important.
2/   The new policy aims at reducing the burden of taxation; reducing statutory
marketing overheads, transferring world price developments more accurately
to producers.



ANNEX 12
Page 3
7.        NSMB accounts are not available beyond 1967/68 and even these are
unreliable; costings used in this annex are therefore derived or implied, and
are not objective measures of actual performance. Estimated 1972 marketing
costs expressed as percentage of the FOB price are as follows:
Groundnuts           Cotton Lint
NPMC                            1                    1
NSMB                            3.3                 5
CAP (BCGA)                      -                   8
LBA                             5    9.3             7    21
Transport, handling
Bags, Insurance,               13                   13
storage, port charges               13                    13
Produce Sales Tax               2                    2
Export Duty                    16   18              15    17
NSMB Profits                         4.7                   -
45.0                51.0
8.        Export duty and produce sales tax have been abolished and replaced
by a single produce tax. This is set at 10% of producer price compared with
about 25% before the reform.
9.        World prices currently at a very high level are expected to decline
over the course of the project; the fixed nature of most marketing costs means
that such a decline would be disproportionately reflected in resulting producer
prices. It is therefore important that new policies and practices result in
the best possible relative return to farmers thereby increasing production
incentives at given crop values. To attempt to overcome the problem by ar-
bitrary reduction of agency allowances, in reflection of individual crop pro-
sperity, would only result in the 'saving' being translated into a direct or
indirect reduction of the price actually paid to producers.
10.       Since the project would operate within the statutory marketing
system it is necessary to identify the deficiencies and outline ways in which
improvements may be made. This may be doubly important in view of the possible
parallel development of a price support program for food grains..



ANNEX 12
Page 4
C. Marketing Policy and Organization
Fragmentation of Responsibility
11.       Absence of total marketing responsibility, particularly at primary
level, frustrates the emergence of corporate pride and wholehearted effort so
important in an operation of this nature. The present system of split respon-
sibilities under which PID and not NSMB are responsible for the conduct of
markets operated by NSMB agents, as well as aspects of produce quality, is
more divisive than cohesive. It appears to provide more opportunities for
abuse than for the imposition of independent checks and balances which it is
intended to do. Clearly, independent inspection is essential at a point or
points in the marketing chain, however the organization responsible for market-
ing should not only control its agents but should also have power to decide
whether or not produce is of sound marketable quality. It should then be in
a position to adequately control storage and accept the consequent risks.
Presently NSMB bears a number of risks without compensatory control. Possible
abuse of quality (and thus price) at primary level may be overcome partially
by inspection but mainly by proper competitive buying.
Late Announcement of Seasonal Guaranteed Prices
12.       In the past prices have seldom been announced prior to planting.
This has prevented firm price considerations from affecting planting decisions.
Furthermore, inter-seasonal world price variations reflect conditions over
which Nigeria has little or no control or influence. Consequently the linking
of annual producer prices to projected world levels is likely to result in
magnified production fluctuations compared with a rather stable domestic
demand. A 'rolling' three to five year minimum price structure, related to
projected world prices, should be developed. This would encourage better
planning at farm level and assist achievement of greater interseasonal pro-
duction stability. Within this structure, minimum seasonal prices would be
announced before planting, and confirmed (or increased if justified) prior
to the opening of markets.
Lack of an effective crop reporting and market intelligence system
13.       This prevents early assessment of crop sizes and dispositions and
either prevents timely marketing decisions from being made, or causes them
to be inadequately based. The establishment of an intelligence service is
a long term undertaking, however early and significant improvements could be
achieved by instituting a more widespread comparative two-way reporting system
among members of MNR, Local Authorities, and NSMB staff, coordinated at
State and Federal level. It must be emphasized that communication needs to
be two way: a field reporter should be kept informed of conditions outside
his immediate area, if only to maintain his interest. The present practice
of keeping crop and weather reports under 'secret' cover is self defeating.
The development of an intelligence service is essential to improving food
grain marketing.



ANNEX 12
Page 5
D. Marketing Practices
The Licensed Buying Agent (LBA)
14.       The number of LBA is relatively small compared to the number of
agents and sub-agents directly or indirectly employed. Although open compet-
ition is officially encouraged it hardly exists except in areas directly tribu-
tary to major highways. Transportation difficulties are very real and non-
competitive practices result, with little encroachment by one LBA on the
'territory' of another. In general, LBA perform reasonably well, but the un-
scrupulous have not been adequately disciplined in the past; the monopolistic
positions achieved for individual LBA by a network of subagents and buyers
means that suspension or dismissal of a LBA, mid season, could result in farm-
ers being worse off through denial of outlets for their produce. Most of the
'normal' methods of cheating in produce marketing are employed at various
degrees at all levels of trade. These include underweighing and undergrading,
imposition of 'dash' payments for inspection services, degradation of gazetted
market services (thereby forcing sales elsewhere) etc. LBA and their agents
naturally do not confine their activities solely to produce, however the advance
of consumer credit at other times, for ultimate repayment with produce, provides
another means of 'buying' at less than gazetted levels. The fact that produce
payments by NSMB are not always prompt or assured, provides constant encourage-
ment for cheating to continue.
15.       Buying Seasons are over extended relative to volume purchased.  This
increases costs and further degrades competition through frequent absences of
buyers and PID staff.
16.       Movement and Storage particularly of groundnuts, is poorly planned
and executed. This is partially due to lack of crop information but also due
to system rigidities which prevent optimum use being made of the resources
and facilities of the local agro-industry. The continuation of LBA responsi-
bility for produce while it is in NSMB depots or dumps adds unnecessarily to
storage costs.
E. Individual Crop Marketing
Cotton Marketing
17.       Cotton marketing is probably the most highly technical of all annual
crops and maximum cooperation among all involved in the industry is essential
if producer and consumer requirements are to be satisfactorily blended and
returns assured.



ANNEX 12
Page 6
18.       Recent experience shows that production is price sensitive; thus
the projected decline in world values makes it even more important that post
harvest and preplanting costs are reduced as much as possible. Ways of effec-
ting further savings need to be explored as well, and in this respect the
project should be able to make a significant contribution. The main defects
include:
(i) high seed waste and loss;
(ii) poor handling and storage techniques up to point of ginning; and
(iii) absence of independent quality assessment of cotton lint.
Seed wastage
19.       Planting seed is dressed and distributed to strategically sited
stores, where it is held before being issued free of charge. Apparent plant-
ing rate is three to six times that recommended but seed is also used for other
purposes, such as fertilizer, and as an effective "smudge" fire against mosqui-
toes. Although theoretically free, cotton seed is traded in village markets,
sellers usually being those who live near a cottonseed store and have managed
to work a corner on local supplies. Cotton seed accounts for about 65% of the
weight and 35% of the total value of seed cotton; it costs about N 15.00 a ton
to dress and deliver to store, thus every ton of seed unnecessarily dressed
and distributed results in a marketing loss of about N 65.00. For 1973/74 the
total cost is probably in excess of N 1.3 million, or, in terms of producer
returns, rather more than 1 kobo/kg seed cotton, equivalent to 8% of the pro-
ducer price in the 1973/74 season. Part of the cause of over supply of plant-
ing seed (for which allowances have been made in arriving at the above figure)
is the relatively poor performance of fuzzy, i.e. undelinted seed. The solu-
tion would seem to lie in progressively moving toward a situation where only
"improved" (i.e. delinted) seed is available and for which at least a nominal
price has to be paid by growers.
20,       By using acid delinting all costs of dressing would be saved (and
the mixers released for other seed treatment), empty seeds would be floated
off and high germination reasonably assured. After allowing a 30% weight loss
-or delinting and seed selection, at least half of the customarily required
quantity of seed would thus become available for sale. Additional expense
would be incurred in packaging seed for sale in 10 kg bags, but this cost as
well as that of actual delinting would be more than compensated by the sav-
ings in both seed and distribution costs. The project would be an excellent
vehicle for developing this approach, all seed being sold through service
centers. Estimated financial benefits would be about N 5.8 per ton of seed
cotton. The estimated cost of delinting, distributing and packaging is about
40 kobo per 10 kg pack (enough for 0.5 ha) so the sale price could be rela-
tively nominal, however some charge must be made, and better seed performance
demonstrated, if the present widespread abuses are to be curbed and a great-
er proportion of the real value of seed cotton realized. In discussions
with CAP and its parent company, the principle of their installing acid delin-
ting plants during the project has been tentatively agreed, subject to suit-
able arrangements being made concerning fees.



ANNEX 12
Page 7
21.       Handling and storage of seed cotton.  After purchase, seed cotton is
packed in 100 lb bags and transported to dumps near the ginneries. From there
it is ultimately moved to the gin, being reweighed, unbagged and check graded
into the suction room. LBA remain responsible for the cotton they have pur-
chased until after it has been weighed into the gin, although provisional pay-
ment will already have been made to them on purchased weights and grades.
There is little enthusiasm or incentive among LBA to ensure proper storage in
the dumps: spoilage of cotton through failure to provide stillage is usual.
PID control is sketchy and it is by no means unusual for LBA to wet seed cotton
before it is weighed into the gin in an attempt to increase weight and/or over-
come losses caused by natural shrinkage or other less easily explained causes.
22.       Efficiency would be improved and losses reduced by providing fixed
(plinth) storage at the dumps and limiting responsibility of LBA to delivering
cotton to point of storage, from which time it would come under the charge of
ginners (CAP), until completion of the ginning process. Although no procedural
changes in marketing have yet been agreed, the project does provide for the
construction of proper seed cotton reception and storage facilities adjacent
to ginneries. Total cost of each storage depot (there would be three con-
structed under the project) is estimated at N 94,000 including cost of a
weighbridge. The possibility of opening ginnery markets, for the purchase of
large lots, i.e. group marketed produce, needs to be investigated, as does
the feasibility of reducing handling by bulk movement of cotton from village
markets.
23.       Absence of independent lint quality assessment.  Seed cotton is
graded on purchase and prior to ginning, and the lint is subsequently classified
by CAP, for which service they receive a flat fee payment from NSMB. The crop
is then allocated to spinners taking account of their stated requirements,
(prepared 12 months in advance) and classes actually available. No selling
samples are furnished nor in cases of dispute is there any provision for in-
dependent quality determination, although these are customary trade practices,
and are performed on all export contracts. A partial consequence is an almost
total lack of cooperation within the industry, and while neither side is blame-
less, the take it or leave it attitude of sellers does not contribute to a
greater degree of understanding. The manufacturers could contribute to a
greater degree of understanding, and could contribute considerably to the well-
being of the cotton industry through their technical and research expertise
and experience elsewhere, and it is important that a more coordinated approach
is adopted.
24.       The whole approach to cotton marketing is lack-lustre and far great-
er enthusiasm needs to be generated if anticipated falls in world values are
not to make the crop unremunerative. Ginneries are well sited and could be-
come catalysts for production instead of just processing agencies; the possible
development of much closer links between the State Government and CAP might
assist this. Greater participation by spinners in development could result
in competitive contract ginning: whatever does happen, marketing costs must
be brought into more reasonable relationship with the value of the crop.



ANNEX 12
Page 8
25.       Cotton Ginning.  The ginning season extends for about 24 weeks from
December to June and ginning capacities are calculated on this basis (Table
3). These levels have been exceeded by up to 20% in exceptional years but
absence of covered storage for seed cotton makes extension of the season im-
practical. Additional facilities,would be needed towards the end of the proj-
ect development period and management of CAP is willing to increase effective
capacity as justified by sustained growth of production. Detailed arrangements
would be concluded by project management, MNR, NSMB and CAP during the devel-
opment period. All ginning is done under contract with NSMB, the present
agreement expiring in 1976. Estimated break even is about 200,000 bales, after
which fees are reduced; mean output over the last 13 years was 262,000 bales
(249,000 omitting highest and lowest years).
Groundnut Marketing
26.       Groundnuts are purchased from farmers at buying points licensed by
the Local Authority, although considerable 'assembly buying' by subagents also
occurs outside licensed markets. Operations are not supervised and farmers,
particularly in areas remote from roads, are frequently cheated. Conversely,
the way in which LBA allowances are calculated results in strong competition
in areas near inspection stations and inducements in cash or kind may be
offered in order to attract offerings from farmers. All purchases are in-
spected and graded at PID supervised inspection stations before being moved
into NSMB depots where they are checked and stacked in pyramids. LBA remain
responsible for stored nuts until they are despatched for export or to local
mills.
27.       Groundnuts should be moved as little as possible in order to minimize
percentage of "splits and brokens" which are more liable to aflatoxin infection
and reduce the quality of a sample. Current practices could be improved and
handling reduced, particularly now that local crushing capacity is beginning
to outstrip groundnut production.  Much of the present overhandling is, how-
ever due to lack of adequate early information concerning the size and geo-
graphical disposition of the crop; until this situation is remedied unnecessary
marketing costs will continue to be incurred. Nevertheless the establishment
of depots adjacent to mills would cut out quite a large amount of present
handling and double transport. Consideration should also be given Mto the
establishment of depot markets to Which Farmer Associations or other similar
marketing groups could deliver directly. This type of operation would be
well suited for development within the project since nuts could be assembled
and graded at a service center, and then either held to the order of NSMB
(storage charges being payable) or despatched to mill or exported.
28.       Export marketing is carried out by NPMC, with local crushers being
allowed to buy at a level equivalent to world price. This price is deter-
mined and fixed daily by NPMC- and representatives of the oil millers, who are
free to make their own arrangements for the sale of oil and cake. Raw mate-
rial price movements are not usually immediately reflected in those of the
products, and vice versa; it can be argued that local millers have the put
and take on the market. This may be true to some extent, certainly a better



ANNEX 12
Page 9
overall price could be realized if the marketing agency had selling options
(i.e. nuts, oil or cake or combinations thereof) always open to it. The
possibilities and potential ramifications of this should be investigated but,
on the face of it, benefits are likely to be small relative to the risks in-
volved and the sophistication of information and action required. Neverthe-
less, the projected declines in world value through increasing competition from
other sources of oil and protein make it essential that marketing practices,
and costs are carefully examined and improvements made, wherever possible.
Marketing and the Project
29.       Trends of marketed production over the last decade combined with
recent food price developments properly reflect the failure of the marketing
systems, statutory and otherwise, to adequately transmit consumer needs to
producers. Relatively poor production performance is not solely the fault of
marketing - lack of education and associated physical inputs such as fertilizer
is also to blame - but the inescapable fact remains that for statutory crops at
least, farmers have not been offered enough incentive in terms of price and/or
service to encourage increased output. This failure of the system has been
heightened by shortcomings within the system which have led to apparently in-
creasing abuses and consequent accelerated producer disenchantment. Under the
project, steps would be taken to rectify this position; indeed, faith in the
systems must be restored if projected benefits are to be generated. To facil-
itate this transition maximum use would be made of the physical facilities
within the project together with some organizational and operational changes.
30.       Abuse and avasion of statutory marketing requirements by LBA and
PID staff has been made easier by the small farmers basic lack of bargaining
power. The cooperative movement has been neither widespread nor effective
enough to alter this situation. Project staffed service centers would not
purchase products but would provide a focal point for farmers from which
they would draw advice and assistance and at which they would collectively
increase their bargaining power. The service center would become a place
of first assembly and storage from which, eventually, deliveries would be
made directly to domestic buyers or point of export. On such a basis the
project would make the following marketing improvements:
(i) Restoring or establishing producers faith in the system
through:
(a) early announcement of producer prices;
(b) adequate control and discipline of marketing agents;
(c) enhanced competition at gazetted markets;
(d) provision of timely and accurate market intelligence;
(e) facilities for increasing individual farmers bargaining
power; and



ANNEX 12
Page 10
(f) assistance in securing profitable outlets for new cash
crops, such as maize.
(ii) Developing a more disciplined approach to statutory marketing
by producers, thereby cutting costs;
(iii) Investigating and developing new approaches in order to ensure
the producer as large a share as possible of ultimate product
value.
31.       Statutory Crop Purchasing.  LBA and their subagents in the project
area would be subject to approval by project management. Since project super-
visory coverage of all sorts would be fairly intensive, abuses by LBA or their
agents should be quickly discovered and similarly dealt with.
32.       In order to ensure that crop purchases are not impeded through lack
of finance, arrangements would be made by NSMB (or NPMC) for working capital
requirements, as estimated by project management, to be advanced to LBA in
the project area.
33.       LBA should quickly realize that there is more to be lost than gained
by attempting to cheat farmers. Apart from much easier transport as a result
of the improved roads, as well as more easily obtained crop finance; LBA oper-
ating in the project area would be best placed to benefit from the immediate
economic consequences of increased production, not least would be back hauls
with farm inputs as well as other generally more attractive consumer goods.
34.       Project management would be given LBA status.  While it is not
intended that the project should carry out any crop purchasing, such an ap-
pointment would enable sub-licensing, and therefore full audit control, of
emergent farmer or other associations wishing and competent to engage in
group marketing. Such a system would greatly ease the potential problems of
training emergent marketers.
35.       Fair buying practices would be encouraged through the careful siting
of markets. Initially these would be situated as jointly agreed between proj-
ect management, PID, NSMB, and appropriate Local Authority. Over the course
of the project gazetted markets would come to be sited only directly adjacent
to a servicing center. Actual purchasing would be carried out under the direct
supervision of the project employed PE or PI. Farmers would be encouraged to
weigh their produce on the dial or self recording scale prior to sale. The
principle of competive servicing of producers would be reasserted and every
market would have at least two authorized buyers. Table 5 shows schematic
layout of a proposed center.
36.       Extended seasons add unnecessarily to marketing costs, reduced super-
vision and competition, and make early assessment of marketable production
more difficult. After peak marketing periods, purchases would only be made
on set market days; in addition the possibility of shortening the marketing



ANNEX 12
Page 11
season would be investigated. Although this would result in greater flows
and increased peak cash requirements during certain months, the benefits of
having earlier control of the crop would outweigh these considerations.
37.       While these steps should result in a considerable improvement in
farmers gross receipts for a given weight of produce, some of their possible
impact would be lost if they are not matched by an effective pricing policy.
It is essential that firm preplanting prices are widely announced.
38.       Food grains and other crops.  The facilities of the service center
would be available to Farmer Associations for assembly and short term storage;
difficulties over identity preservation would make it impractical to allow sim-
ilar use by individuals. Lack of accurate information is one of the major
constraints to improving food grain marketing. Under the project a market
intellingence system would be established to collect, disseminate and analyze
news about supply, demand and prices in various market centers. Services
would be centered on each Development Centre Unit and coordinated by the
marketing section in Project Headquarters. Collection and dissemination
in the project area would be achieved through the Service Centers, while a
regular reporting system from major markets which could be supplied by proj-
ect production would be established in conjunction with MNR.
39.       In addition to supervising the information systems, the marketing
section at project headquarters would also have prime responsibility for
market and marketing development. Market development would include, for
example, exploring and expanding the possibilities of securing profitable
outlets through some form of contract growing for, inter alia, the feed indus-
try. It should also cover the identification of potentially profitable 'new'
crops, such as confectionary groundnuts, and provide commercial guidelines for
the research effort. Marketing development would be specifically aimed at
cutting costs or increasing values through such things as better crop handling
and storage techniques, contract processing, and the employment of a generally
more imaginative approach to marketing problems.
G. Prices and Marketing of Maize, Sorghum and Cowpeas
Present Situation
40.       Traditionally Nigeria has been self sufficient in cereals other than
wheat. Sorghum and millet are the most important staple grains; consumption
of maize as grain occurs mainly in the southern and western states, although
this crop is now beginning to receive wider attention, particularly in the
north. Data on food production and consumption are inadequate and at best
provide only general indications of trends. Recent food crop production
figures underline however the significant role of the northern States as
cereal producers. Examination of other information 1/ indicates an insig-
nificant trade in food grains, other than wheat.
1/   FAO Commodity Balance work sheets and Food Balance Sheet for 1968/69
computed for NADC.



ANNEX 12
Page 12
41.       The situation of apparent self sufficiency has recently changed into
one where shortages persist and are rather marked in some areas. Repeated
droughts and the effects of normal population changes have been compounded
by burgeoning urbanisation and income growth associated with economic recovery,
reconstruction and increased oil earnings. Consequently, food prices have
tended to increase steadily and in urban areas rose by 24% in 1970 and 21% in
1971. Some decline occurred in 1972 but the upward tendency returned in 1973.
Prior to recent sharp gains in world prices those in Nigeria were double US
farm prices for coarse grains, and three times world level for rice.
42.       The effect of long distances on distribution costs is increased by
bottlenecks in the transportation system; as a result the spread between im-
port and export parities (already exaggerated by a 40% import duty) is too
big to enable world conditions to have any significant effect on domestic
price formation. Furthermore, the fragmented nature of production and, to
a certain extent, demand, difficulties of communication combined with lack
of market intelligence, make it impossible to speak of a Nigerian market per
se. Instead there exists a mass of relatively small market areas which
appear to exert little or no influence upon those adjacent.
43.       Staple food marketing is almost exclusively confined to the private
sector although some official purchase, storage and distribution schemes are
run in efforts to relieve local shortages. A number of studies have concluded
that performance within the marketing system is rather efficient. This may
tend to mislead; while reported functional margins within study areas are low,
this cannot be taken as an indication of the efficiency of the system itself
in interpreting the needs of producers and consumers. Lack of cohesion between
market centers shows that it is the system which may be inadequate regardless
of any efficiency within it. While exploitive practices are probably being
curbed by competition in individual markets, there is no reason to suggest
that a similar conclusion may be drawn in any broader context. In fact the
reverse is likely to apply.
44.       Lack of communication between markets may be illustrated by comparison
of officially reported monthly prices for sorghum at Daudawa and Zaria in the
North Central State. These markets are about 80 kilometers apart and connected
by a full width tarmac road and a railway, yet over a period of 42 months (up
to July 1973), the price between them varied by as much as N 54.00 per ton.
During the period, Zaria prices were higher by an average of N 26.47 per ton
in 32 months and Daudawa prices higher by an average of N 30.00 in three months
(no prices were reported in one or other market for the remaining seven months).
Moreover, monthly price movements in the two markets were in opposite directions
in sixteen months. Prices within the markets varied substantially from month
to month with lowest prices not necessarily occurring around harvest. Stand-
ard deviation from monthly mean prices was 24.03 in Daudawa and 19.76 in Zaria.
A similar situation was obtained for millet (Table 7).



ANNEX 12
Page 13
45.       This erratic price behavior is indicative of the very thin markets
that are to be expected under present circumstances. Food grains have not
yet become an accepted cash crop, as such, and most grain storage takes place
at farm level. Sales are made to meet requirements not provided for by
'conventional' cash crops. Worthwhile market intelligence is unavailable and
price is extremely sensitive to slight changes in daily supply or demand. It
is not unknown for merchants to capitalise on this situation by attracting
supplies through paying high prices when offerings are limited, reducing them
sharply when the consequent increased supplies eventually reach the market.
This type of operation is assisted by the absence of adequate feeder roads
which effectively discourage most farmers from refusing a low price and taking
their grain back home. The overall picture is thus one of a rather passive,
inelastic and poorly informed system, in which market risks are taken more by
the farmer and consumer than by the merchant.
H. Future Developments, Markets and Prices
46.       As part of the policy of improving production and marketing and to
prevent a chronic supply deficiency situation from arising, FMG is developing
a scheme for the establishment of strategic grain storage and is encouraging
State food production farms; some form of price support program is also con-
templated. Details have not yet been announced (January 1974) but clearly
this could usher in a completely new phase in Nigerian agriculture. Overall
pricing policies would have to be most carefully developed to match new pro-
duction techniques and ensure that capacity is most beneficially utilized.
Although the introduction of price support could go some way to overcoming
the inherent inelasticity of the food grain market, massive 'official' pro-
duction could at the same time inhibit or retard small holder development.
In this context, and particularly in the light of expected incremental maize
production from the Funtua, Gombe and Gusau projects (117,000 tons), the
evolution of the feed and starch industries will provide an increasing
'spillway' for surplus food grain output.
47.       There are a number of projections for food demand and supply for
Nigeria of which "A Quantitive Analysis of Food Requirements, Supplies and
Demand in Nigeria 1968-1985" is the most recent. The study is among the most
comprehensive, but the quality of the basic data is such that quoted figures
should be regarded as providing only indicative value. Demand projections
have been based on the 1968/69 food balance sheet which in itself has a number
of shortcomings. Growth of population is estimated at 3% annually and that of
per capita income 4%. Income elasticity of demand for maize, sorghum and
millet is estimated at 0.4, with cowpeas at 0.3. Food supply has been pro-
jected on the assumption that past (estimated) rates of growth will be main-
tained. These rates, and estimates derived from them, carry the same degree
of reliance as the relative estimates in the sample survey; projected surplus/
deficits should be regarded as broad orders of magnitude only (Table 8).



ANNEX 12
Page 14
48.       Demand and supply for livestock feed have not been included in these
figures, nor has any provision been made for possible changes in diet (e.g. a
move away from sorghum to maize and/or wheat based products). About 45,000
tons of maize was fed commercially to livestock (mainly poultry) in 1971 and
the study aspects consumption increase fairly dramatically, reaching 106,000
tons in 1974, and over 375,000 tons in 1980. Demand has, however, proved to
be rather more price elastic than anticipated and total production has remained
rather static in the face of sharp increases in raw materials costs. Unless
there is a significant and sustained reduction of prices relative to incomes
it seems unlikely that total 1980 demand for grain for (commercial) feed would
reach 65% of previously anticipated requirements.
49.       Comparison between demand and supply projections nevertheless reveals
a deficit for all crops except cowpeas. The surplus shown for this crop is
somewhat questionable: data are extremely fragmentary but the persistent
price trend indicates a shortage situation for the preferred varieties.
Demand also appears more elastic than for the staple grains which should
encourage steady increase in production. Although the projected deficien-
cies are substantial it is important to differentiate between 'total demand'
and 'effective market demand' when considering likely price developments.
Allowance must be made for the fact that despite current high prices, market-
able production and effective market demand are probably only very slightly
out of balance, when expressed in percentage terms. Any rapid increase in
marketed production without the benefit of a support program, could have a
dramatic effect on prices.
Maize and Sorghum
50.       In the north, maize is not yet an entirely acceptable dietary sub-
stitute for either sorghum or millet. Research on this subject has been
markedly lacking, a most serious omission when the amount of production re-
search and development is considered. Limited subjective investigation re-
vealed marked differences of opinion and preference both as to the possible
part of maize in the diet and the most desirable color (white maize is pre-
ferred in the south). Being harder than sorghum, maize is unpopular to process,
both by hand and by machine. The customary diesel powered light plate type
provender mill is not very suitable for maize and typical milling charges show
a 30% premium is required. Conversely, development of surplus production will
be relatively gradual, manufacturers are likely to continue keen, if not always
easily accessible buyers, and any periods of general food grain shortage should
tend to hasten the acceptance of 'new' tastes, particularly in urban areas.
51.       Although import substitution is a convenient peg from which to hang
price formulation for food grains produced as cash crops, such a calculation
can only provide a broad indication. In fact, import parity may reasonably
be taken as a long term ceiling price for any significant quantities; however,
in the event of serious shortages it is unlikely that the consumer would be
penalized to the extent of the 40Z import duty now imposed.



ANNEX 12
Page 15
52.       Consideration has been given to the following factors in estimating
maize and sorghum prices:
(i) projected cost of imports;
(ii)  relative production costs (4:5 maize:sorghum);
(iii)  the maize production encouragement that should be generated
by t#e starch and feed industries;
(iv)  the established place of sorghum in the diet;
(v) relative inelasticity of market demand for food grains;
(vi)  probable field and storage losses that farmers would suffer
through dealing with grains on a far larger scale than pre-
viously. These are expected to be greater in the case of
maize, because of relatively larger production and longer
storage periods.
53.       The low value volume ratio of maize and sorghum compared to cotton
or groundnuts will tend to limit the radius within which profitable marketing
may occur, this could contribute to possible price instability. Because so
little information exists from which to determine probably ultimate centers
of market demand, an arbitrary 400 mile 'import' trunk haul to wholesale
market has been assumed. It has also been assumed that this market would be
served by local producers within a radius of 150 miles, first assembly being
carried out at a service center or equivalent. Prices are estimated as
follows (see Table 9):
(N per metric ton)
Maize       Sorghum
Economic                    76.00         74.00
Financial                   86.00         90.00
Cowpeas
54.       Owing to lack of data an arbitrary price has been taken in a known
major market center (Ibadan). This corresponds to the lowest post harvest
price for white cowpeas in recent years. Cowpeas are very susceptible to
pre and post harvest infestation, storage and fumigation procedures are in-
adequate at all levels so a 20% weight loss has been calculated. Prices
are estimated as follows (see Table 10):
(N per metric ton)
Economic                          99.00
Financial                         91.00



ANNEX 12
Page 16
55.       In all calculations inland transport has been assumed to be by road
at rates quoted by local firms. On trunk routes rates for big articulated
vehicles or truck/trailer combines are fairly competitive with the railway
which is still heavily congested.
Cotton
56.       The projected incremental production of 70,000 tons seed cotton
(130,000 bales lint) from the Gombe (39,000 bales), Funtua and Gusau projects
represents only 0.2 percent of expected world consumption in 1980 and, even
if all exported, would have practically no impact on likely world trade. A
comprehensive survey of the local textile industry is presently being carried
out; however, it is provisionally estimated that 1980 requirements will reach
400,000 bales lint or 210,000 tons seed cotton. Earlier demand projections,
as made by CSNRD, FAO and others over 1966-68 put probable 1980 demand at
680,000 bales.
57.       The local industry is emerging from a substantial shake out which
arose as a result of liberalization of imports in 1971, duties having now
been reimposed. 1/ Nevertheless, a number of factors adversely affecting
the originally anticipated rate of expansion still apply; these include:
(i) real income development combined with inflationary rise in
living costs has reduced the scope of textile demand. This
has been accentuated by the recent price rises in cotton
(and synthetic) products in line with world market changes;
(ii) the changing pattern of demand towards suiting materials,
generally synthetic based, in the emerging consumer generation;
(iii) the recent inability of the industry to satisfy the demand for
better cloths and textures;
(iv) competition from legal and illegal imports.
58.       Existing techniques do not permit the spinning of high counts from
present cotton varieties and this section of total demand would continue to
have to be met by imports for the foreseeable future. The projected decline
in world cotton prices will also increase the pressure of imports on local
industry. However, given recent performance in the face of factors already
mentioned, the provisional total demand of 400,000 bales in 1980 maybe some-
what conservative.
1/   Duties were removed in the 1971 budget in a successful attempt to curtail
profiteering by certain sections of the local industry. Official statis-
tics show that as a result, recorded 1971 imports reached the equivalent
of 9 months normal total supply.

59.       With incremental production from the three projects totalling
70,000 tons, maintenance of recent production growth rates elsewhere would
result in a total crop of about 190,000 tons seed cotton in 1980. This is
equivalent to 370,000 bales lint, about 30,000 bales below anticipated demand.
All incremental production from the project area would thus be effective im-
port substitution.
60.       Investment in the seed crushing industry 1/ demands a seed supply
of around 120,000 tons, or the equivalent of 200,000 tons seed cotton after
allowing for provision of planting seed. Most of the output of this industry,
however, would be confined to the export-market; although there would be some
freight savings, and possibly slightly better oil recoverv, the cottonseed
price as a factor in producer price calculations is unlikely to improve
above levels for direct exports.
61.       The local textile industry needs to be oriented towards world market
levels if substantial subsidy and, or, penal import levels are to be avoided.
Conversely, during the past two years when higher producer taxes were imposed,
'export parity' did not appear to provide sufficient inducement to farmers who
had a choice of cash crops. Consequently an extra charge of 2.5 K/kg lint had
to be made to spinners in order to permit producer prices to be raised to in-
centive levels. This charge has now fallen away as a consequence of the pres-
ent very high world prices. (1973/74 season producer prices are 13.2 K/kg and
11.0 K/kg for Grade I and II seed cottor. respectively.)

Groundnuts
63.       Projected incremental production in the project areas (about 27,000
tons) would be less than the anticipated decline elsewhere if the trend of the
last decade is maintained. Indeed, if production is to meet the capacity of
local crushing facilities some 300,000 tons of increased marketed output would
be required. This would necessitate an overall production growth rate in ex-
cess of 8% per annum. 1/
64.       Nigeria is the largest exporter of nuts and the third largest pro-
ducer (after India and China). Servicing the needs of the local crushing
industry would have a dramatic effect on world export availabilities of whole
nuts. This is not however expected to have a similar effect on prices; pro-
duct values are far more influenced by availability and prices of competing
sources of protein and oils, such as soya beans, sunflowerseed etc. Although
The trend for protein feed prices is slightly upward, that for oil is static
to declining. As a result, world prices for groundnuts are projected to de-
-line from recent levels. Since by far the greater part of the output of the
-ushing industry would be exported, farmgate prices are expected to follow
the world trend. Estimated farmgate prices are as follows: (see Table 12)

ANNEX 13
Page 1
NIGERIA
NORTH EASTERN STATE
GOMBE AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Economic Benefits
1.        The annual costs and returns used in calculating the economic rate
of return are shown in Table 1.
2.        The following assumptions and methods have been used:
(a) Project life is assumed as 25 years. Most project development
activities will be completed by project year 5 (1979), so a
salvage value is added to benefits in project years 6 and 7
to account for those houses and buildings no longer required
by the project and transferred to other Government agencies.
(b) Incremental production of crops and values of production are
shown in Table 2. The Bank Economic Analysis and Projections
Division's projections for 1980 have been used as the basis
for estimating import substitution and export prices. Details
of estimates are shown in Annex 12.
(c)  Incremental benefits accruingto investments from oxdrawn
equipment and tractors have been calculated (Schedules 1 and
2). The total benefits are offset against incremental labor
costs as shown in Table 3.
(d) Economic costs include adjustments for identifiable taxes,
and appropriate amendments to offshore costs to reflect a
shadow foreign exchange rate of US$ 1.27 per N 1.00.  Incremental
farm labor (Table 3) required by i.nproved cropping practices
has been estimated at the shadow price of N 0.40 per six-hour
day compared to the official minimum wage rate of N 0.6 for
the same working period. Economic costs do not include
project costs for water development, soil conservation, project
evaluation, and preparation of feasibilitv studies as benefits
from them are intangible and cannot be calculated with any
accuracy.
3.        Using the above assumptions the economic rate of return is 17%0.
A sensitivity analysis has been carried out on a number of variables, the
main comparison being between shadow rated and non shadow rated foreign ex-
change. In each instance two cost streams have been used, or, stream compris-
sing of project overhead costs, and the other variable from inputs. The result
of this analysis is shown below:

ANNEX 13
Schedule 1
NIGERIA
NORTH EASTERN STATE
COMBE AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPIlENT PROJFCT
Returns to Farmers using Work Oxen
1.        Work oxen are traditionally used in land preparation work in northern
Nigeria. At present insufficient and poor quality equipment limits the amount
of land preparation done by oxen.
2.        Farmers owning work oxen would be able, through the project to obtain
credit for the purchase of ox-drawn equipment, including a plow, a ridger, and
a cart.
3.        The one-ha budgets for all the major crops show a heavy utilization
of labor for land preparation. In the one-ha maize budget, cultivation costs
using hand-labor are estimated to be between N 30.40 and N 34.80 dependent
on crop yields. This cost represents between 456 and 522 hours per ha assuming
a labor cost of N 0.066 per man-hour.
Benefits
4.        The use of oxen would result in considerable savings in labor as
demonstrated below:
A. Ox Time per Operation using 2 ox-teams
Plowing                 30 hours/ha
Cultivating             10 hours/ha
Ridging                 15 hours/ha
Total              55 hours/ha
B.   Area of Cultivation achievable per ox-team per annum: 4 ha
C.   Costs per ox-team
Oxen upkeep             N 14.0 p.a.
Ox depreciation           20.0 p.a.
Subtotal     N 34.0
Cost per ha             N  8.5
Labor cost per ha:
- using 1 driver plus
1 laborer    N 11.0
Total cost per ha  N 19.5
Total cost per
using hand-labor N 33.0

ANNEX 13
Schedule 2
NIGERIA
NORTH EASTERN STATE
GOMBE AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Returns to Tractor Users
Tractors
1.        The Government operates a very uneconomic tractor hire unit within
the project area.
2.        Loans would be made available under the project to larger farmers
for tractors and equipment for use on their own farms and to individuals who
would provide contract tractor services.
3.        A farmer or contract tractor operator would be expected to provide
=0% of the total cost of a tractor unit.
4.        A tractor unit would include:  a 65hp tractor, a chisel plow, a
ridger, and a trailer. The total cost is estimated at N 6,000. The farmer
or contract tractor operator would provide N 1,800 in cash and a loan of
N 4,200 would be made available.
Tractor Benefits
5.        Savings in labor can be calculated as follows:  Estimated hours
of hand labor cultivation required on one ha of maize to achieve a yield of
3,000 kg = 500 hours. Estimated tractor hours required to prepare 1 ha land
for maize: Plowing 2.5 hours; Cultivating 2.75 hours; Ridging 1.25 hours;
Weeding 3.50 hours; total 10.00 hours.
Tractor costs are estimated as follows:
Actual Cost    Economic Cost
Fuel                               N 0.30/hour    N 0.10/hour
Driver                               0.20/hour       0.20/hour
Repairs                              1.00/hour       1.00/hour
N 1.50          N 1.30
Add:  Depreciation               N 1.00             -
Total hourly cost                N 2.50/hour    N 1.30
Total economic cost for land preparation for maize per ha - 10 x 1.3
- N 13.0

Minimum ha required to justify tractor = 70 ha
